The Creation of Human Acts: Jabriyya and the Middle Path
Nasafi introduces the central issue and states the Jabriyya and mainstream positions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the central issue of the creation of human acts and the distinction between the Jabriyya's denial of human action and the mainstream view that the human is an acquisitor and agent.

Core Idea
The servant is an acquisitor, not the ultimate doer — only God is the true actor.

Nasafi begins by anchoring the discussion of divine justice and human responsibility in the question of who creates human actions. He presents the Jabriyya as outright denying real human action, while the mainstream (including his own school) affirms that the human is an acquisitor and agent, but not the ultimate doer. This lesson lays the groundwork for understanding the Sunni kasb theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage on the issue
Read the first part of the Arabic passage (up to 'والفاعل في الحقيقة هو الله') and its translation. Notice how Nasafi sets up the problem.
2
Identify the Jabriyya position
What do the Jabriyya say? They deny any real human action. Understand why this extreme view is rejected.
3
Contrast with the mainstream view
Those other than the Jabriyya say the servant is an 'acquisitor' and 'agent', but not a 'doer' in the ultimate sense. Only God is the real doer. This is the basis of kasb.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسائل التعديل والتجوير الأصل في هذه المسألة مسألة خلق أفعال العباد، وعلى هذا فالجبرية يقولون : إنه لا فعل للعبد في الحقيقة، وما سواهم يقولون : إن العبد مكتسب عامل ولا يسمى فاعلا والفاعل في الحقيقة هو الله. وعلى هذا فمن موجد أفعال العباد؟ القدرية يقولون : العبد ولا صنع الله. أما أهل الحق فيقولون: لا خالق إلا الله تعالى والعبد مكتسب وفاعل. وسار أهل الحديث، والنجارية، والكرامية.
The issues of declaring [God] just or unjust (al-taʿdīl wa al-tajwīr). The basis of this issue is the issue of the creation of the acts of servants. Upon this, the Jabriyya say: There is no action for the servant in reality. And those other than them say: The servant is an acquisitor (muktasib) and an agent (ʿāmil), and he is not called a 'doer' (fāʿil), and the real doer is God. Upon this, who is the originator (mūjid) of the acts of servants? The Qadariyya say: The servant, and there is no act of God (wa lā ṣunʿa Allāh). As for the people of truth (ahl al-ḥaqq), they say: There is no creator except God, exalted is He, and the servant is an acquisitor and a doer (muktasib wa fāʿil). And the people of ḥadīth (ahl al-ḥadīth), the Najjāriyya, and the Karāmiyya proceeded [upon this].
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage introduces the topic of al-taʿdīl wa al-tajwīr, which concerns divine justice and human responsibility. The core issue is the creation of human actions. The author outlines three positions. (1) The Jabriyya deny any real human action, attributing all actions to God. (2) The majority (including the author's school) say the human is an 'acquisitor' (muktasib) and an agent (ʿāmil), but not a 'doer' in the ultimate sense; the true doer is God. (3) The Qadariyya respond to the question 'who originates human acts?' by saying the servant alone, denying any creative act of God in them. The Ahl al-Haqq affirm that God alone is the creator, while the human is both an acquisitor and a doer (muktasib wa fāʿil) — not a contradictory position. This view was followed by Ahl al-Hadith, the Najjariyya, and the Karramiyya.

Key Terms
خلق أفعال العباد
الجبرية
مكتسب
عامل
فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Sunni position denies any human role in actions.
Correction: Actually, Sunnis affirm acquisition (kasb) and agency; the human is not passive. But ultimate creation is from God alone.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do those other than the Jabriyya say about the servant?
A) The servant is the real creator of his acts.
B) The servant has no action at all.
C) The servant is an acquisitor and agent, but not the ultimate doer.
D) The servant is both creator and doer.

Key Takeaway
The servant is an acquisitor and agent, but only God is the true doer; this is the foundation of the Sunni kasb theory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you succeed in a good deed, do you secretly feel that it was your own doing? If you truly believe that God is the only real doer, why do you still feel pride in your achievements? Write down one way you will redirect praise to God the next time you feel self-congratulatory.
?
The Jabriyya deny all human action, but the mainstream teaches that you are an agent and acquirer. Does your daily life reflect this balance—taking responsibility for your actions while knowing God creates them? Where do you lean too far toward self-reliance or fatalism? Name one concrete situation where you will consciously remind yourself: 'I am not the doer; God is.'
?
If God alone is the real doer, then every sin you commit was also created by God. Does this thought make you feel less guilty? It shouldn't—because you are the agent who acquires the sin. How can you use this truth to deepen both your repentance and your awe of God's sovereignty?

Who Creates Human Acts? The Qadariyya and the Ahl al-Haqq
Nasafi presents the Qadariyya and Ahl al-Haqq answers to the question of who originates human acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Qadariyya claim that the servant alone originates acts and the Ahl al-Haqq affirmation that God alone is creator while the servant is both acquisitor and doer.

Core Idea
God alone creates, yet the servant truly does acts through acquisition.

After setting the basis, Nasafi poses the key question: who originates human acts? The Qadariyya answer by attributing creation solely to the servant, denying any divine involvement. In contrast, the Ahl al-Haqq affirm that God alone is the creator, while maintaining that the servant is both an acquisitor and a doer. This lesson clarifies these two contrasting positions and identifies the schools that follow the Ahl al-Haqq.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read from 'وعلى هذا فمن موجد أفعال العباد؟' to the end. Notice how the question is posed and the two answers.
2
Analyze the Qadariyya position
The Qadariyya say the servant is the originator (mūjid) of acts and deny any act of God. Understand why this is considered excessive.
3
Learn the Ahl al-Haqq position
Ahl al-Haqq say there is no creator but God, and the servant is both an acquisitor and a doer. This is the balanced Sunni view.
Also note the schools that follow: Ahl al-Hadith, Najjariyya, Karramiyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسائل التعديل والتجوير الأصل في هذه المسألة مسألة خلق أفعال العباد، وعلى هذا فالجبرية يقولون : إنه لا فعل للعبد في الحقيقة، وما سواهم يقولون : إن العبد مكتسب عامل ولا يسمى فاعلا والفاعل في الحقيقة هو الله. وعلى هذا فمن موجد أفعال العباد؟ القدرية يقولون : العبد ولا صنع الله. أما أهل الحق فيقولون: لا خالق إلا الله تعالى والعبد مكتسب وفاعل. وسار أهل الحديث، والنجارية، والكرامية.
The issues of declaring [God] just or unjust (al-taʿdīl wa al-tajwīr). The basis of this issue is the issue of the creation of the acts of servants. Upon this, the Jabriyya say: There is no action for the servant in reality. And those other than them say: The servant is an acquisitor (muktasib) and an agent (ʿāmil), and he is not called a 'doer' (fāʿil), and the real doer is God. Upon this, who is the originator (mūjid) of the acts of servants? The Qadariyya say: The servant, and there is no act of God (wa lā ṣunʿa Allāh). As for the people of truth (ahl al-ḥaqq), they say: There is no creator except God, exalted is He, and the servant is an acquisitor and a doer (muktasib wa fāʿil). And the people of ḥadīth (ahl al-ḥadīth), the Najjāriyya, and the Karāmiyya proceeded [upon this].
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage introduces the topic of al-taʿdīl wa al-tajwīr, which concerns divine justice and human responsibility. The core issue is the creation of human actions. The author outlines three positions. (1) The Jabriyya deny any real human action, attributing all actions to God. (2) The majority (including the author's school) say the human is an 'acquisitor' (muktasib) and an agent (ʿāmil), but not a 'doer' in the ultimate sense; the true doer is God. (3) The Qadariyya respond to the question 'who originates human acts?' by saying the servant alone, denying any creative act of God in them. The Ahl al-Haqq affirm that God alone is the creator, while the human is both an acquisitor and a doer (muktasib wa fāʿil) — not a contradictory position. This view was followed by Ahl al-Hadith, the Najjariyya, and the Karramiyya.

Key Terms

أهل الحق
مكتسب

أهل الحديث

الكرامية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Ahl al-Haqq position contradicts itself by saying both 'no creator but God' and 'the servant is a doer'.
Correction: There is no contradiction because 'doer' (fāʿil) is used in a derivative sense: the servant performs the act by acquiring it, while God is the true creator.

Checkpoint
According to the Ahl al-Haqq, who is the creator of human acts?
A) The servant alone
B) God alone
C) Both God and the servant
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
God alone creates human acts, but the servant genuinely does them through acquisition; this is the Sunni middle path.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look back at your greatest sins, do you ever find yourself silently blaming God for creating the desire or circumstance? How does this lesson's affirmation that you are both 'muktasib' and 'fāʿil' force you to own that sin completely?
?
If every single action you take today is created by God, yet you are its true acquirer and doer, what specific thing will you do right now to align your intention with that truth?
?
The Qadariyya denied God's role in actions to protect human responsibility. Are you protecting your ego by secretly assuming you control your own destiny, while neglecting to ask God for help in every small act? How will you change that today?

Origins of the Human Action Debate: The Necessity of Capacity
Nasafi discusses origins of human action debate and necessity of capacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the origins of the debate on human action, the sectarian positions, and why Nasafi prioritizes the discussion of power and capacity.

Core Idea
Human action debates hinge on who creates actions—God alone, humans alone, or both through acquisition.

This passage from Nasafi's Tabṣira launches into one of the most contested issues in kalām: the creation of human actions. Nasafi first surveys the key sectarian positions—Jabriyya, Qadariyya, and Ahl al-Ḥaqq—then argues that since the servant is an agent, he must possess power and capacity, setting the stage for a deeper investigation. By grounding the discussion in kasb (acquisition), Nasafi navigates between determinism and free will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Nasafi organizes the discussion from origins to sectarian views to deduction.
2
Identify the sectarian views
List the three main groups mentioned (Jabriyya, Qadariyya, Ahl al-Haqq) and their respective positions on who creates actions.
Notice that the 'others' (non-Jabriyya) are the Ash'aris and Maturidis who affirm kasb.
3
Understand Ahl al-Haqq's position
Ahl al-Haqq says the servant is a 'muktasib' (acquirer) and 'fa'il' (doer) while affirming God as the sole creator. Reflect on how this differs from Jabriyya and Qadariyya.
This is the middle path between determinism and free will.
4
Note the deduction and plan
Nasafi deduces that since the servant is a doer, he must have power (qudra) and capacity (istita'a). Then he announces the plan to discuss these first before the creation of actions.
This sets the agenda for the coming lessons in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسائل التعديل والتجوير
الأصل في هذه المسألة مسألة خلق أفعال العباد، وعلى هذا فالجبرية يقولون : إنه لا فعل للعبد في الحقيقة، وما سواهم يقولون : إن العبد مكتسب عامل ولا يسمى فاعلا والفاعل في الحقيقة هو الله. وعلى هذا فمن موجد أفعال العباد؟
معهم
القدرية يقولون : العبد ولا صنع الله.
أما أهل الحق فيقولون: لا خالق إلا الله تعالى والعبد مكتسب وفاعل. وسار
أهل الحديث، والنجارية، والكرامية.
ثم لما كان العبد فاعلا ومكتسبا لابد أن له قدرة واستطاعة إذ لا فعل بدون القدرة، والكسب إنما يمتاز عن الأفعال الضرورية بالاستطاعة، وعلى هذا فإن النسفي يقول : نتكلم أولا في القدرة والاستطاعة، ثم نرتقي إلى الكلام في خلق أفعال العباد.
Issues of attributing justice and injustice (al-ta'dil wa al-tajwir). The origin of this issue is the issue of the creation of actions of servants. On this basis, the Jabriyya say that the servant has no act in reality. Others besides them say that the servant is an acquirer (muktasib) and an agent ('amil) but is not called a 'doer' (fa'il), and the real doer is God. On this, then, who is the originator of the actions of servants? With them, the Qadariyya say: the servant, and God has no part in it. As for the People of Truth (Ahl al-Haqq), they say: there is no creator except God Almighty, and the servant is an acquirer (muktasib) and a doer (fa'il). And the people of hadith, al-Najjariyya, and al-Karramiyya followed [this]. Then, since the servant is a doer and an acquirer, he must have power (qudra) and capacity (istita'a), because there is no action without power, and acquisition (kasb) is distinguished from necessary actions only by capacity. On this basis, al-Nasafi says: we first speak about power and capacity, then we ascend to speaking about the creation of actions of servants.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage addresses the issue of al-ta'dil wa al-tajwir (attributing justice or injustice to God), which stems from the problem of the creation of actions of servants (khalaq af'al al-'ibad). It first notes that the Jabriyya (determinists) deny any real human act. Others (the Mu'tazila and Ash'aris) affirm that the servant is a 'muktasib' (acquirer) and 'amil (agent), but not a 'fa'il' (doer) in reality; the real doer is God. Then it asks who originates actions. The Qadariyya hold that the servant does, and God has no involvement. The Ahl al-Haqq (i.e., Maturidis and Ash'aris) assert that there is no creator but God, yet the servant is a 'muktasib' and a 'fa'il' in a metaphorical sense. The people of hadith, al-Najjariyya, and al-Karramiyya follow this view. Finally, the passage deduces that since the servant is a doer and acquirer, he must possess power (qudra) and capacity (istita'a), for action requires power, and acquisition (kasb) is distinguished from involuntary actions by capacity. Therefore, al-Nasafi announces the plan to first discuss power and capacity, then the creation of actions.

Key Terms

تجوير
جبرية
قدرية
كسب
فاعل
خلق أفعال العباد
النجارية
الكرامية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ahl al-Haqq deny any human agency; they claim God does everything directly.
Correction: Ahl al-Haqq affirm kasb (acquisition): the servant performs actions with capacity given by God, while God is the sole creator of the act's existence.

Checkpoint
What does the passage identify as the origin of the issue of al-ta'dil wa al-tajwir?
A) The attributes of God
B) The creation of actions of servants
C) The nature of faith
D) The names of God

Key Takeaway
The debate on human action centers on whether God or the servant originates actions, with Ahl al-Haqq affirming God as creator and the servant as acquirer, necessitating a discussion of power and capacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every action you do is created by Allah, and yet you are responsible? How does this affect your view of your own sins and good deeds?
?
When you sin, do you find yourself blaming Allah's decree, or do you take full ownership? What does this passage demand of you?
?
The Qadariyya said 'the servant, and God has no part in it.' Do any of your daily actions reflect that attitude? Examine your heart: do you ever act as if God is not the creator of your actions?

Meaning and Types of Istita'a in Nasafi
Nasafi defines istita'a, divides it into two types, provides proofs, and refutes opposing views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of istita'a, its two types according to Nasafi, and the refutations of deviant views.

Core Idea
Istita'a is two-fold: physical readiness and God-given power — neither mere selfhood nor mere tools.

This lesson introduces the concept of istita'a (capacity) as defined by Imam Nasafi. We will explore how he borrows from earlier Ash'ari definitions, then divides it into two essential categories. With Quranic verses and logical arguments, Nasafi refutes several erroneous views, clarifying that human capacity is a created accident, not a part of the self.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of istita'a
Nasafi defines istita'a as synonymous with qudra (power), quwwa (strength), and taqa (energy). This is the same definition used by Ash'ari and Baqillani.
2
Identify the two types
The first type is soundness of causes and health of instruments; the second is an accident created by God in the living being. Note how each type relates to action.
The first precedes action, the second is simultaneous.
3
Examine the Quranic proofs
For the first type, Nasafi cites 58:4; for the second, 11:20. Understand how each verse applies to the respective type.
In 11:20, the disbelievers had hearing organs but lacked true capacity.
4
Analyze the refutations
Nasafi refutes three groups: those who deny any capacity beyond the self (Nazzam, Uswari, Asamm), those who say capacity is only soundness of causes, and those who say capacity is part of the capable one (Dirar, Hafs al-Fard).
The last is impossible because capacity is an accident, not a part of a body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في الاستطاعة:
يعرف الاستطاعة بما عرفها به الكثير من المتكلمين مثل الأشعري و
والباقلاني
بأنها : القدرة والقوة والطاقة، وكلها معان متقاربة، ويقسمها إلى قسمين: ١ - سلامة الأسباب وصحة الآلات وهذه تتقدم الأفعال وهي من نعم الله سبحانه وتعالى. وهي تحد بالتهيؤ لتنفيذ الفعل عند إرادة المختار .
۲- فهو معنى يتعذر حده وهو عرض يخلقه الله تعالى للحيوان ليفعل به أفعاله الاختيارية، وهو علة للفعل.
ثم يدلل النسفي على وجود الاستطاعة وانقسامها إلى هذين القسمين، فعن القسم الأول يستشهد بقوله تعالى : فَمَن لَّمْ يَسْتَطِعْ فَإِطْعَامُ سِيِّينَ مِسكينًا ﴾ [المجادلة: ٤] والمراد هنا سلامة الأسباب والآلات.
ويستشهد للقسم الثاني بقوله تعالى: مَا كَانُوا يَسْتَطِيعُونَ السَّمْعَ ﴾ [هود: ٢٠] والمراد منه نفي حقيقة القدرة لا نفي الأسباب والآلات، لأنها كانت ثابتة. وهذا موجب للذم ، لأنه تضييع للقدرة المعطاة من الله تعالى.
وبعد أن يدلل على وجود الاستطاعة بقسميها يقول : إنه بهذا يبطل قول من يقول لا استطاعة للإنسان وراء ذاته بل مستطيع بنفسه. كما قال النظام وعلى الأسواري، والأصم ، وكذا قول من يقول : إنه لا استطاعة غير سلامة الأسباب والآلات.
وكذا قول ضرار، وحفص الفرد بأن الاستطاعة بعض المستطيع، لأن الاستطاعة عرض، والقول بكون العرض بعض الجسم محال.
Discussion on capacity (istita'a): He defines istita'a as many theologians such as al-Ash'ari and al-Baqillani defined it: that it is power (qudra), strength (quwwa), and energy (taqa), all of which are close in meaning. He divides it into two parts: 1) Soundness of causes and health of instruments; these precede actions and are among the blessings of God Almighty. They are defined as readiness for executing the action when the free chooser wills. 2) It is a meaning difficult to define; it is an accident ('arad) that God creates in the living being to perform his voluntary actions, and it is the cause of the action. Then al-Nasafi adduces evidence for the existence of istita'a and its division into these two parts. For the first part, he quotes the verse: 'So whoever does not have the capacity (yastati') then the feeding of sixty poor people' [al-Mujadila:4] and the intended meaning here is soundness of causes and instruments. For the second part, he quotes the verse: 'They did not have the capacity (yastati'una) to hear' [Hud:20]; the intended meaning here is negation of the real power, not negation of causes and instruments, because they were established. And this warrants blame, because it is squandering the power given by God. After he demonstrates the existence of istita'a with its two parts, he says: by this is invalidated the saying of those who say there is no capacity for man beyond his self, rather he is capable by himself, as al-Nazzam, al-Uswari, and al-Asamm said; also the saying of those who say there is no capacity other than soundness of causes and instruments; also the saying of Dirar and Hafs al-Fard that capacity is a part of the capable one, because capacity is an accident, and the statement that an accident is part of a body is impossible.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage discusses istita'a (capacity/power). It begins by defining it as many theologians like al-Ash'ari and al-Baqillani did: it is synonymous with qudra (power), quwwa (strength), and taqa (energy). Then it divides istita'a into two types: (1) soundness of causes and health of instruments (salama al-asbab wa sihha al-alat), which precede the action and are blessings from God; they are defined as readiness and preparedness to execute the act when the willer chooses. (2) A second type, difficult to define, which is an accident ('arad) that God creates in the living being enabling voluntary actions; this capacity is the cause of the action. The author then provides Quranic evidence: for the first type, he cites the verse on expiation where inability (yastati') refers to lack of physical means. For the second type, he cites the verse about the disbelievers not being able to hear; this negation refers to real power (qudra) not the soundness of causes, since they still had hearing organs. This lack is blameworthy because they wasted the power God gave them. Finally, the passage refutes several wrong views: (a) those who deny any capacity beyond the self, like al-Nazzam, al-Uswari, al-Asamm (they say man is capable by himself); (b) those who say capacity is only soundness of causes; (c) Dirar and Hafs al-Fard who say capacity is a part of the capable one—this is impossible because capacity is an accident and cannot be a part of a body.

Key Terms
استطاعة
قدرة
قوة
طاقة
سلامة الأسباب
صحة الآلات
عرض
اختيارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think istita'a is either only the soundness of physical causes or a part of the agent's self.
Correction: Nasafi shows that istita'a includes a second type (a created accident) and that it is not a part of the body.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, which type of istita'a precedes the action?
A) The accident created by God.
B) The capacity that is part of the agent.
C) Soundness of causes and health of instruments.
D) The power of the soul.

Key Takeaway
Istita'a is twofold: the readiness of means (a blessing from God) and an accident (a created power) that enables voluntary acts; it is neither a part of the self nor merely the soundness of organs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accomplish a good deed, do you credit your own ability ('I was able because I am strong') or do you see it as a gift of power from Allah? Which of the two types of istita'a are you acknowledging, and which are you neglecting?
?
Al-Nasafi warns that the disbelievers 'could not hear' not because they lacked ears, but because they wasted Allah's gift of real power. Have you ever had the physical ability to do something good (like praying, giving charity, or seeking knowledge) yet failed to act? What did that reveal about your second type of istita'a?
?
Dirar and Hafs al-Fard thought capacity is a part of the person — something intrinsic. Do you subtly believe that your talents and abilities are 'yours' by nature, rather than a created accident that Allah gives and takes? How would your daily gratitude change if you truly felt that every moment of capability is a new creation from Allah?

Three Grounds for Denying Creation of Acts and the Kasb Conclusion
Al-Nasafi enumerates and refutes three grounds for denying God's creation of acts, concluding with the kasb doctrine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three reasons some deny that God creates human actions, Al-Nasafi's refutation of each, and the final Maturidi position that the act is created by Allah and acquired by the servant, preserving free choice.

Core Idea
Denying God's creation of acts rests on unsupported grounds; true doctrine affirms creation by God and acquisition by man.

Al-Nasafi concludes his extensive discussion on human actions by summarizing the positions of those who deny that God creates them. He identifies three possible motivations for such denial and counters each briefly. This passage encapsulates the key arguments and reaffirms the Maturidi doctrine of kasb, which holds that while God creates the act, the servant acquires it, thus maintaining moral responsibility and free choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the author's flow sink in.
Notice the threefold enumeration: 'إما أن يكون... أو... أو...'.
2
Identify the three reasons for denial
Find the three possible grounds for denying that God creates human acts: (1) lack of evidence, (2) alleged impossibility, (3) that it leads to compulsion and negates moral responsibility.
Each reason is introduced with 'إن كان إنكارهم...'.
3
Understand Al-Nasafi's refutations
For each reason, note Al-Nasafi's response: (1) he has provided proofs, (2) the deniers have only illusion, (3) he has refuted the compulsion argument.
The phrase 'وهم' (illusion) is key – the deniers lack real evidence.
4
Grasp the final conclusion on kasb
Read the concluding statement: the act relates to God as creation (ikhtirāʿ) and to the servant as acquisition (iktisāb). This leads to the servant being free (mukhtār), not compelled.
This is the Maturidi position that balances divine omnipotence and human responsibility.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this conclusion matters: it defends both God's sole creative power and human moral accountability.
Contrast with views that deny God's role (free will) or reduce humans to automatons (compulsion).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي النهاية يقول : إن الكلام في هذا الموضوع يحتاج إلى مجلد ضخم مثل تبصرة الأدلة. ويلخص الأمر فيها يلي: أن إنكار تخليق أفعال العباد إما أن يكون لما لا دلالة عليها أو للإحالة أو لما القول به من إيجاب الضرورة وامتناع التكليف، وبعد هذا الترديد يقول: إن كان إنكارهم لعدم الدلالة فقد أقمنا الكثير من الدلائل العقلية والسمعية. وإن كان إنكارهم للإحالة فيطالبون بدليلها، ولا دلائل معهم سوى الوهم، وإن كان إنكارهم لما فيه من إيجاب الضرورة فقد أجبنا عن ذلك ودفعنا الشبه، وثبت في النهاية أن الفعل يتعلق بالله سبحانه على طريق الخلق أي الاختراع، وبالعبد على طريق الاكتساب . وهذا يؤدي إلى أن العبد مختار، وليس بمضطر.
And in the end he says: Indeed, the discussion on this topic requires a large volume like Tabsirat al-Adilla. And he summarizes the matter as follows: The denial of the creation of the actions of servants is either due to lack of evidence for it, or due to impossibility, or due to what follows from saying it of necessitating compulsion and precluding moral responsibility. And after this enumeration he says: If their denial is due to lack of evidence, we have established many intellectual and scriptural proofs. If their denial is due to impossibility, they are required to provide evidence for that, and they have no proofs except illusion (wahm). And if their denial is due to what follows from saying it of necessitating compulsion, we have responded to that and refuted the ambiguities. And it is established in the end that the act is related to Allah the Exalted in the way of creation, i.e., invention (ikhtirāʿ), and to the servant in the way of acquisition (iktisāb). And this leads to the conclusion that the servant is free (mukhtār), not compelled (muḍṭarr).
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion on the creation of human actions. The author notes that a full treatment would require a large volume like the Tabsirat al-Adilla itself, but he summarizes the refutation of those who deny that God creates human acts. He identifies three possible grounds for their denial: (1) lack of evidence; (2) alleging impossibility; (3) claiming that affirming creation of acts leads to inherent compulsion (idṭirār) and thus negates moral responsibility (taklīf). He counters each: for (1), he says many rational and scriptural proofs have been presented; for (2), the deniers have no proof except illusion (wahm); for (3), the arguments for compulsion have been refuted. The settled conclusion is that the act relates to God as creation (khalq, i.e., invention), and to the servant as acquisition (kasb/iktisāb). This preserves the servant's free choice (mukhtār) without compulsion.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That kasb means the servant has no real role, merely passive reception.
Correction: Al-Nasafi affirms that the act is related to the servant 'through acquisition' (iktisāb), which implies an active role, leading to the conclusion that the servant is free (mukhtār), not compelled.

Checkpoint

A) Lack of evidence.

Key Takeaway
The act is created by Allah and acquired by the servant, preserving human free choice and moral responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Decreed Term of the Murdered Person
Nasafi argues that the murdered person dies at his decreed term, and his death is created by God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that the murdered person dies at his decreed term and that death is created by God, not caused by the killer.

Core Idea
The murdered person dies exactly at his appointed term, not a moment sooner.

This passage addresses a critical question in Islamic theology: does murder cut short a person's lifespan? Nasafi defends the Sunni view that every soul dies at its decreed term, and that death itself is an act of God. Through scriptural evidence, he refutes the Mu'tazili position and clarifies the relationship between divine decree and human action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the key terms like 'ajal' (term) and 'qatl' (killing).
Notice that the passage begins with a question and then gives Nasafi's position.
2
Identify the main claim and evidence
Identify Nasafi's central claim: the murdered person dies at his term. Then find the Quranic verse and two hadiths he uses as evidence.
The verse (16:61) emphasizes the fixedness of the term.
3
Understand the distinction between death and killing
Nasafi distinguishes between death (created by God in the victim) and killing (the action of the killer). This is crucial for absolving God of injustice while holding the killer accountable.
The killer is sinful for his action, but the death is still by God's decree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في الآجال: يتحدث النسفي عن المقتول هل مات بأجله أم قطع عليه أجله؟ فيثبت أنه ميت بأجله، وما يخلق الله فيه من المعنى المنافي للحياة مفعول الله تعالى، وليس بفعل ، وهو الموت، والقتل فعل القاتل قائم به ليس بحال في المقتول. للقاتل، ثم يعرض آراء المعتزلة، ليبطل الخارج منها عن رأي أهل السنة والجماعة مثل من يقول : المقتول مقطوع عليه أجله، وكان يمكن أن يعيش أكثر من ذلك ويستدل النسفي بقوله تعالى: فَإِذَا جَاءَ أَجَلُهُمْ لَا يَسْتَأْخِرُونَ سَاعَةٌ وَلَا يَسْتَقْدِمُونَ ) [النحل: ٦١] وبحديث : إن أحدكم ليطلبه رزقه كما يطلبه أجله) وبحديث: (رزق العبد وأجله يكتب على جبهته وهو في بطن أمه....).
Discussion on decreed lifespans (ajal): Nasafi discusses the murdered person: does he die at his term or is his term cut off? He affirms that he dies at his term, and what God creates in him of the meaning contrary to life is an act of God Almighty, and is not an action, and it is death. Killing is the action of the killer, subsisting in him, not occurring in the murdered. For the killer? Then he presents the views of the Mu'tazila, to refute what is contrary to the view of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a, such as those who say: the murdered is cut off from his term, and he could have lived longer. And Nasafi adduces the verse: 'So when their term comes, they will not delay an hour nor advance it' (16:61), and the hadith: 'One of you is sought by his provision as he is sought by his term,' and the hadith: 'The provision and term of the servant are written on his forehead while he is in his mother's womb.'
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about predestination and lifespan. Nasafi addresses the question of whether a murdered person dies at his decreed term (ajal) or whether his term is cut short by the murder. He affirms the former: he dies exactly at his appointed term. The death itself is a meaning that God creates in the person, which is an act of God, not an action of the killer. The killer's act of killing is an action that subsists in the killer, not in the victim. Thus, the murderer does not cause death; God creates death at the appointed time. Nasafi then criticizes the Mu'tazili view that the murdered's term is cut short and he could have lived longer, using Quranic verse (16:61) and hadiths to support that the term is fixed and cannot be delayed or advanced.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this view absolves the killer of responsibility.
Correction: It does not absolve the killer; killing is a sinful action of the killer, but the death is still by God's decree at the appointed term.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi affirm about the murdered person's term?
A) His term is cut short.
B) He dies at his term.
C) He dies without a term.

Key Takeaway

?

Nasafi on Sustenance (Arzaq): Each Person Consumes His Own Provision
Nasafi states his view on sustenance and responds to Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's position on sustenance and how he reconciles with the Mu'tazila by distinguishing between sustenance as physical food and as ownership.

Core Idea
Every person consumes their own designated sustenance, whether halal or haram.

Nasafi addresses a key point in predestination: whose sustenance does a person actually consume? He firmly states that each person eats his own, but he acknowledges the Mu'tazila objection and offers a nuanced concession. This lesson clarifies that position and the key distinction between sustenance as physical provision and as a right of ownership.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
Notice that Nasafi uses a very concise style.
2
Identify Nasafi's main claim
Find the sentence where Nasafi states that each person receives and eats his own sustenance, whether halal or haram.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila view
Read how Nasafi presents the Mu'tazila objection: that a person might eat another's sustenance.
This is the contrary position he is addressing.
4
Grasp Nasafi's concession
Note the final part where Nasafi says he permits the Mu'tazila view if 'sustenance' is interpreted as ownership (tamalluk).
This is a linguistic and theological nuance.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this concession matters: it shows Nasafi's flexibility in reconciling predestination with apparent experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في الأرزاق: ويتحدث في مسألة الأرزاق فيبين أن كل شخص يستوفى رزق نفسه، ويأكله حلالا كان أو حراما، خلافا للمعتزلة الذين يقولون من الجائز أن لا يأكل الإنسان رزق نفسه، بل يأكل رزق غيره، كما يأكل غيره رزقه، ويجوز النسفي ذلك إذا حمل الرزق على التملك.
Discussion on sustenance (arzaq): He discusses the issue of sustenance, clarifying that every person receives his own sustenance and consumes it, whether it is lawful or unlawful, contrary to the Mu'tazila who say it is possible that a person does not eat his own sustenance but eats the sustenance of another, just as another eats his sustenance. Nasafi allows that if sustenance is interpreted as ownership (tamalluk).
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a brief discussion on predestination and sustenance. Nasafi affirms that a person always consumes his own designated sustenance (rizq), regardless of whether it is halal or haram. This is in contrast to the Mu'tazila who claim that a person might eat what belongs to someone else's sustenance. Nasafi, however, concedes that such a scenario could be allowed if one understands 'sustenance' as 'ownership' (tamalluk) — meaning the sustenance is not predetermined as the exact physical food but rather as the right to possess. This nuance shows that Nasafi is willing to reconcile linguistic usage.

Correction: Nasafi actually affirms that people can eat haram food, but that food is still their designated sustenance.

C) The Mu'tazila are correct.
D) It never happens.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi maintains that every person consumes their own predetermined sustenance, whether halal or haram, but he allows a linguistic concession that if 'sustenance' means ownership, then one could theoretically consume another's.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Term of the Killed Person
Al-Nasafi proves that the killed person dies by his appointed term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in Sunni theology, a person who is killed dies exactly at his appointed term, and that the killer's act does not cut short the victim's life.

Core Idea
The killed person dies by his decreed term, not an hour earlier or later.

This lesson examines a key issue in qadar (divine decree): does a murder cut short the victim's life? Al-Nasafi affirms that every soul dies at its appointed term, and the killer's act is separate from the creation of death. He supports this with clear Quranic and prophetic evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall flow. Notice that it begins with a question and then affirms a position.
The passage contains a theological distinction and evidence.
2
Identify the main claim
Al-Nasafi's main claim is that the killed person dies by his appointed term, not that his term is cut short.
This is the central thesis.
3
Understand the distinction
Note that death is created by God, while killing is the act of the killer. The two are not the same; the killer does not cause death directly.
This distinction is key to the argument.
4
Consider the refuted view
Al-Nasafi presents the Mu'tazilite view that the killed person could have lived longer, then refutes it.
He argues that this view contradicts divine decree.
5
Examine the evidence
He uses Quran 16:61 and two hadiths to support his position. Reflect on how these texts affirm that the term is fixed and cannot be changed.

Defining Rizq: Provision as Decreed Nourishment
al-Nasafi defines rizq as the nourishment God has decreed for each person.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's definition of rizq as the nourishment God decrees for each person, and how it differs from the Mu'tazilite view.

Core Idea
Rizq is not what you own, but what you consume by God's decree.

Al-Nasafi tackles the theological definition of provision (rizq), a key issue in discussions of divine decree and human action. He contrasts his view with that of the Mu'tazila and ultimately settles on a definition that focuses on actual consumption rather than ownership. This passage clarifies a common confusion about what exactly constitutes one's provision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage attentively
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the contrast with the Mu'tazila.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite view
Find the statement that a person might not eat his own provision but another's. Understand why al-Nasafi initially opposes this.
Al-Nasafi allows it only if rizq means ownership.
3
Note the scholarly resolution
Al-Nasafi mentions two shaykhs who say the disagreement is verbal. He agrees. Consider the significance: the debate is about wording, not substance.
4
Extract the final definition
Al-Nasafi defines rizq as the nourishment (ghidha') that God decrees for a person, which no one else will eat. Write it down.
This definition emphasizes actual consumption, not ownership.
5
Reflect on the theological implications
How does this definition relate to qadar? Why does it matter that rizq is guaranteed to be consumed?
This resolves concerns about whether someone can miss their provision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتحدث في مسألة الأرزاق فيبين أن كل شخص يستوفى رزق نفسه، ويأكله حلالا كان أو حراما، خلافا للمعتزلة الذين يقولون من الجائز أن لا يأكل الإنسان رزق نفسه، بل يأكل رزق غيره، كما يأكل غيره رزقه، ويجوز النسفي ذلك إذا حمل الرزق على التملك. ويعرف الرزق ويقول في النهاية: إن الشيخين الرستغفني، وأبا إسحاق الأسفراييني قالا: إن الخلاف في العبارة. وهو يرى أن هذا هو الصواب. ويعرف النسفي الرزق بأنه الغذاء، فما قدره الله تعالى ليكون غذاء لفرد لا يأكله أحد أبدا.
And he discusses the issue of provisions (arzaq) and explains that every person receives his own provision and eats it, whether lawful or unlawful, contrary to the Mu'tazila who say that it is possible that a person does not eat his own provision but eats the provision of another, just as another eats his provision. Al-Nasafi permits that if provision is interpreted as ownership. And he defines provision and says in the end: The two shaykhs, al-Rustughfani and Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, said that the disagreement is in expression. And he sees that this is correct. And al-Nasafi defines provision as the nourishment; what God Almighty has decreed to be nourishment for an individual, no one ever eats it.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage discusses the definition of rizq (provision). Al-Nasafi first explains that every person receives his own provision and eats it, whether it is lawful or unlawful, contradicting the Mu'tazilite view that a person can eat the provision of another. However, al-Nasafi allows that possibility if rizq is interpreted as ownership (tamalluk). Then he mentions that the two shaykhs al-Rustughfani and Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini said the disagreement is merely verbal, and al-Nasafi agrees that this is correct. Finally, al-Nasafi defines rizq as the nourishment (ghidha') that God has decreed for a particular individual, and which no one else will ever eat. This definition emphasizes that rizq is what is actually consumed, not what is owned.

Key Terms
الرزق
الغذاء
التملك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rizq is everything one owns or earns, regardless of consumption.
Correction: Al-Nasafi defines rizq specifically as the nourishment God decrees for a person, which that person actually consumes, not merely what they own or earn.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi's final definition, what is rizq?
A) Everything one owns.
B) The nourishment that God decrees for a person and which he actually eats.
C) Any property acquired by lawful means.
D) Whatever a person earns through his own efforts.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi defines rizq strictly as the nourishment God decrees for a person, which that person will inevitably consume, regardless of its lawfulness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you worry about losing your income or possessions, are you truly believing that your rizq—the nourishment you need—is already decreed and cannot be taken by anyone? What would change in your daily anxiety if you internalized this definition of rizq as consumption rather than ownership?
?
Al-Nasafi insists that even if you acquire your provision unlawfully, it is still your decreed rizq. Have you ever justified a doubtful income by telling yourself 'it's my rizq'? How does this lesson challenge that excuse, and what will you do the next time such a temptation arises?
?
If you truly believed that no one can eat the sustenance Allah has decreed for you, how would your envy of others' wealth or your fear of poverty dissolve? What concrete step can you take today to act on that belief, rather than just intellectually acknowledging it?

Al-Qattan and al-Ash'ari on God's Will for Evil
Nasafi presents al-Qattan's and al-Ash'ari's views on God's will for evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Qattan's general statement on God's will for all events and al-Ash'ari's detailed position on how to speak about God's will for evil actions.

Core Idea
God's will encompasses all, but evil must be attributed with a qualifier.

This lesson presents two early Kalam positions on God's will for evil. Al-Qattan states a broad principle: God wills everything that happens. Al-Ash'ari refines this by insisting that when speaking of evil, we must add a qualifying phrase to avoid misunderstanding. Their views set the stage for later discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Qattan's statement
Notice that al-Qattan asserts God's will over all occurrences without exception.
This is a general principle.
2
Examine al-Ash'ari's qualification
Al-Ash'ari requires a qarina: God wills unbelief as an ugly acquisition from the disbeliever.
This avoids attributing evil directly to God.
3
Compare the two views
See how al-Ash'ari builds on al-Qattan but adds a protective caveat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يعرض بعض الأقوال فيقول : قال عبد الله بن سعيد القطان: إن الله تعالى مريد حدوث كل ما علم حدوثه، ولا يكون في سلطانه إلا ما يريد كونه ما شاء الله كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن. وقد اختار الأشعري القول عند التفصيل أن لا يقتصر على القول: أن الله سبحانه أراد الكفر، والزنا بل أزيد عليه قرينة، فأقول : أراد من الكافر الكفر كسبا له قبيحا منه، مذموما. ويقول النسفي : إن رأى الأشعري قريب من رأي أبي منصور الماتريدي، ولا خلاف بينهما، فأبو منصور يحدد في هذه المسألة وجهين: الأول: الإطلاق على المفهوم من الإرادة، والثاني : منع الإطلاق إذا لم يفهم مراد السائل.
Then he presents some sayings and says: 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan said: Indeed, Allah Almighty is the willer of the occurrence of everything whose occurrence He knows, and nothing occurs in His dominion except what He wills to be; whatever Allah wills is, and whatever He does not will is not. And al-Ash'ari chose, in detailed discussion, that one should not restrict to saying: Allah, exalted, willed unbelief and adultery, but rather I add a qualifying indication (qarina), so I say: He willed from the disbeliever unbelief as an acquisition (kash) from him, which is ugly (qabih) from him, blameworthy (madhmun). And al-Nasafi says: Indeed, al-Ash'ari's view is close to the view of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, and there is no disagreement between them. For Abu Mansur specifies in this matter two aspects: first, speaking absolutely according to the understood meaning of will; second, forbidding absolute statement if the questioner's intent is not understood.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage collects several positions on how to speak about God's will for evil. First, al-Qattan states that God wills everything that He knows will occur, and nothing occurs against His will. Then al-Ash'ari's detailed position: one should not simply say 'God willed unbelief,' but should qualify it by stating that He willed the unbelief as an ugly and blameworthy acquisition from the disbeliever. This prevents absolute attribution of will to evil without context. Al-Nasafi then draws a comparison: al-Ash'ari's view is close to Abu Mansur al-Maturidi's. Al-Maturidi distinguishes two scenarios: if the meaning of 'will' is understood, one may speak absolutely; if the questioner's intent is unclear, one should avoid absolute statements to prevent misunderstanding. Thus the difference is more about expression than doctrine.

Key Terms
عبد الله بن سعيد القطان
الأشعري
الكسب القبيح
القرينة
الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think al-Ash'ari simply says that God wills evil without any qualification.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari explicitly adds a qarina (qualifying indication) to state that God wills evil only as an ugly acquisition from the human agent, not as an unqualified divine act.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, how should one correctly articulate God's will for unbelief?
A) Simply say 'God willed unbelief'
B) Add that He willed it as an ugly acquisition from the disbeliever
C) Deny that God wills it at all
D) Say that God willed it as a good act

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari qualifies God's will for evil by framing it as an ugly human acquisition, not an unqualified divine attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely thought of evil acts in the world as outside God's will. Does al-Ash'ari's qualification change how you view the evil you have committed? Are you ready to see your own sins as ugly acquisitions that God willed only in terms of existence, not approval? What will you do with that awareness today?
?
You may have blamed God for the evil you see. But this lesson teaches that God's will for evil includes a qualifier—it is blameworthy from the human. Can you accept responsibility for your wrongdoings without excusing them as God's will? When you sin, do you attribute it to your own ugly acquisition or to God's decree? What change in your repentance does this demand?
?
Al-Ash'ari teaches that we should not simply say 'God willed unbelief' without qualification. How often do you speak about God's involvement in evil without careful thought? Will you commit to speaking more precisely about God's will, especially regarding evil, so that you do not attribute ugliness to Him?

Al-Nasafi's Comparison: Al-Ash'ari and Al-Maturidi
Nasafi compares al-Ash'ari and al-Maturidi and presents al-Maturidi's two-aspect approach.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi reconciles al-Ash'ari and al-Maturidi's views on articulating God's will for evil, including al-Maturidi's two aspects.

Core Idea
Ash'ari and Maturidi are close; the difference is in expression, not doctrine.

Al-Nasafi concludes his presentation by comparing al-Ash'ari's view with that of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. He asserts that there is no fundamental disagreement. Al-Maturidi then elaborates two contexts: one where absolute statements are permissible and another where they must be avoided. This lesson clarifies that the two schools are united on the core issue.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Nasafi's comparison
Notice that al-Nasafi says the views of al-Ash'ari and al-Maturidi are close.
This is a surprising claim given perceived differences.
2
Study al-Maturidi's two aspects
Al-Maturidi distinguishes between when the meaning is understood (absolute statements allowed) and when it is not (avoid absolute statements).
This shows sensitivity to context.
3
Reflect on the reconciliation
Consider how al-Ash'ari's qarina aligns with al-Maturidi's second aspect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يعرض بعض الأقوال فيقول : قال عبد الله بن سعيد القطان: إن الله تعالى مريد حدوث كل ما علم حدوثه، ولا يكون في سلطانه إلا ما يريد كونه ما شاء الله كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن. وقد اختار الأشعري القول عند التفصيل أن لا يقتصر على القول: أن الله سبحانه أراد الكفر، والزنا بل أزيد عليه قرينة، فأقول : أراد من الكافر الكفر كسبا له قبيحا منه، مذموما. ويقول النسفي : إن رأى الأشعري قريب من رأي أبي منصور الماتريدي، ولا خلاف بينهما، فأبو منصور يحدد في هذه المسألة وجهين: الأول: الإطلاق على المفهوم من الإرادة، والثاني : منع الإطلاق إذا لم يفهم مراد السائل.
Then he presents some sayings and says: 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan said: Indeed, Allah Almighty is the willer of the occurrence of everything whose occurrence He knows, and nothing occurs in His dominion except what He wills to be; whatever Allah wills is, and whatever He does not will is not. And al-Ash'ari chose, in detailed discussion, that one should not restrict to saying: Allah, exalted, willed unbelief and adultery, but rather I add a qualifying indication (qarina), so I say: He willed from the disbeliever unbelief as an acquisition (kash) from him, which is ugly (qabih) from him, blameworthy (madhmun). And al-Nasafi says: Indeed, al-Ash'ari's view is close to the view of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, and there is no disagreement between them. For Abu Mansur specifies in this matter two aspects: first, speaking absolutely according to the understood meaning of will; second, forbidding absolute statement if the questioner's intent is not understood.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage collects several positions on how to speak about God's will for evil. First, al-Qattan states that God wills everything that He knows will occur, and nothing occurs against His will. Then al-Ash'ari's detailed position: one should not simply say 'God willed unbelief,' but should qualify it by stating that He willed the unbelief as an ugly and blameworthy acquisition from the disbeliever. This prevents absolute attribution of will to evil without context. Al-Nasafi then draws a comparison: al-Ash'ari's view is close to Abu Mansur al-Maturidi's. Al-Maturidi distinguishes two scenarios: if the meaning of 'will' is understood, one may speak absolutely; if the questioner's intent is unclear, one should avoid absolute statements to prevent misunderstanding. Thus the difference is more about expression than doctrine.

Key Terms

الإطلاق
مراد السائل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ash'ari and Maturidi fundamentally disagree on whether God wills evil.
Correction: Al-Nasafi says there is no disagreement; their views are close. Al-Maturidi's two aspects align with al-Ash'ari's qualified formulation.

Checkpoint
According to al-Maturidi, when is it permissible to speak absolutely about God's will?
A) Always
B) When the meaning of 'will' is understood
C) Never
D) Only when talking about good deeds

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi shows that al-Ash'ari and al-Maturidi are essentially agreed; al-Maturidi's two aspects provide a contextual guideline for speaking about God's will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about God's will in relation to your own difficulties, do you ever make absolute statements like 'God willed this evil for me' without considering how others might misunderstand? How can you adopt al-Maturidi's caution in your conversations today?
?
Think of a time you were certain about God's will in a situation—perhaps when something bad happened. Were you speaking about God's general will (which includes everything) or arrogantly claiming specific knowledge? What would change if you applied al-Maturidi's second aspect—refusing to speak absolutely when intent is unclear?
?
This lesson shows that even great scholars are careful to avoid misunderstanding when discussing God's decree. Where in your life are you quick to make absolute claims about what God wills for others or yourself? Commit to one specific change in how you speak about qadar, starting now.

The Identity of Divine Will and Creation of Actions
Nasafi identifies will with creation and presents Mu'tazila proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi equates the issue of divine will with the creation of human actions, and you will recognize the Mu'tazila's scriptural and rational objections to this view.

Core Idea
Divine will and creation of actions are one issue; Mu'tazila objections are based on analogy and scripture.

In this lesson, we see Imam al-Nasafi asserting a key Maturidi principle: the issue of divine will is essentially the same as the issue of God creating actions. He then presents the two main proofs that the Mu'tazila use to argue that God does not will evil or disbelief. Understanding these objections is crucial before encountering their refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text from 'ثم يقول' up to 'فكذا في الغائب'. Note how Nasafi declares the issues identical and then lists the Mu'tazila's two types of proofs.
The sentence 'وهذه المسألة هي عين مسألة' appears incomplete but expresses identity.
2
Identify the linkage of issues
Observe that Nasafi says the proof for God creating all actions also applies to the issue of divine will. This shows that for Maturidis, will and creation are inseparable.
This is a fundamental principle in Maturidi theology.
3
Examine the scriptural objection
The Mu'tazila cite Q. 51:56, stating that God created jinn and humans only to worship Him. They argue that if God willed disbelief, He would be contradicting His stated purpose.
The Maturidi response is that the verse states the general purpose, not that God cannot will otherwise.
4
Analyze the rational objection
The Mu'tazila use analogy from the witnessed world: one who wills foolishness is foolish, and one who wills unbelief is an unbeliever. They apply this to God, concluding that if God willed disbelief, He would be an unbeliever—which is impossible, so He cannot will disbelief.
This is a key objection that Maturidis refute by denying the validity of such an analogy for God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقول: إن مسألة خلق أفعال العباد، وقد بينا أن خالقها كلها هو الله تعالى، وما هو دليله هناك، فهو دليله هنا. وهذه المسألة هي عين مسألة ويبدأ في الرد على شبه المعتزلة النقلية والعقلية فمن النقلية قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَمَا خَلَقْتُ الجنَّ وَالْإِنسَ إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُونِ ﴾ [الذاريات: ٥٦] ومن العقلية قولهم: مريد السفه سفيه اعتبارا بالشاهد، ومريد الكفر كافر في الشاهد، فكذا في الغائب. وبعد رده يذكر لتأكيد كلامه قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَا يَحْسَبَنَّ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا أَنَّمَا نُمْلِي هُمْ خَيْرٌ لأَنفُسِهِمْ إِنَّمَا نُمْلِي لَهُمْ لِيَزْدَادُوا إِثْمًا } [آل عمران: ۱۷۸] فقد دلت الآية أنه أراد منهم أزدياد الإثم، وقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَقَدْ ذَرَأْنَا لِجَهَنَّمَ كَثِيرًا مِّنَ الْجِنِّ وَالْإِنسِ ﴾ [الأعراف: ۱۷۹] أي أراد منهم ما به يصيرون لجهنم.
Then he says: Indeed, the issue of the creation of the actions of servants – and we have shown that their Creator is Allah Almighty – and whatever is the proof for that there (i.e., in that issue) is the proof here. And this issue is exactly the issue of... And he begins to refute the Mu'tazila's scriptural and rational doubts. As for the scriptural, His statement, exalted: 'And I did not create the jinn and mankind except to worship Me' [Q. 51:56]. And as for the rational, their statement: 'The willer of foolishness is foolish, by analogy to the witnessed world; and the willer of unbelief is an unbeliever in the witnessed world; thus also in the unseen.' And after his refutation, he mentions to confirm his speech His statement, exalted: 'And let not those who disbelieve think that what We grant them is good for themselves; We only grant them so that they may increase in sin' [Q. 3:178]. For the verse indicates that He willed from them an increase of sin. And His statement, exalted: 'And We have certainly created for Hell many of the jinn and mankind' [Q. 7:179], meaning He willed from them that by which they become (destined) for Hell.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage begins by explicitly linking the issue of divine will to the issue of the creation of human actions. Al-Nasafi states that the proof he has already presented for God creating all actions serves as the proof here as well, indicating that these two issues are essentially the same. Then he starts refuting the Mu'tazila's objections, both scriptural and rational. The scriptural objection is the verse 'I did not create jinn and men except to worship Me,' implying that God wills only worship, not disbelief. The rational objection is an analogy: in the witnessed world, one who wills foolishness is called foolish, and one who wills unbelief is called an unbeliever; therefore, since God wills unbelief, He would be an unbeliever – which is absurd, so God cannot will unbelief. Al-Nasafi then presents his refutation (though the details of the refutation are not given here) and then brings additional Quranic verses to confirm his own position: Surah Al-Imran 3:178 shows that God wills increase of sin for disbelievers, and Surah Al-A'raf 7:179 shows that God created many for Hell, meaning He willed their ultimate destiny.

Key Terms
خلق أفعال العباد
شبه المعتزلة
النقلية
العقلية
الذاريات: ٥٦
الشاهد
الغائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the verse 'except to worship Me' proves God only wills good and cannot will disbelief.
Correction: Maturidis interpret this verse as stating the general purpose of creation, not a limitation on God's will. God may will disbelief for some while still creating them for worship in a broader sense.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazila's rational doubt (al-shubha al-ʿaqliyya) presented by Nasafi?
A) That God cannot will evil because He is good
B) That the willer of foolishness is foolish, and the willer of unbelief is an unbeliever, by analogy from the witnessed world
C) That God's will is limited by human free will
D) That the Qur'an does not mention God's will for evil

Key Takeaway
For Nasafi, divine will and creation are identical; Mu'tazila objections rely on a literal reading of one verse and an analogy from human experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own actions, both the good and the bad, do you truly believe that Allah is the sole Creator of every single one of them? How does this belief change the way you view your own sins and the sins of others? What would it mean to accept that even your disobedience is created by Allah, and yet you are still responsible? Commit to one specific way you will let this truth transform your repentance this week.
?
The Mu'tazila argued that if God willed disbelief, He would be like a willer of disbelief in the human world. Are there areas in your life where you unknowingly judge Allah by human standards? Do you ever question Allah's wisdom because He allows evil or disbelief to exist? How can you actively guard against such comparisons and instead submit to His absolute sovereignty? Choose one doubt you often have and decide today to replace it with trust in Allah's perfect wisdom.
?
The verse 'I only created jinn and mankind to worship Me' might be misunderstood as limiting God's will. Have you ever felt that certain events or people's actions contradict God's stated purpose? How does this lesson help you reconcile God's ultimate purpose with the reality of widespread disbelief and sin? What step will you take this week to strengthen your conviction that Allah's will is not constrained by His purpose, and that He is the Creator of all things, including what seems contrary to His command?

Confirming Divine Will with Quranic Verses
Nasafi cites Quran 3:178 and 7:179 to confirm divine will for sin and Hell.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Quranic verses from Surah Al-Imran and Al-A'raf confirm that Allah wills increase of sin for disbelievers and creates people for Hell.

Core Idea
Quranic verses affirm that Allah wills sin and disbelief for some, refuting the Mu'tazila.

After refuting the Mu'tazila's objections (not detailed here), Imam al-Nasafi cites two powerful Quranic verses to support the Maturidi position that Allah indeed wills disbelief and sin. The first verse shows that Allah prolongs the lives of disbelievers to increase their sin, and the second declares that Allah created many for Hell. These verses directly counter the claim that God only wills good.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage from 'وبعد رده' to end
Read the Arabic for the two verses and Nasafi's interpretation. Notice that he says these verses confirm his previous refutation.
The actual refutation is not given here; only the supporting verses are provided.
2
Understand the first verse (Q. 3:178)
This verse states that disbelievers should not think that the respite given to them is good; rather, it is to increase their sin. Nasafi concludes that God willed from them an increase of sin.
The Arabic says 'أراد منهم أزدياد الإثم' - He willed from them increase of sin.
3
Understand the second verse (Q. 7:179)
This verse says Allah created many jinn and humans for Hell. Nasafi interprets this as meaning He willed from them the actions that lead to Hell.
The phrase 'أي أراد منهم ما به يصيرون لجهنم' clarifies that God's will is involved.
4
Reflect on the combined evidence
These verses together show that God actively wills sin and unbelief for certain individuals, contradicting the Mu'tazila claim that He only wills good.
This is a direct scriptural argument for the Maturidi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقول: إن مسألة خلق أفعال العباد، وقد بينا أن خالقها كلها هو الله تعالى، وما هو دليله هناك، فهو دليله هنا. وهذه المسألة هي عين مسألة ويبدأ في الرد على شبه المعتزلة النقلية والعقلية فمن النقلية قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَمَا خَلَقْتُ الجنَّ وَالْإِنسَ إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُونِ ﴾ [الذاريات: ٥٦] ومن العقلية قولهم: مريد السفه سفيه اعتبارا بالشاهد، ومريد الكفر كافر في الشاهد، فكذا في الغائب. وبعد رده يذكر لتأكيد كلامه قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَا يَحْسَبَنَّ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا أَنَّمَا نُمْلِي هُمْ خَيْرٌ لأَنفُسِهِمْ إِنَّمَا نُمْلِي لَهُمْ لِيَزْدَادُوا إِثْمًا } [آل عمران: ۱۷۸] فقد دلت الآية أنه أراد منهم أزدياد الإثم، وقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَقَدْ ذَرَأْنَا لِجَهَنَّمَ كَثِيرًا مِّنَ الْجِنِّ وَالْإِنسِ ﴾ [الأعراف: ۱۷۹] أي أراد منهم ما به يصيرون لجهنم.
Then he says: Indeed, the issue of the creation of the actions of servants – and we have shown that their Creator is Allah Almighty – and whatever is the proof for that there (i.e., in that issue) is the proof here. And this issue is exactly the issue of... And he begins to refute the Mu'tazila's scriptural and rational doubts. As for the scriptural, His statement, exalted: 'And I did not create the jinn and mankind except to worship Me' [Q. 51:56]. And as for the rational, their statement: 'The willer of foolishness is foolish, by analogy to the witnessed world; and the willer of unbelief is an unbeliever in the witnessed world; thus also in the unseen.' And after his refutation, he mentions to confirm his speech His statement, exalted: 'And let not those who disbelieve think that what We grant them is good for themselves; We only grant them so that they may increase in sin' [Q. 3:178]. For the verse indicates that He willed from them an increase of sin. And His statement, exalted: 'And We have certainly created for Hell many of the jinn and mankind' [Q. 7:179], meaning He willed from them that by which they become (destined) for Hell.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage begins by explicitly linking the issue of divine will to the issue of the creation of human actions. Al-Nasafi states that the proof he has already presented for God creating all actions serves as the proof here as well, indicating that these two issues are essentially the same. Then he starts refuting the Mu'tazila's objections, both scriptural and rational. The scriptural objection is the verse 'I did not create jinn and men except to worship Me,' implying that God wills only worship, not disbelief. The rational objection is an analogy: in the witnessed world, one who wills foolishness is called foolish, and one who wills unbelief is called an unbeliever; therefore, since God wills unbelief, He would be an unbeliever – which is absurd, so God cannot will unbelief. Al-Nasafi then presents his refutation (though the details of the refutation are not given here) and then brings additional Quranic verses to confirm his own position: Surah Al-Imran 3:178 shows that God wills increase of sin for disbelievers, and Surah Al-A'raf 7:179 shows that God created many for Hell, meaning He willed their ultimate destiny.

Key Terms
آل عمران: ۱۷۸
الأعراف: ۱۷۹
أزدياد الإثم
جهنم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that these verses only describe consequences or God's foreknowledge, not His deliberate will.
Correction: Nasafi explicitly states that the verses show God 'willed' (arada) the increase of sin and the destiny for Hell, indicating active volition.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what does the verse from Surah Al-Imran (3:178) indicate about God's will?
A) That God willed to punish disbelievers in this world
B) That God willed from them an increase of sin
C) That God willed to give them a chance to repent
D) That God willed to test their patience

Key Takeaway
Quranic evidence shows that Allah wills both good and evil deeds, and even creates some for eternal punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you read that Allah created some for Hell, does it stir fear in your heart about your own destiny? How do you reconcile the doctrine of divine will with personal responsibility? What concrete step will you take today to ensure you are not among those for whom Hell is decreed?

The Argument That God's Will Prevails Over the Servant's Will
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila position via reductio, using a king analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument proving that God's will cannot be undermined by any created will, and why the Mu'tazila position leads to an impossible consequence.

Core Idea
If God's will could be thwarted, He would not be God.

This passage presents a powerful reductio ad absurdum argument. Nasafi asks us to consider what would happen if God willed belief from a disbeliever but the disbeliever willed disbelief, and disbelief actually occurred. The conclusion is clear: such a scenario would imply that God's will is ineffective and the servant's will is more powerful — an impossibility for the All-Powerful Creator. This argument thus decisively refutes the Mu'tazila view and exposes its logical flaw.

Guided Path
1
Understand the hypothetical scenario
Read the first part of the passage where Nasafi sets up a conditional: if God wills belief from a disbeliever, the disbeliever wills disbelief, and disbelief occurs instead of belief. Consider the implications of this scenario.
This is the core of the reductio: assuming the Mu'tazila position leads to a contradiction.
2
Analyze the analogy of the king
Nasafi compares God to a king in whose kingdom something occurs that he does not will, and what he wills does not occur. This indicates the king's weakness. Since God cannot be weak, the scenario is impossible.
The analogy makes the logical flaw concrete and intuitive.
3
Grasp the theological conclusion
Nasafi concludes that the Mu'tazila view leads to dualism (positing two independent wills), which is shirk. Understand why this is a devastating refutation.
This argument safeguards divine omnipotence and transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقول : أنه تعالى لو شاء من الكافر الإيمان والكافر شاء من نفسه الكفر ثم كان الكفر دون الإيمان، لتعطلت مشيئة الله، وكانت مشيئة الكافر أنفذ من مشيئة الله، ومن أول الدلائل على عجز الملك وضعفه أن يقع في ملكه ما لا يشاء ويشاء أشياء فلا تقع، ووصف الله بذلك محال. وهذا الكلام يرد على دلالة التمانع بالإبطال ويؤدي إلى جعل مذهب الثنوية
Then he says: If He, exalted, willed from the disbeliever belief, and the disbeliever willed from himself disbelief, and then disbelief occurred instead of belief, then the will of Allah would be thwarted, and the will of the disbeliever would be more effective than the will of Allah. And one of the first proofs of a king's inability and weakness is that something he does not will occurs in his kingdom and he wills things that do not occur. And describing Allah with that is impossible. And this speech refutes the implication of mutual exclusion (dalālat al-tamānuʿ) by invalidating it and leads to adopting the sect of dualism (madhhab al-thunawiyya).
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents a reductio ad absurdum argument against the Mu'tazila position that God wills belief from the disbeliever but the disbeliever wills disbelief and then disbelief happens. If that were so, God's will would be ineffective and the servant's will would be more powerful. This is analogous to a king in whose kingdom something he does not want occurs while what he wants does not occur – that would indicate the king's weakness. Since describing God with weakness is impossible, the Mu'tazila view is invalid. Al-Nasafi concludes that this argument refutes the Mu'tazila's proof of mutual exclusion (dalālat al-tamānuʿ) by showing it leads to dualism (thunawiyya), i.e., positing two independent wills, which is shirk.

Key Terms
مشيئة الكافر
تعطل مشيئة الله
عجز الملك
دلالة التمانع
مذهب الثنوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the Mu'tazila position protects God's justice by affirming human free will.
Correction: Nasafi argues that this very position undermines God's sovereignty, implying weakness in God, which is impossible. It also leads to dualism, which is shirk.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi conclude would happen if God wills belief from a disbeliever but the disbeliever wills disbelief and disbelief occurs?
A) It shows God's mercy
B) It proves human free will
C) It implies God's will is thwarted and the servant's will is more effective
D) It is a mystery

Key Takeaway
God's will is absolutely effective; any view that implies otherwise leads to affirming a partner with God in sovereignty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that your will conflicted with what Allah decreed, and in your heart you resented that? How does this passage challenge you to submit to Allah's absolute sovereignty even when you don't understand His decree?
?
Consider a time when you insisted on your own plan and it failed. Did you react as if your will should have prevailed? This lesson says that if your will could override Allah's, He would be weak. Are you willing to relinquish the illusion that your will is independent or more effective than His?
?
The passage warns that denying God's effective will leads to dualism—essentially associating partners with Him. Examine your inner state: Do you ever act as if there is a ‘power’ outside Allah that can truly resist His will? What would it mean to live with the conviction that nothing happens except by His will, even the things you dislike?
