Context of the Ease Verse: Address to Believers
Al-Nasafi refutes the objection from verse 2:185 about ease and hardship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verse 'Allah intends ease for you' does not negate that Allah may will disbelief for some people.

Core Idea
The verse about ease is specific to believers fasting, not a universal rule.

Al-Nasafi encounters another objection from Qadirites or Mu'tazilites who argue that if Allah intends ease, He cannot intend the hardship of disbelief. But the verse is not about all people. Let's see how he refutes it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice the structure: the objection is presented first, followed by the response.
2
Identify the objection
The opponents claim that since disbelief is the hardest hardship, Allah cannot intend it because He says He intends ease.
This is based on a general interpretation of the verse.
3
Note al-Nasafi's response
Al-Nasafi points out that the verse is an address to believers in the context of the command to fast, not a general statement about all people.
The verse's context is key.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why this distinction matters: it shows that the verse cannot be used to argue against Allah's willing disbelief for some.
This maintains the doctrine of divine will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتعلقهم بقوله تعالى: يُرِيدُ اللَّهُ بِكُمُ الْيُسْرَ وَلَا يُرِيدُ بِكُمُ الْعُسْرَ [البقرة: ١٨٥] وقولهم: إن الكفر أعسر العسر يجيب على ذلك أن هذا خطاب للمؤمنين بقوله يا أيها الذين أمنوا كتب عليكم الصيام. وتعلقهم بقوله تعالى: لَوْ شَاءَ اللَّهُ مَا أَشْرَكْنَا [الأنعام: ١٤٨] وذلك على لسان المشركين. يقول النسفي: مراد الكفرة من ذكر المشيئة الأمر، لا حقيقة المشيئة.
And their clinging to His statement: 'Allah intends for you ease and does not intend for you hardship' [al-Baqarah:185] and their saying: 'Indeed disbelief is the hardest of hardship' – he responds to that that this is an address to the believers in His statement: 'O you who have believed, fasting is prescribed upon you.' And their clinging to His statement: 'If Allah had willed, we would not have associated (others with Him)' [al-An'am:148] and that is on the tongues of the polytheists. Al-Nasafi says: The intended meaning of the disbelievers in mentioning the will is command, not the reality of will.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage addresses two more Qur'anic objections. First, the opponents argue that Allah's statement 'He intends ease for you' shows He does not intend hardship, and kufr is the greatest hardship, so He does not intend kufr. Al-Nasafi responds by noting that this verse (2:185) is specifically addressing believers in the context of fasting, not a general statement about all people. Thus it does not negate Allah's willing kufr. Second, the opponents cite the polytheists' statement 'If Allah had willed, we would not have associated others' (6:148). Al-Nasafi explains that this is a statement on the tongues of the polytheists, not a divine assertion. The polytheists meant [by 'will'] 'command' (amr), not the actual will (haqiqat al-mashi'a). That is, they were claiming that Allah commanded them to associate, which is false. So this verse does not prove that Allah's actual will determines human actions in the way the opponents claim.

Key Terms
خطاب المؤمنين
اليسر
العسر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Allah intends ease for you' is a general statement that Allah never intends hardship for anyone.
Correction: The verse is specifically addressed to believers regarding fasting, not a universal decree.

Checkpoint
What is the context of the verse 'Allah intends ease for you' according to al-Nasafi?
A) It is a general statement about all people
B) It addresses believers regarding the command to fast
C) It addresses disbelievers regarding their disbelief
D) It is abrogated

Key Takeaway
The verse about ease is context-specific to believers and cannot be used to argue that Allah does not will disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever taken a verse of the Quran out of its original context to justify a desire or avoid a difficulty? What will you do to ensure you seek the full meaning before applying it to your life?
?
The opponents used a verse about ease to argue against Allah's decree over disbelief. Are there areas in your life where you resist accepting Allah's decree because it seems too hard? Will you submit even when it is difficult?
?
Allah addresses the believers directly in the verse about fasting. Are you truly among those being addressed? What concrete evidence in your actions today shows that you are responding to His call?

The Polytheists' Statement: Will or Command?
Al-Nasafi refutes the objection from verse 6:148 about the polytheists' claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi explains the polytheists' statement 'If Allah had willed, we would not have associated others' as referring to command, not actual will.

Core Idea
The polytheists meant 'command' by 'will', not Allah's actual decree.

The second objection comes from the polytheists' own claim in the Quran that Allah's will would have prevented their shirk. Al-Nasafi clarifies that this is not a divine statement but a human misinterpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the polytheists
Read the Arabic and translation for the second objection. Note that this is a quote from the polytheists.
2
Understand the objection
The opponents argue that if Allah had willed, polytheists would not have committed shirk, so Allah must have willed it.
This would make Allah responsible for shirk.
3
Note al-Nasafi's explanation
Al-Nasafi says this is on the tongues of the polytheists, and they meant 'command' not 'will'.
The polytheists claimed Allah commanded them to associate partners.
4
Consider the difference
Reflect on the distinction between Allah's will (what He decrees) and His command (what He orders). They are not the same.
This preserves Allah's transcendence.

Defining Qada' and Qadar according to al-Nasafi
Al-Nasafi defines qada' as act with precision and qadar in two aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's definition of qada' as the act of creation with precision (al-fi'l bi al-ihkam) and the two aspects of qadar: the eternal measure (al-hadd) and the specific decree (al-bayan).

Core Idea
Qada' is Allah's precise act of creation; qadar encompasses an eternal limit and a detailed decree beyond human knowledge.

Al-Nasafi begins this passage by reaffirming that Allah creates all actions, which logically leads to His decree and measurement of them. He then surveys definitions of qada' from other scholars and selects 'act with precision' over mere command or completion. Finally, he divides qadar into two aspects: one eternal and prescriptive (the limit that defines good and evil), and another that specifies time, place, and recompense—knowledge of which is beyond human reach. This lesson unpacks these essential terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening premise
Read the first sentence: 'If it is established that Allah creates actions, then it is established that He decreed their coming into being and measured them.' This sets the foundation that qada' and qadar are directly tied to creation.
Note the conditional structure: from creation, qada and qadar follow.
2
Examine the definitions of qada'
The passage lists three definitions (command, completion, act) and shows that al-Nasafi chooses 'act with precision' (al-fi'l bi al-ihkam). Identify why he rejects the other two and what 'with precision' adds.
The term 'al-ihkam' implies perfect execution and firmness.
3
Analyze the two aspects of qadar
The passage presents qadar in two senses: first, the eternal limit that determines good/bad nature of things; second, the specification of time, place, and recompense. Notice that the first is eternal and applies to all actions, while the second is beyond human knowledge.
Verse 54:49 is cited for the first aspect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت أن الله تعالى هو الذي خلق الأفعال، ثبت أنه تعالى قضى تكونها وقدرها على ما هي عليه من حسن وقبح. ويعرف النسفي القضاء كما عرفه غيره بمجموعة من التعاريف، منها: الأمر، ومنها الفراغ، ومنها: الفعل، وهو ما اختاره النسفي، إذا فالقضاء هو الفعل مع الإحكام، وعلى هذا فكل شيء بقضاء الله أي بفعله وخلقه وتكوينه. ثم يعرف القدر بوجهين: الأول: الحد الذي يخرج عليه الشيء من حسن أو قبح من خير أو شر، وهذا يكون أزلا، يقول تعالى: إِنَّا كُلَّ شَيْءٍ خَلَقْتَهُ بِقَدَرٍ [القمر: ٤٩]. والثاني: بيان ما يقع عليه كل شيء من زمان ومكان وما له من الثواب والعقاب - والأول قائم في أفعال الخلق والثاني لا يبلغه علمهم.
And if it is established that Allah Most High is He who created the actions, it is established that He Most High decreed (qada) their coming into being and measured them (qaddara) as they are of good and evil. And al-Nasafi defines qada' as others have defined it, with a set of definitions, among them: al-amr (command), al-faragh (completion), and al-fi'l (act), which is what al-Nasafi chose. So qada' is the act (al-fi'l) with precision (al-ihkam). Upon this, everything is by the qada' of Allah, i.e., by His act, His creation, and His bringing into existence. Then he defines qadar in two aspects: First: the limit (al-hadd) upon which a thing emerges of good or evil, of good or bad, and this is eternal. He says Exalted: 'Indeed, all things We created with a measure (bi-qadar)' [al-Qamar:49]. Second: the specification (bayan) of what each thing occurs upon of time and place, and what is for it of reward and punishment. The first is established in the actions of creation, and the second their knowledge does not reach.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage provides definitions of qada' and qadar after establishing that Allah creates actions. First, al-Nasafi defines qada'. He lists three definitions from scholars: (1) al-amr (command), (2) al-faragh (completion), and (3) al-fi'l (act). He chooses al-fi'l with al-ihkam (precision). Thus qada' means Allah's act of creating with precise execution; everything is by Allah's act and creation. Then he defines qadar in two ways. The first qadar is the eternal limit (hadd) that determines the nature of a thing as good or evil, good or bad. This is supported by verse 54:49. The second qadar is the specification of time, place, and recompense for each act. The first qadar is present in the actions of creatures (we can know that things are created with certain limits), but the second qadar (the detailed temporal and spatial specifications and the exact reward/punishment) is beyond human knowledge.

Key Terms
القضاء
القدر
الإحكام
الحد
البيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think qada' means only a command or decree without actual creation.
Correction: Al-Nasafi explicitly chooses the definition of qada' as 'act with precision' (al-fi'l bi al-ihkam), meaning Allah's actual creative act, not just a command.

Checkpoint
Which definition of qada' does al-Nasafi prefer?
A) al-amr (command)
B) al-faragh (completion)
C) al-fi'l (act) with precision
D) al-qiyas (measurement)

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi defines qada' as Allah's act of creation with precision (al-fi'l bi al-ihkam), and qadar as comprising an eternal measure of good/evil and a specific decree of circumstances unknowable to humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face unexpected hardship, do you truly believe that it came to you by Allah's precise act (qada')—not as a random accident? What would change in your heart if you internalized this fully?
?
The second aspect of qadar—the detailed specification of your life's events—is beyond your knowledge. Does that make you anxious or trusting? How can you actively surrender that anxiety to Allah today?
?
If every action of yours is created by Allah with precision, then even your mistakes and sins are under His decree. How does this reality humble you, and what will you do differently when you next fall into sin?

Allah's Decree of Kufr: Qada' vs Maqdi
Al-Nasafi distinguishes qada' from maqdi to refute Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's key distinction between Allah's decree (qada') itself and the thing decreed (maqdi), and why divine decree of falsehood does not taint Allah's truth.

Core Idea
Allah's decree is always true; the decreed thing can be false.

The Mu'tazilites argued that because Allah's decree is truth, He cannot decree falsehood like kufr. Al-Nasafi responds by distinguishing the act of decreeing from the content decreed. Let's read his precise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite claim
Begin by reading the Mu'tazilite position as presented by al-Nasafi: that Allah does not decree disbelief because His decree is truth and right.
Notice the premise: qada' is haqq and sawab.
2
Identify al-Nasafi's distinction
Find where al-Nasafi separates qada' (decree) from maqdi (decreed). He affirms that qada' is truth, but the maqdi (kufr) is false. Yet decreeing falsehood is wise.
Key terms: qada', maqdi, haqq, batil, hikma.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that Allah's decree of kufr is not wrong because the act of decreeing is separate from the thing decreed. The wisdom behind decreeing kufr makes it right.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقول: إن المعتزلة يقولون: إن الله تعالى لا يقضي الكفر، لأن قضاءه حق وصواب. ويرد عليهم بقوله: إن الكفر مقضي الله وليس بقضائه وقضاؤه حق أما مقضيه وهو الكفر فباطل، وقضاء هذا المقضي صواب لما فيه من الحكمة. ويبين احتجاج المعتزلة ويناقشهم. ثم يقول: لا عذر لأحد في التخليق والإرادة والقضاء والقدر، لأن هذه المعاني لم تجعلهم مضطرين إلى ما فعلوا، بل فعلوا ما فعلوا مختارين، فصار خلق الأفعال وإرادتها والقضاء بها وتقديرها كخلق الأوقات والأمكنة التي تقع فيها الأفعال ولا تقع بدونها، ولم يصر تخليق شيء من ذلك عذرا، لأنه لا يوجب اضطرارهم ولأنه لم يخطر شيء من ذلك ببالهم وقت الفعل.
Then he says: Indeed the Mu'tazila say that Allah Most High does not decree (yaqdi) disbelief, because His decree (qada') is truth (haqq) and right (sawab). And he refutes them by his saying: Indeed disbelief is decreed (maqdi) by Allah, but it is not His decree (qada'). His decree is truth, but as for the decreed (maqdiyyuhu), which is disbelief, it is falsehood (batil). And the decree of this decreed is right due to the wisdom (hikma) in it. And he explains the argument of the Mu'tazila and discusses with them. Then he says: There is no excuse for anyone in [the matter of] creation (takhliq), will (irada), decree (qada'), and predestination (qadar), because these meanings did not make them compelled (mudtarrin) to what they did. Rather, they did what they did as free choosers (mukhtarin). So the creation of actions, willing them, decreeing them, and determining them became like the creation of times and places in which actions occur and without which they do not occur, and the creation of none of that became an excuse, because it does not necessitate their compulsion, and because none of that occurred to their minds at the time of the action.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage contains the Mu'tazilite claim and al-Nasafi's response. The Mu'tazila argue that Allah cannot decree kufr because His decree is true and right, and kufr is falsehood. Al-Nasafi refutes by distinguishing between the decree (qada') and the decreed (maqdi). Allah's decree is indeed truth, but the decreed thing (kufr) is falsehood; however, decreeing that falsehood is itself wise (sawab) because of the wisdom behind it. Thus Allah does decree kufr in the sense of creating it, but His act of decreeing is not tainted by the falsehood of the thing decreed. Then al-Nasafi addresses the excuse objection: some may argue that since Allah creates actions, wills them, decrees, and predestines them, people have an excuse for their sins. He denies this: these divine attributes do not compel humans. Humans act with free choice (mukhtarin). The creation of actions, will, decree, and predestination are like the creation of time and place – they are necessary preconditions for actions, but they do not force a particular action. People do not think of these divine acts at the moment of their choice, so they are not an excuse. Thus divine predestination does not contradict human responsibility.

Key Terms
قضاء
مقضي
حق
صواب
باطل
حكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If Allah decrees kufr, then His decree must be false or unjust.
Correction: Allah's decree is always true and wise; the falsehood is in the decreed thing (kufr), not in the decree itself.

Checkpoint
How does al-Nasafi distinguish qada' from maqdi?
A) Qada' is eternal, maqdi is temporal
B) Qada' is truth, maqdi can be falsehood
C) Qada' is for believers, maqdi for disbelievers
D) Qada' is from Allah, maqdi from humans

Key Takeaway
Allah's decree is always true; falsehood can be in the decreed object without affecting the decree's truth and wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever blamed Allah for your own sins, thinking that since He decreed them, you are not responsible? How does the distinction between qada' and maqdi change your understanding of your accountability before Him? Will you start taking full ownership of your actions, even as you trust His decree?
?
If Allah decrees kufr in some people, does that give you an excuse to be complacent about your own faith? How does the wisdom (ḥikma) behind His decree call you to increase your gratitude and obedience, knowing that you have been guided? What specific step will you take today to safeguard your faith?
?
When you experience pain or evil in your life, do you question Allah's justice? This lesson teaches that the decree itself is always true and wise, even if the decreed thing is hard. Can you submit to His decree without fully understanding it? What hidden pride prevents you from trusting His wisdom?

No Excuse in Divine Predestination
Al-Nasafi denies that divine predestination excuses human sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine creation, will, decree, and predestination do not excuse human sin, according to al-Nasafi.

Core Idea
Divine predestination does not compel; humans act by free choice.

Some might argue that since Allah creates, wills, and decrees all actions, humans have an excuse. Al-Nasafi refutes this by showing that these divine acts do not force anyone, just as time and place do not force actions. Let's explore his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the no-excuse argument
Read al-Nasafi's statement that there is no excuse in takhliq, irada, qada, and qadar. Note his denial that these make people compelled.
He says 'la 'udhra' (no excuse).
2
Understand the comparison
Al-Nasafi compares these divine attributes to the creation of times and places: they are necessary conditions but not coercive causes.
Think about how time and place enable actions but do not determine them.
3
Reflect on why it's not an excuse
Two reasons: these attributes do not necessitate compulsion (idtirar), and people do not even think of them at the moment of choice.
Human responsibility remains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Guidance and Misguidance Come from Allah
Al-Nasafi asserts that both guidance and misguidance are from Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Nasafi grounds the doctrine of guidance and misguidance in the principle that Allah creates all actions, directly opposing the Mu'tazilite view.

Core Idea
Both guidance and misguidance are created by Allah alone.

This lesson examines a concise but powerful statement by al-Nasafi. He ties the issue of guidance and misguidance directly to the foundational principle of the creation of human actions. By understanding this connection, you will see how Maturidi theology maintains divine omnipotence while addressing human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation to grasp the basic claim.
Notice the brief structure: a statement followed by a reason.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage states that guidance (al-ihitda') and misguidance (al-idlal) are from Allah. This is the main doctrinal point.
The terms al-huda and al-idlal are used for guidance and misguidance as actions of Allah.
3
Understand the basis
Al-Nasafi says he builds this on the creation of actions of servants. This means the previous discussion on khalq af`al al-`ibad is the foundation.
Recall that Allah creates all actions, including human acts.
4
Contrast with the Mu'tazilite view
The passage ends by saying this is contrary to the Mu'tazila. The Mu'tazila believed humans create their own guidance and misguidance.
This contrast highlights the Maturidi emphasis on divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في الهدى والإضلال ويبني كذلك على خلق أفعال العباد مسألة الهدي والإضلال، لأن كل شيء مخلوق الله تعالى فالاهتداء من الله والإضلال من الله، وهذا بخلاف قول المعتزلة.
Discussion on guidance (al-huda) and misguidance (al-idlal). And he also builds upon the creation of the actions of servants the issue of guidance and misguidance, because everything is created by Allah Most High, so guidance (al-ihitda') is from Allah and misguidance (al-idlal) is from Allah, and this is contrary to the saying of the Mu'tazila.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage introduces the topic of al-huda wa al-idlal (guidance and misguidance). Al-Nasafi states that this issue is built upon the earlier established principle of the creation of human actions (khalq af`al al-`ibad). Since everything is created by Allah, both the state of being guided (al-ihitda') and the state of being misguided (al-idlal) are from Allah. This directly opposes the Mu'tazilite view that guidance and misguidance are solely from the individual, not from Allah. The passage is brief but makes a clear doctrinal statement: Allah is the creator of all things, including the guidance and misguidance of people, while still maintaining human responsibility as discussed earlier.

Key Terms
الهدى
الإضلال
الاهتداء
خلق أفعال العباد
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
On what principle does al-Nasafi build the discussion of guidance and misguidance?
A) The principle of human free will
B) The principle that Allah creates all actions
C) The principle of divine justice
D) The principle of rational obligation

Key Takeaway
Guidance and misguidance are both from Allah because all actions are created by Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your current state of faith is a gift from Allah alone, and how does that realization affect your humility and gratitude today?
?
When you see others who are misguided, do you feel a sense of superiority or do you recognize that their misguidance is also by Allah's decree, and how does that change your attitude towards them?
?
If guidance and misguidance are both from Allah, what does that mean for your daily struggle to remain firm on the truth, and what concrete action will you take today to seek guidance from Him alone?

Qada: Act with Perfection
Nasafi enumerates three definitions of qada and states his preferred one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three definitions of qada in kalam and why Nasafi prefers the meaning of 'act with perfection.'

Core Idea
Qada is God's act of creating with perfection, not just a decree.

Before explaining qada, Nasafi establishes that God creates all actions. He then presents three possible meanings of qada found among scholars, and chooses one. This lesson unpacks that choice, showing that qada refers to God's direct creative act with perfection (ihkam), not merely an abstract command or completion.

Guided Path
1
Read the premise: God creates actions
Start by reading the first sentence which establishes that God is the creator of all actions. This premise grounds the definition.
2
Identify the three definitions of qada
Listen for 'command', 'completion', and 'the act with perfection'. Notice that Nasafi presents them as options from other scholars.
The Arabic words: الأمر, الفراغ, الفعل
3
Note Nasafi's preferred definition
Nasafi chooses 'the act with perfection'. Understand that this emphasizes both God's action and its flawless execution.
4
Reflect on the implication
Everything is by God's qada, meaning by His act, creation, and formation. This ties qada directly to divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت أن الله تعالى هو الذي خلق الأفعال، ثبت أنه تعالى قضى تكونها وقدرها على ما هي عليه من حسن وقبح. ويعرف النسفي القضاء كما عرفه غيره بمجموعة من التعاريف، منها: الأمر، ومنها الفراغ، ومنها : الفعل، وهو ما اختاره النسفي، إذا فالقضاء هو الفعل مع الإحكام، وعلى هذا فكل شيء بقضاء الله أي بفعله وخلقه وتكوينه. ثم يعرف القدر بوجهين: الأول: الحد الذي يخرج عليه الشيء من حسن أو قبح من خير أو شر ، وهذا يكون أزلا ، يقول تعالى: إِنَّا كُلَّ شَيْءٍ خَلَقْتَهُ بِقَدَرٍ ﴾ [القمر: ٤٩]. والثاني: بيان ما يقع عليه كل شيء من زمان ومكان وما له من الثواب والعقاب - والأول قائم في أفعال الخلق والثاني لا يبلغه علمهم.
And when it is established that God Almighty is the one who created actions, it is established that He decreed their occurrence and determined them as they are of good and evil. And Nasafi defines qada as others have defined it with a group of definitions, among them: command, and among them: completion, and among them: the act, which is what Nasafi chose, so qada is the act with perfection, and on this everything is by God's qada, i.e., by His act, creation, and formation. Then he defines qadar in two ways: first: the limit upon which a thing emerges of good or evil, of good or bad, and this is eternal, He says تعالى: 'Indeed, We have created everything by a measure' [al-Qamar 49]. Second: the specification of what every thing occurs upon of time and place and what it has of reward and punishment – and the first is established in the actions of creatures and the second their knowledge does not reach.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage defines qada and qadar. It begins by establishing the premise that God creates all actions, then states that He decrees (qada) and determines (qadar) them. Nasafi then gives three meanings of qada found among scholars: command, completion, and the act. He chooses the meaning of 'act' but with the nuance of perfection (ihkam). Thus, everything is by God's qada meaning by His act, creation, and formation. Next, he defines qadar under two aspects: first, the eternal limit that determines whether something is good or evil, supported by Quran 54:49; second, the specification of the spatiotemporal context and subsequent reward or punishment. The first aspect is knowable to creatures in their actions, but the second is beyond their knowledge. This careful distinction preserves both divine omnipotence and human accountability.

Key Terms
قضاء
أمر
فراغ
فعل
إحكام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think qada is merely a command or a general decree without creative action.
Correction: Nasafi shows qada is specifically the act with perfection, i.e., God's direct creative action, not just a command.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, which definition of qada does he prefer?
A) Command (amr)
B) Completion (faragh)
C) The act (fi'l) with perfection (ihkam)
D) Predestination of evil only

Key Takeaway
Qada is God's creative act with perfection, encompassing all things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When a difficulty strikes, do you view it as a blind fate or as God's perfect act of creation tailored for you? What would change if you truly saw it as His 'act with perfection'? Commit to one moment today where you will replace despair with trust by recalling that qada is a creative, perfect act from God.
?
If everything in your life—successes, failures, and mundane moments—is 'by God's qada', meaning His act and creation, then how does that challenge your sense of control? What is one area you need to surrender to God's active decree, asking Him to perfect you through it?
?
You often attribute your achievements to your own effort and your failures to fate. But Nasafi teaches that qada is God's active creation with perfection. How does this reframe the way you view your own role? Will you begin to see both good and bad as part of God's perfect decree and respond with gratitude and patience starting today?

Qadar: Two Aspects
Nasafi defines qadar in two aspects: eternal limit and temporal specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two aspects of qadar: the eternal limit and the temporal specification, and their implications for human knowledge.

Core Idea
Qadar has two facets: the eternal measure of good and evil, and the temporal details beyond human knowledge.

After defining qada, Nasafi turns to qadar, explaining it in two ways. This distinction is crucial for balancing divine decree and human responsibility. The first aspect is the eternal limit that determines whether something is good or evil, while the second specifies the spatiotemporal context and consequences. The first is knowable in human actions, but the second is hidden from us.

Guided Path
1
Read the two definitions of qadar
Start by reading the sentence that introduces the two aspects. Notice the first is about the limit (had) upon which a thing emerges as good or evil.
2
Identify the Quranic evidence
The first aspect is supported by Quran 54:49. Read the verse and see how it ties to the concept of measure.
3
Notice the epistemic boundary
The second aspect includes time, place, reward, and punishment. Nasafi says this is beyond human knowledge. Ask yourself: why would this be hidden?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage defines qada and qadar. It begins by establishing the premise that God creates all actions, then states that He decrees (qada) and determines (qadar) them. Misconception: Some think qadar is only about the predetermined fate of events (like time and place) and that humans cannot know anything about it.
Correction: Nasafi distinguishes two aspects: the eternal measure of good/evil is knowable to humans in their actions, while the temporal details are hidden. Humans are accountable for what they can know.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, which aspect of qadar can human knowledge reach?
A) The temporal specification of time and place
B) The eternal limit of good and evil in actions

Qadar comprises an eternal measure known to God and a temporal specification hidden from humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial, do you seek to understand the ‘limit’ (had) of good and evil in that situation as revealed by Allah, or do you dwell anxiously on the hidden details of time and place which are beyond your knowledge? How will you shift your focus to what you can know?
?
If we accept that the second aspect of qadar—the exact when, where, and reward—is beyond human knowledge, how does that change your reaction to unexpected outcomes in your life? Will you still trust Allah’s measure without needing to know the details?
?
The first aspect of qadar is established in the actions of creatures. Are you actively using the revelation to discern the ‘limit’ of good and evil in your daily choices, or do you sometimes neglect that divine measure? What is one change you will make today to align with Allah’s decree?

Mu'tazila Objection on Qada and Kufr Answered
Nasafi presents and refutes the Mu'tazila claim that God does not decree disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's distinction between God's decree (qada') and the thing decreed (maqdi), and how this answers the Mu'tazila objection.

Core Idea
God's decree is always true; the thing decreed may be false, but decreeing it is wise.

The Mu'tazila argued that God cannot decree disbelief because His decree is always true. But what is exactly being decreed? Nasafi draws a crucial distinction between the act of decreeing and the content of what is decreed. This passage shows how a correct understanding of qada' and maqdi preserves both God's justice and His absolute sovereignty.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila objection
Read the first part of the passage where the Mu'tazila claim is stated. Notice their assumption: God's decree must be true and right, therefore cannot be disbelief.
The Mu'tazila believe God cannot do evil.
2
Identify Nasafi's distinction
Find where Nasafi distinguishes between qada' (the decree) and maqdi (the thing decreed). He says disbelief is maqdi, not qada'.
3
Understand the implications
Nasafi says the decree itself is true, but the decreed thing (disbelief) is false. Yet decreeing it is correct because of wisdom. Reflect on how this preserves God's justice.
This is a key Maturidi defense of qadar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقول : إن المعتزلة يقولون : إن الله تعالى لا يقضي الكفر، لأن قضاءه حق وصواب. ويرد عليهم بقوله : إن الكفر مقضي الله وليس بقضائه وقضاؤه حق أما مقضيه وهو الكفر ،فباطل، وقضاء هذا المقضي صواب لما فيه من الحكمة.
Then he says: The Mu'tazila say that God Almighty does not decree disbelief, because His decree is truth and correctness. And he refutes them by saying: Disbelief is decreed by God and is not His decree, and His decree is truth, but the decreed thing, which is disbelief, is false, and decreeing this decreed thing is correct because of the wisdom in it.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage records the Mu'tazila objection and Nasafi's response. The Mu'tazila argue that God cannot decree disbelief because His decree is always true and right, and disbelief is false. Nasafi refutes by distinguishing between the decree (qada') itself and the thing decreed (maqdi). God's decree as an act is always true and right, but the decreed thing—such as disbelief—is itself false and evil. However, the act of decreeing this false thing is correct because it contains wisdom (hikma). This preserves God's justice while affirming that He decrees all things, including evil.

Key Terms
قضاء
مقضي
حق
صواب
باطل
حكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God decrees disbelief, then His decree becomes false or evil.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that the decree itself is always true and wise; only the thing decreed (disbelief) is false. The act of decreeing remains correct because of the wisdom behind it.

Checkpoint
How does Nasafi refute the Mu'tazila claim that God does not decree disbelief?
A) He says disbelief is not decreed
B) He distinguishes between the decree (qada') and the decreed thing (maqdi)
C) He says disbelief is actually good
D) He denies that God creates actions

Key Takeaway
God's decree is always true and wise, but the thing decreed may be false; this distinction answers the Mu'tazila objection while preserving divine sovereignty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness people rejecting faith, do you ever feel a tension between God's sovereignty and their responsibility? How does Nasafi's distinction between God's decree and the decreed thing help you trust God's wisdom rather than question His justice?
?
Is there an area of your life where you have blamed God for something 'evil' or difficult, forgetting that His decree is always wise? Will you now commit to viewing your trials as decreed with hikma, even when you don't understand?
?
If God decrees even disbelief for a wise purpose, how should that change the way you look at those who oppose Islam? Can you see them as part of a larger divine plan, and how does that affect your compassion and du'a for them?

No Excuse in Predestination
Nasafi denies that divine predestination excuses human sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine predestination does not remove human moral responsibility according to Nasafi's argument.

Core Idea
Predestination does not compel; humans act voluntarily and have no excuse.

This lesson examines a common objection to divine predestination: if God creates all actions, how can humans be blamed? Nasafi responds with a clear argument that predestination does not imply compulsion. He uses an analogy to show that just as the creation of time and place does not excuse actions, so the divine decree does not excuse sin. Let's explore his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the flow: claim, reason, analogy, conclusion.
2
Identify the key claim about compulsion vs choice
Nasafi states that divine creation, will, decree, and destiny do not make people compelled (mudtarrin). Instead, they act as choosers (mukhtar). Underline this contrast.
This is the heart of the argument.
3
Understand the analogy of times and places
He compares the creation of actions to the creation of times and places: both are necessary for actions but do not compel them. Ask yourself why he uses this analogy.
Reflect on how the analogy strengthens his case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقول: لا عذر لأحد في التخليق والإرادة والقضاء والقدر، لأن هذه المعاني لم تجعلهم مضطرين إلى ما فعلوا، بل فعلوا ما فعلوا مختارين، فصار خلق الأفعال وإرادتها والقضاء بها وتقديرها كخلق الأوقات والأمكنة التي تقع فيها الأفعال ولا تقع بدونها، ولم يصر تخليق شيء من ذلك عذرا، لأنه لا يوجب اضطرارهم ولأنه لم يخطر شيء من ذلك ببالهم وقت الفعل.
Then he says: There is no excuse for anyone in creation, will, decree, and destiny, because these meanings did not make them compelled to what they did, rather they did what they did as choosers. Thus the creation of actions, willing them, decreeing them, and determining them became like the creation of times and places in which actions occur and without which they do not occur, and the creation of none of that became an excuse, because it does not necessitate their compulsion and because none of that occurred to their minds at the time of the action.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This paragraph addresses a common fatalist objection: that if God creates, wills, decrees, and determines actions, then humans are compelled and have an excuse. Nasafi denies this. He argues that these divine acts do not make humans compelled (mudtarrin); rather, humans act voluntarily (mukhtar). He draws an analogy: God creates the times and places in which actions occur, but that does not excuse anyone because they do not feel compelled and the thought of divine predestination does not cross their minds at the moment of action. Thus, predestination does not negate moral responsibility.

Key Terms

إرادة
قضاء
قدر
اضطرار
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine predestination means humans are compelled and thus not responsible for their sins.
Correction: Nasafi argues that predestination does not compel; humans act voluntarily, so they are morally responsible.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi say divine creation of actions does not provide an excuse for sin?
A) Because humans are not the real agents
B) Because humans act voluntarily and are not compelled
C) Because God only creates good actions
D) Because predestination removes all responsibility

Key Takeaway
Nasafi establishes that predestination does not compel human action; humans remain voluntary agents with moral responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you sin, do you truly believe you could have chosen otherwise? If yes, why do you still sin? If no, then you are claiming compulsion—what does this passage say to you?
?
Think of a time you blamed your circumstances or fate for a moral failure. If you were to stand before God with that excuse, how would Nasafi's words challenge you?

Guidance and Misguidance from God
Nasafi affirms divine guidance and misguidance and refutes Mu'tazila with Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maturidi school affirms that both guidance and misguidance come directly from God, and how the Quranic verse refutes the Mu'tazila interpretation.

Core Idea
True guidance is God's act of opening the heart, not mere explanation.

In this passage, Nasafi builds on the doctrine of the creation of actions to affirm a core Maturidi principle: guidance and misguidance are both from God. He contrasts this with the Mu'tazila view, which reduces divine guidance to instruction and misguidance to providing tools. Then he brings a decisive Quranic proof that exposes the flaw in their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle
Read Nasafi's opening statement that because God creates all things, guidance and misguidance are from Him. This is applied to human actions.
This builds on the earlier discussion of the creation of actions.
2
Analyze the Mu'tazila alternative
Notice how the Mu'tazila reinterpret 'guidance' and 'misguidance' to preserve human free will. They say guidance is just explaining the path, and misguidance is creating tools for error.
3
See the Quranic refutation
Read the verse from al-An'am (6:125). Nasafi argues that if guidance were only explanation, then everyone who is called would have an expanded breast. But the verse mentions constriction, proving that guidance involves a transformative act.
This refutation shows the logical absurdity of the Mu'tazila interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويبني كذلك على خلق أفعال العباد مسألة الهدي والإضلال، لأن كل شيء مخلوق الله تعالى فالاهتداء من الله والإضلال من الله ، وهذا بخلاف قول المعتزلة، لأنهم يقولون : إن العبد يخلق فعل نفسه، وعليه فإن فى رأيهم ما أضيف إلى الله من الهداية أي بيان طريق الدين، ومن الإضلال أي خلق الآلات التي بها يرتكب العبد الضلال. ويعرض النسفي لتفسيرات كثيرة للمعتزلة في هذا الشأن. ثم يدلل على بطلان كلامهم بعدد من الأدلة منها قول تعالى: ﴿ فَمَن يُرِدِ اللَّهُ أَن يَهْدِيَهُ يَشْرَحْ صَدْرَهُ لِلإِسْلَامِ ﴾ [الأنعام: ١٢٥] فلو كان المراد الدعوة وبيان الطريق فينبغي أن يقال كل من دعاه الله للإيمان ويبين له الطريق فهو مشروح الصدر، فيصير قوله تعالى: يَجْعَلْ صَدْرَهُ ضَيِّقًا حَرَجًا ﴾ [الأنعام: ١٢٥] باطلا كذبا. وهو كفر.
And he builds likewise upon the creation of servants' actions the issue of guidance and misguidance, because everything is created by God Almighty, so guidance is from God and misguidance is from God, and this is contrary to the saying of the Mu'tazila, because they say that the servant creates his own action, and accordingly in their view what is attributed to God of guidance is only explaining the path of religion, and of misguidance is creating the tools by which the servant commits misguidance. And Nasafi presents many interpretations of the Mu'tazila in this regard. Then he demonstrates the falsehood of their statement with a number of proofs, among them the saying of تعالى: 'So whoever God intends to guide, He expands his breast for Islam' [al-An'am 125]. So if the intended meaning were the call and the explanation of the path, then it should be said that everyone whom God calls to faith and explains the path to him has an expanded breast, making His saying تعالى: 'He makes his breast tight and constricted' [al-An'am 125] false and a lie, and that is disbelief.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage discusses divine guidance (huda) and misguidance (idlal). Nasafi begins by affirming that since God creates everything, guidance and misguidance are from God directly, not merely as the Mu'tazila claim. The Mu'tazila, who hold that humans create their own actions, interpret 'guidance from God' as merely explaining the path, and 'misguidance' as creating the instruments by which the servant goes astray. Nasafi then refutes this interpretation using Quran 6:125. If guidance were only explanation, then everyone who receives the call would have an expanded breast, but the verse also mentions those whose breasts are constricted, which would contradict the Mu'tazila interpretation, implying that true guidance is a divine act of opening the heart, not mere instruction.

Key Terms

إضلال
بيان
شرح صدر
دعوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Guidance from God is merely explaining the path of religion; misguidance is only providing the tools for error.
Correction: True guidance is a divine act of opening the heart to faith, and misguidance is a divine act of sealing or constricting the heart, as shown by Quran 6:125.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, how does the Mu'tazila interpretation of guidance contradict Quran 6:125?
A) The verse says guidance is only for believers
B) The verse distinguishes between those whose breasts are expanded and those constricted
C) The verse does not mention explanation
D) The verse was abrogated

Key Takeaway
Guidance and misguidance are direct creative acts of God, not mere instruction or provision of tools, as proven by the Quranic distinction between expanded and constricted hearts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Sunni and Mu'tazila Positions on al-Aslah
Nasafi states Sunni and Mu'tazila positions on al-Aslah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core disagreement between Ahl al-Haqq and the Mu'tazila regarding divine grace (lutf) and the obligation of al-Aslah.

Core Idea
God is not obligated to grant grace to disbelievers; His withholding is neither miserly nor unjust.

This lesson opens the discussion on al-Aslah, a key theological debate about what God must do for His creation. Nasafi first presents the Sunni position that God has the power to grant a special grace that would cause all to believe, but He is not compelled to do so. Then he contrasts the Mu'tazila view that if such grace were possible, God must bestow it. This clash defines the larger debate about divine justice and obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening passage
Read the Arabic passage once, noting the three distinct positions mentioned: Ahl al-Haqq, majority Mu'tazila, and Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir.
Notice the contrast in language: 'تفضلا' (favor) vs 'واجب' (obligatory).
2
Identify the Sunni claim about grace
Ahl al-Haqq affirm that God has power over a grace (lutf) that would lead disbelievers to believe voluntarily. Identify how they characterize God's withholding: not miserly nor unjust.
The term 'تفضل' emphasizes that giving grace is a favor, not a duty.
3
Contrast with the Mu'tazila position
The majority Mu'tazila deny such a grace exists in God's power. If it did, they argue, withholding would make God foolish and miserly. They affirm an obligation upon God to do what brings welfare.
Note the logical consequence: for Mu'tazila, God's goodness necessitates action.
4
Observe Bishr's exceptional view
Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir held a position close to Ahl al-Haqq, leading the Mu'tazila to declare him a disbeliever. Reflect on why this deviation was considered so serious.
This shows the high stakes of the debate within the Mu'tazila school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في الأصلح: يقول أهل الحق : إن في مقدور الله تعالى لطفا، لو فعل ذلك بالكفار لآمنوا اختيارا، وإذا لم يعطهم لم يكن بخيلا ولا ظالما، وإعطاء اللطف منه تعالى يكون تفضلا ولا يجب عليه شيء. أما جمهور المعتزلة فإنهم يدعون أنه ليس في مقدور الله تعالى لطف لو فعل بالكفار لأمنوا ، ولو كان ذلك في مقدوره ولم يفعل لكان سفيها بخيلا، وغاية ما يقدر عليه مما به صلاح الخلق واجب عليه. أما بشر بن المعتمر فيقول بما هو قريب من أهل الحق ولهذا كفرته معتزلة ثم يعرف النسفي الأصلح عند معتزلة بغداد بأنه الأصلح في الحكمة والتدبير، وعند بعض البصريين الصلاح هو النفع والأصلح هو الأنفع. ثم يعرض لدليل المعتزلة الذي يؤكد قولهم بوجوب الأصلح على الله تعالى ثم يقدم أدلة أهل الحق من القرآن والوجود وإجماع أهل الأديان، وأخيرا الدليل العقلي. فمن القرآن مثلا قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَوْ شِئْنَا لَآتَيْنَا كُلَّ نَفْسٍ هُدَاهَا .
Discussion on al-Aslah (the most appropriate): The people of truth say: There is within God's power a grace (lutf), if He were to do that to the disbelievers, they would believe voluntarily, and if He does not give it to them, He is not miserly nor unjust, and giving the grace from Him is a favor and nothing is obligatory upon Him. As for the majority of the Mu'tazila, they claim that there is not within God's power a grace which if done to the disbelievers they would believe, and if that were within His power and He did not do it, He would be foolish and miserly, and the utmost of what He is capable of that brings about the welfare of creation is obligatory upon Him. As for Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir, he says what is close to the people of truth, and for this the Mu'tazila declared him a disbeliever. Then Nasafi defines al-aslah according to the Mu'tazila of Baghdad as that which is most appropriate in wisdom and administration, and according to some Basrans, welfare (salah) is benefit and the most appropriate (aslah) is the most beneficial. Then he presents the evidence of the Mu'tazila that affirms their statement of the obligation of al-aslah upon God Almighty, then he presents the proofs of the people of truth from the Quran, existence, the consensus of the people of religions, and finally the rational proof. Among the Quranic proofs is His saying تعالى: 'And if We had willed, We would have given every soul its guidance.'
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage introduces the kalam topic of al-Aslah (the most appropriate). It begins with the position of Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth, i.e., Sunni theologians): God has the power to grant a special grace (lutf) that would cause disbelievers to freely believe, but He is not obligated to do so; if He withholds it, He is neither miserly nor unjust. In contrast, the majority of Mu'tazila deny that such a grace exists in God's power; they claim if it did exist and He withheld it, He would be foolish and miserly, so God must do whatever brings about the best welfare for His creatures. Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir actually sided closer with Ahl al-Haqq, and for this the Mu'tazila declared him a disbeliever. Nasafi then defines al-aslah according to two Mu'tazila groups: the Baghdad school sees it as 'most appropriate in wisdom and administration,' while the Basran school sees it as 'more beneficial.' Finally, the passage notes that Nasafi will present the Mu'tazila evidence and then the Sunni proofs from Quran, empirical observation, consensus of religions, and reason, giving as an example Quran 32:13 (incomplete).

Key Terms
أصلح
لطف
تفضل
وجوب
بخل
ظلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God can make all believe, He must do so; otherwise He is unjust.
Correction: Ahl al-Haqq affirm God is not obligated; His actions are not constrained by human notions of justice.

Checkpoint
What is the position of Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir on the issue of al-Aslah?
A) He agreed with the majority Mu'tazila
B) He said God is obligated to do the most appropriate
C) He held a view close to the people of truth
D) He denied that grace (lutf) is in God's power

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position asserts God's freedom from obligation to grant grace, while the Mu'tazila insist on a moral necessity for God to do the most appropriate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone who rejects faith despite clear signs, do you ever feel that God should have given them more grace? What does this passage teach you about your own implicit assumptions about God's obligations? Will you now submit to His wisdom, even when you don't understand His withholding?
?
Do you live with a sense of entitlement to God's blessings? How does the Sunni position that nothing is obligatory upon God challenge your daily expectations of Him? Take a moment to repent for any resentment you've harbored when He didn't give what you thought you deserved.
?
The Mu'tazila declared Bishr a disbeliever for his view. Are you willing to stand for the truth even if the majority opposes you? Where in your life do you compromise on theological truths for social approval? What is one step you will take this week to stand firm on a difficult truth?

Defining al-Aslah and the Sunni Evidence
Nasafi defines al-Aslah for Mu'tazila schools and outlines Sunni proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila definitions of al-Aslah and the types of evidence Nasafi marshals for the Sunni position.

Core Idea
For Mu'tazila, al-Aslah means either wise administration or maximum benefit; Sunni proofs cover reason, revelation, and consensus.

Having laid out the opposing positions, Nasafi now clarifies what the different Mu'tazila schools mean by al-Aslah. He then signals the comprehensive case he will build for the Sunni view, drawing from Qur'an, observable reality, consensus of religions, and rational argument. This lesson examines these definitions and the structure of the Sunni evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Focus on the definitions and the mention of evidence. The passage gives two Mu'tazila definitions and then lists four sources of proof for Ahl al-Haqq.
The example verse is incomplete but clearly supports the Sunni position.
2
Distinguish the two Mu'tazila definitions
The Baghdad school defines al-Aslah as 'most appropriate in wisdom and administration.' The Basran school defines it as 'most beneficial.' Understand the nuance between these.
The Basran definition is more utilitarian, while the Baghdad one emphasizes divine wisdom.
3
Identify the four Sunni evidence categories
Nasafi lists: Quran, existence (wujud), consensus of religions (ijma' ahl al-adyan), and rational proof (dalil 'aqli). Reflect on why each is important.
The order shows a progression from revelation to reason.
4
Consider the example verse
The verse 'And if We had willed, We would have given every soul its guidance' (32:13) directly refutes the Mu'tazila claim that God must guide all.
This verse is a key proof from the Quran for the Sunni position.

Commentary
This passage introduces the kalam topic of al-Aslah (the most appropriate). It begins with the position of Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth, i.e., Sunni theologians): God has the power to grant a special grace (lutf) that would cause disbelievers to freely believe, but He is not obligated to do so; if He withholds it, He is neither miserly nor unjust. In contrast, the majority of Mu'tazila deny that such a grace exists in God's power; they claim if it did exist and He withheld it, He would be foolish and miserly, so God must do whatever brings about the best welfare for His creatures. Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir actually sided closer with Ahl al-Haqq, and for this the Mu'tazila declared him a disbeliever. Nasafi then defines al-aslah according to two Mu'tazila groups: the Baghdad school sees it as 'most appropriate in wisdom and administration,' while the Basran school sees it as 'more beneficial.' Finally, the passage notes that Nasafi will present the Mu'tazila evidence and then the Sunni proofs from Quran, empirical observation, consensus of religions, and reason, giving as an example Quran 32:13 (incomplete).

Key Terms
صلاح
نفع
حكمة
تدبير
دليل عقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Basran Mu'tazila, what does al-Aslah mean?
A) Most appropriate in wisdom and administration
B) Most beneficial
C) Obligatory grace
D) Divine decree

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila disagree among themselves on the meaning of al-Aslah, while Sunni theologians rely on a multi-source argument to affirm God's freedom from obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself assuming that God 'owes' you something good? How does the Sunni teaching that nothing is obligatory on God challenge that assumption? Will you today actively remind yourself that every blessing is a gift, not a right?
?
The Mu'tazila argued God must do the most beneficial. In your life, when something difficult happens, do you immediately question God's wisdom? How can you train your heart to trust His freedom and wisdom even when you don't understand?
?
Nasafi presents multiple proofs (Quran, existence, consensus, reason). Which of these evidences do you rely on most to affirm God's attributes? This week, commit to reflecting on one of them daily to strengthen your conviction.

Two Options for the Mu'tazila and Their Consequences
Nasafi presents two hypothetical Mu'tazilite responses and shows each leads to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite position by showing that their only two possible responses lead to absurd consequences: either denying human agency or abandoning their own doctrine regarding God's speech.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila cannot escape between denying human agency or denying God's speech.

Nasafi has already argued against the Mu'tazilite claim that the Qur'an is created. Now he anticipates their possible responses and demonstrates that both options are untenable. One leads them to agree with Jahm in pure disbelief, the other forces them to abandon their own doctrine and leaves them unable to define God's speech. This passage uses internal logic to corner the opponent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two responses
Notice the conditional structure: 'If they say...' and 'If they claim...'. These are the two hypothetical responses Nasafi anticipates from the Mu'tazila.
The first response denies that the recitation is the servant's act or speech; the second affirms it is the servant's act but not God's speech.
2
Understand the first consequence
If the Mu'tazila say the recitation is not the servant's act, they close the door to proving any human action and agree with Jahm in pure disbelief. Even their own leader considered Jabrites worse than anthropomorphists.
The passage quotes Abu al-Hudhayl: 'The Jabrites are worse than the Anthropomorphists.'
3
Understand the second consequence
If they claim the recitation is the servant's act and speech but not God's speech, they abandon their own doctrine (which holds the recitation is God's speech). The author notes that Jabr (compulsion) is with them, and they have no way to prove or conceive what God's speech would be.
The phrase 'al-jabr ma'ahum' indicates they become Jabrites themselves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: إن هذه القراءة الحاصلة من العبد بعد أن لم تكن بقرأته واختياره وقصده إلى تحصيله، ليس بفعل العبد وليس بكلام له، فقد سدوا على أنفسهم طرق إثبات الفعل للعباد، ووافقوا جهما في ذلك كفرا محضا زائدا رتبته على رتبة تشبيه الله تعالى بخلقه على ما حكوا عن أبي الهذيل العلاف رئيسهم أنه كان يقول: المجبرة شر من المشبهة. وإن زعموا أنه فعل العبد وكلامه وليس بكلام الله تعالى، فقد تركوا مذهبهم. الجبر مع عدهم وقيل لهم: فكلام الله إذا ما هو؟ ولا سبيل لهم إلى إثباته وتصوره.
If they say: 'This recitation that occurs from the servant after it was not, by his reading and his choice and his intent to obtain it, is not the act of the servant nor his speech,' then they have closed upon themselves the paths of proving the act for the servants, and they have agreed with Jahm in that, pure disbelief, the rank of which exceeds the rank of likening God to His creation, according to what they narrated from Abu al-Hudhayl al-‘Allaf, their leader, that he used to say: 'The Jabrites are worse than the Anthropomorphists.' And if they claim that it is the act of the servant and his speech and not the speech of God Most High, then they have abandoned their doctrine. Jabr is with them. And it was said to them: 'Then what is the speech of God?' And there is no way for them to prove it or conceive it.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage presents two possible responses the Mu'tazila might give to the previous refutation, and shows that both lead to absurdity. First, if they say the recitation is not the servant's act or speech, then they have no way to prove that servants have acts at all, and they agree with Jahm ibn Safwan in pure disbelief, which is even worse than anthropomorphism according to their own leader Abu al-Hudhayl al-‘Allaf, who said the Jabrites (those who deny human agency) are worse than the Anthropomorphists. Second, if they say it is the servant's act and speech but not God's speech, then they abandon their own doctrine (which claims the recitation is God's speech). The author then notes that the Jabr (compulsion) is with them (i.e., they end up being Jabrites themselves). Finally, the author asks them: if the recitation is not God's speech, then what is God's speech? They have no way to prove or conceive it.

Correction: Nasafi shows that any attempt to separate the recitation from being both God's speech and the servant's act leads to absurdity; there is no coherent third option.

Checkpoint

A) They maintain their doctrine consistently.

C) They prove that God's speech is created.
D) They affirm human agency.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi forces the Mu'tazila into a dilemma: either they deny human agency (becoming worse than anthropomorphists) or they deny that the Qur'an is God's speech (abandoning their own position).

?
The Mu'tazila ended up with no way to prove God's speech after their reasoning. In what areas of your life do you rely on your own reasoning to define God rather than accepting what He has revealed? Are you willing to repent of that and let the text shape your understanding?

The Origin of Human Actions: Jahm's View
Jahm's denial of human agency and his metaphor of locus vs. agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational question of human action creation and the extreme determinist view of Jahm ibn Safwan.

Core Idea
Jahm denied any real human action—our deeds are only metaphorically ours.

This passage opens a new section on divine justice by asking a radical question: Who truly originates human actions? Before exploring the nuanced Maturidi position, the author presents the extreme view of Jahm ibn Safwan, who strips humans of all real agency. His position, known as Jabr (compulsion), sets the stage for the critical debate that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation once, noting the key terms. Let the author's own formulation sink in before analysis.
Pay attention to the metaphor of 'locus' vs. 'agent'.
2
Identify the core claim
Find Jahm's exact statement: 'لا فعل للعبد في الحقيقة' (no real action for the servant). Understand that he denies any genuine human contribution to action.
3
Understand the metaphor
Jahm says actions are attributed to humans like motion is attributed to a place (locus), not like to a real agent. For example, a pen moves but the real agent is the writer.
This metaphor is central to his determinism.
4
Connect to the bigger picture
This debate ties to God's attributes (especially power and creation). Jahm's view implies God is the sole creator of all actions, both good and evil.
5
Prepare for the refutation
Recognize that this is just the first position. The author will likely offer arguments against it in subsequent passages. Anticipate what evidence might counter this view.
Keep an eye on how the author uses reason and revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل في هذه المسائل مسألة خلق أفعال العباد وهي مبنية على ما يتعلق بصفات الله تعالى ويدخل تحتها على ما نبين. من موجد فعل العبد؟ واختلف الناس فيها فزعم جهم بن صفوان أن لا فعل للعبد في الحقيقة وما يضاف إليهم يضاف على حسب إضافة الأشياء إلى محالها دون إضافتها إلى محصلها، وساعده على هذه المقالة أصحابه وهم الجبرية.
The foundational principle in these issues is the question of the creation of actions of servants (khalaq af‘al al-‘ibad), and it is based on what relates to the attributes of Allah Almighty, and what falls under it as we will explain. [Question:] Who originated the servant's action? And people disagreed about it. Jahm ibn Safwan claimed that the servant has no real action (la fi‘l lil-‘abd fi al-haqiqa), and what is attributed to them is attributed in the manner of attributing things to their loci (mihal) without attributing them to their agents (muhsil). He was supported in this statement by his followers, and they are the Jabriyya (determinists).
Source pages p. 784

Commentary
This passage is the introduction to a new section on 'issues of justice and injustice' (ta'dil wa tajwir), beginning with the foundational question of the creation of human actions. The author states that this issue is connected to the attributes of God. He then poses the core question: 'Who originates the servant's action?' and immediately presents the first opinion, that of Jahm ibn Safwan. Jahm denies that humans have any real action; their apparent actions are only metaphorically attributed to them, like attributing a motion to its locus (e.g., the wind) rather than to a real agent. His followers are the Jabriyya (extreme determinists). This sets up the debate that will be elaborated with other views in subsequent passages.

Key Terms

الجبرية
لا فعل للعبد في الحقيقة
محصل
محل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Jahm's view is that humans have no choice, but the passage specifies he denies real action altogether, not just choice.
Correction: Jahm denies any real action (fi‘l haqiqi) by the servant; actions are only metaphorically attributed to them. This is more extreme than simple denial of free will.

Checkpoint

A) As real actions created by God through human acquisition.
B) By way of metaphor, like attributing motion to a place (locus) rather than to an agent.
C) As part of a human free will that God does not control.
D) Only good actions are attributed to humans, evil actions to God.

Key Takeaway
Jahm's extreme determinism denies any real human action, attributing all deeds to God alone through a metaphor of locus rather than agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your most sinful deed were only metaphorically yours—like a shadow belongs to a body—would you feel any less accountable to Allah? Have you ever secretly wished your actions were not really yours to avoid responsibility?
?
Jahm’s view made some people feel safe from blame. Does your heart ever lean toward blaming God for your own wrong choices? What would it take for you to fully own your sins before Him today?
?
If you truly believed that every action you do is placed in you without your agency, how would your daily prayers, your striving against sin, and your hope for reward change? Will you test your own heart: do you live as an agent or as a mere locus?

Quranic Proof for Two Types of Istita'a
Quranic evidence for two types of istita'a.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran distinguishes between two types of istita'a (capacity) using two key verses.

Core Idea
The Quran itself proves that istita'a can mean either capacity to act or availability of means.

The author now turns to the Quran to demonstrate that istita'a is not a single concept but is divided into two types. By analyzing two verses, he shows that the same word can refer to either the soundness of causes or the power to perform an act. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and helps you see the scriptural basis for this theological distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic proof passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to grasp the author's overall argument. Note that he cites two verses and explains why each refers to the first type of istita'a.
The passage is dense but logical; take your time.
2
Analyze the first verse (Q. 58:4)
Focus on how the author argues that 'istita'a' in this verse must mean soundness of causes (health, instruments) because the actual power to fast cannot exist two months in advance.
This is a key move: the impossibility of pre-existing qudra proves the meaning.
3
Examine the second verse (Q. 9:42) and the hypocrites
Understand why Allah declared the hypocrites liars. The author explains that if they meant the true capacity to perform jihad, they would not be liars, because that capacity is only present at the time of action. Their lie was in claiming lack of means when they had them.
This shows the second type (qudrat al-ada') is different from the first.
4
Synthesize the division
Combine the two proofs to see that the Quran uses 'istita'a' in two distinct senses: the first is about having the means (health, wealth), the second is the actual ability to perform an act simultaneously with it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الدليل على وجود الاستطاعة وانقسامها إلى قسمين قوله تعالى: ﴿ فَمَن لَّمْ يَسْتَطِعْ فَإِطْعَامُ سِتِّينَ مِسْكِينًا ﴾ (٢) والمراد منه استطاعة الأسباب والآلات إذ لا يتصور وجود قدرة أداء صوم شهرين (٣) من قبل الشروع في أدائه، ويستحيل بقاء القدرة التي(٤) كانت موجودة عند الخصوم إلى شهرين، فدل أنه (٥) أراد به استطاعة سلامة الأسباب وصحة الآلات. والدليل عليه ما عير الله تعالى به من قال من أهل النفاق : لَوِ اسْتَطَعْنَا لَخَرَجْنَا مَعَكُمْ (٦) وكذبهم الله تعالى في ذلك القول، ولو كانوا أرادوا بذلك الكلام الاستطاعة التي هي حقيقة قدرة الفعل ما كانوا بنفيها عن أنفسهم كاذبين، إذ لا شك أن استطاعة فعل الجهاد لا تبقى من وقت كونهم بالمدينة إلى أن يلقوا العدو ويباشروا القتال، وكان الخروج مطلوبًا لذلك، وحيث كذبهم دل أنهم أرادوا بذلك المرض أو فقد المال على ما بين الله تعالى بقوله : لَيْسَ عَلَى الضُّعَفَاءِ وَلَا عَلَى الْمَرْضَى ) إلى أن قال: إِنَّمَا السَّبِيلُ عَلَى الَّذِينَ يَسْتَأْذِنُونَكَ وَهُمْ أَغْنِيَاءُ (٨) )
Then the proof for the existence of istita'a and its division into two types is His statement the Exalted: 'And whoever does not have the capacity (lam yastaṭi‘), then a feeding of sixty needy persons' (Q. 58:4). What is meant by it is istita'a of causes and instruments, since it is inconceivable that the capacity (qudra) to perform the fasting of two months exists before commencing its performance, and it is impossible for the capacity that existed according to the opponents to last for two months. So it indicates that He meant by it istita'a of soundness of causes and integrity of instruments. And the proof for it is what Allah the Exalted reproached those who said from the hypocrites: 'If we had the capacity (istaṭa‘nā), we would have gone out with you' (Q. 9:42), and Allah the Exalted declared them liars in that statement. Had they meant by that speech the istita'a which is the true capacity to perform the act, they would not have been liars by denying it of themselves, for there is no doubt that the istita'a of performing jihad does not remain from the time they were in Medina until they meet the enemy and engage in battle, and going out was required for that. And since He declared them liars, it indicates that they meant by that illness or lack of wealth, as Allah the Exalted clarified in His statement: 'There is not upon the weak nor upon the ill...' until He said: 'The way (of blame) is only against those who ask permission of you while they are rich' (Q. 9:91-93).
Source pages p. 787

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (dalīl) for the existence of istita'a and its division into two types, using two Quranic verses. The first verse is from Surah al-Mujadila (58:4), where a person incapable of expiating by fasting is instructed to feed sixty poor. The author argues that the 'capacity' (istita'a) here cannot be the actual qudra to perform the fast, because that capacity could not logically exist two months before the act. Therefore, it must refer to the first type: soundness of causes and instruments (e.g., health enabling fasting). The second proof uses the hypocrites' excuse in Surah al-Tawba (9:42): 'If we had the capacity, we would have gone out with you.' Allah declared them liars. If they meant the true capacity to perform jihad (the second type), they would not be liars, because such capacity is only present at the time of action, not beforehand. Since they were at home, they genuinely lacked that simultaneous capacity. Their lie was in claiming inability due to lack of means (first type) when they were actually healthy and wealthy. Thus, the verse shows that 'istita'a' in that context refers to the first type (soundness of means), confirming the division.

Key Terms
دليل
استطاعة الأسباب
قدرة الأداء
ضرب المثل
كذب المنافقين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'istita'a' in the Quran always refers to the capacity to perform an act at the time of the act.
Correction: The verses show that 'istita'a' can also refer to the availability of means (health, wealth), which is a different type.

Checkpoint
Why did Allah curse the hypocrites as liars when they said 'law istaṭa‘nā la-kharajnā ma'akum'?
A) They actually had no ability to go out.
B) They meant the true capacity to perform jihad, which they lacked.
C) They had the means and health but claimed they did not.
D) They were lying about wanting to go out.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic evidence confirms that istita'a is of two kinds: the capacity to perform an act and the soundness of means.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If Allah called the hypocrites liars for claiming they lacked capacity while having means, what should I check in my own life about the excuses I make to avoid doing good?
