Refuting the Impossibility of Action from the Incapable
Imam al-Nasafi refutes an objection about action being impossible from the incapable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that action cannot occur from an incapable person, and why power must be simultaneous with the action.

Core Idea
Action is impossible from the incapable not due to incapacity, but due to absence of power.

This passage presents a key dialectical exchange in the debate on divine power and human action. The opponents argue that an incapable person cannot act because incapacity negates power. Imam al-Nasafi turns their own premises against them, showing that their denial of the survival of power leads to a contradiction. By examining this argument, we see the Maturidi insistence that power must be concurrent with the act.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's objection
Read the first sentence: the opponents claim action is impossible from the incapable because power is absent, as incapacity is the opposite of power.
This is the starting point of the argument.
2
Identify the opponent's own doctrine
The author points out that according to the opponents, power at the time of action is non-existent. Notice how he uses their own view against them.
The opponents deny the survival (baqa') of power.
3
Follow the refutation via the intermediate state
The author concedes an intermediate state but argues that power is still absent, so negating incapacity does not help. Action remains impossible due to lack of power.
This undercuts their attempt to avoid the conclusion.
4
Consider the reductio ad absurdum
The author proposes a hypothetical: if their logic held, action could occur even long after power is absent. This absurdity confirms his own position.
This cements the need for simultaneous power.
5
Conclude the necessity of concurrent power
Reflect on how the argument establishes that power must exist exactly at the time of the act, not before.
This is the core Maturidi doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قالوا إنما يستحيل ثبوت الفعل من العاجز لانعدام القدرة إذ هو يضادها فينعدم عند ثبوت ضدها. قلنا: والقدرة عندكم عند الفعل منعدمة، فهب أن إنسانا ساعدكم على قولكم الفاسد أن الفاعل في حال انعدام القدرة عنه ليس بعاجز، لثبوت الواسطة إلا أن القدرة منعدمة واستحال ثبوت الفعل من العاجز لعدم القدرة إلا لثبوت العجز، فينبغي أن لا يتصور منه الفعل حال عدمها، وإذا ثبت الفعل دل أنه ثبت لثبوت القدرة وهي لا تبقى فكانت موجودة وقت وجود الفعل لا سابقًا عليه، وإذا كان الأمر على ما بينا لم يكن لكم في نفي العجز عنه وقت وجود الفعل منفعة لما مر أن استحالة ثبوته من العاجز ليس لثبوت العجز بل لفقد القدرة، وقد تحقق الفقد على أن الأمر لو كان على ما زعمتم لكان ينبغي أن يجوز ثبوت الفعل في العاشر من وقت عدم القدرة ويكون في تلك الحالة غير عاجز، وإن كان عديم القدرة، واستحالة ذلك تقرر ما قلنا والله الموفق.
And if they say: 'Action is impossible from the incapable due to the absence of power, since it opposes it and thus ceases at the existence of its opposite.' We say: 'And power according to you at the time of action is non-existent. Suppose someone agrees with you on your corrupt statement that the agent in the state of absence of power is not incapable, due to the existence of an intermediate, but power is non-existent and action is impossible from the incapable due to lack of power, except for the existence of incapacity. So action should not be conceivable from him in the state of its absence. And if action is established, it indicates that it is established due to the existence of power, and it does not endure, so it existed at the time of the existence of the action, not prior to it. And if the matter is as we have explained, you have no benefit in negating incapacity from him at the time of the existence of the action, because it has passed that its impossibility from the incapable is not due to the existence of incapacity but due to the absence of power, and the absence has been realized. Moreover, if the matter were as you claim, then it should be permissible for action to occur in the tenth [time] from the time of absence of power, and he would be in that state not incapable, even though he lacks power, and the impossibility of that confirms what we said. And God is the grantor of success.'
Source pages p. 824

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from opponents (likely Mu'tazila) who argue that an action cannot occur from an incapable person because incapacity contradicts power, and power ceases when its opposite (incapacity) exists. The author responds by pointing out that according to the opponents' own doctrine, power is non-existent at the time of action (since they deny survival of power). If power is absent, then the agent is indeed incapable, but the opponents try to avoid this by positing an intermediate state. The author argues that even if there is an intermediate, power is still absent, and action from the incapable is impossible only because of lack of power, not because of the presence of incapacity. Therefore, negating incapacity does not help them. Furthermore, if their claim were true, action could occur long after power is absent, which is absurd. Thus the argument confirms the author's position that power must be concurrent with the action.

Key Terms
قدرة
عجز
فعل
بقاء
مقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power can precede the action and still be causally effective.
Correction: The argument shows that if power were not simultaneous, action could occur at any later time, which is impossible. Therefore, power must exist at the exact moment of the act.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the real reason action is impossible from the incapable?
A) Because of the existence of incapacity (عجز)
B) Because of the absence of power (قدرة)
C) Because power contradicts incapacity
D) Because the agent is not capable

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi position that power must be simultaneous with the act is defended by showing that denying simultaneity leads to logical absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fail to perform a good deed, do you attribute it to your own incapacity (a lack in yourself) or do you recognize that it was simply a moment when Allah did not grant you the power to act? How does this distinction change your next step when you attempt the deed again? Will you now beg Allah for power at that very moment rather than relying on a stored ability?
?
The author argues that power does not endure; it is only present at the moment of action. Think of an act of worship you performed yesterday. Was your power to pray or give charity something you stored from a previous day? Or do you admit that without a fresh gift of power from Allah at that specific time, you could not have done it? Confess this truth in your heart right now: that you have no permanent power of your own.
?
The Mu'tazila argued that if power were absent, action would be impossible only if incapacity were present. They missed the real reason: mere absence of power is enough. How often do you excuse yourself from an action by saying 'I can't' (implying a fixed incapacity) when in reality you simply have not been given the power at this moment? Will you change your language and your reliance, turning to the One who gives power instant by instant?

The First Horn: Surviving Power as a Power for Another Action
Al-Nasafi presents the first horn of the dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first horn of the dilemma refutes the Mu'tazila claim that power can survive by showing that if it is a power for another action, then the present action is not caused by its own power, which is absurd.

Core Idea
A surviving power that is not for the action is no power at all.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi continues his refutation of the Mu'tazila position on divine power. He first dismisses their claim that accidents can survive, then raises a dilemma: if power survives, is it a power for this action or another? The first possibility—that it is a power for another action—leads to a clear absurdity, as we will see.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial claim and refutation
Examine how the Mu'tazila claim power exists before the action and survives to its time, and note the author's immediate rejoinder that the survival of accidents has already been proven false.
This sets the stage for the hypothetical argument.
2
Understand the dilemma setup
Notice the author poses a question: if power survives, is it a power for this action simultaneously, or for another action later? This is the fork in the road.
3
Analyze the first horn
Focus on the Mu'tazila response that it is a power for another action. The author shows that then this action is not performed by its own power, which is self-defeating.
The phrase 'كل فعل ثابتًا لا بما له من القدرة' is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحمل هذا الكلام بعض المعتزلة على أن ركبوا المحال، وجوزوا بقاء القدرة وقالوا: القدرة توجد قبل الفعل، ثم تبقى إلى وقت وجود الفعل فلا يكون الفعل ثابتا ممن ليس بقادر. وقد أقمنا الدلالة على بطلان القول ببقاء الأعراض فلم يمكنهم التعلق بهذا المحال، ثم نقول : هب أن القدرة تبقى إلى وقت وجود الفعل ولكن مع هذا لا ينفعكم ذلك فإنا نقول لكم : إذا بقيت إلى وقت وجود الفعل أكانت حالة البقاء قدرة على هذا الفعل الذي اقترن بها وقت البقاء ؟ أم كانت قدرة لا على هذا الفعل بل لفعل غيره يفعل بها في الثاني من وقت وجود هذا الفعل؟ فإن قالوا: هي في حالة البقاء قدرة على فعل آخر غير هذا الذي وجد في حال بقاءه وقدرة هذا الفعل هي في أول أحوال وجودها ويتعقبها الفعل، ثم هي في الثاني في حال وجودها قدرة فعل آخر يوجد في الثالث من حال وجودها وهو قول بعضهم. قلنا هذا مثل الأول فإنها لما لم تكن فى كل حال قدرة لما يقارنها من الفعل بل هي قدرة على ما يتعقبه من الفعل كان كل فعل ثابتًا لا بما له من القدرة، ووجود ما هو قدرة على غيره لا يجدي نفعا ولا يدفع ما ألزمنا. وإن قالوا: هي في الثاني قدرة على ما ثبت فيه من الفعل المقارن فيها. قلنا: إذا كانت هي في الثاني قدرة على ما يثبت فيه من الفعل فكانت هي قدرة الفعل الذي اقترن بها، وبطل جميع سعيهم، واضمحل أكثر شبهاتهم، ولم يحصل لهم من القول بتقدمها على زمان وجود الفعل - مع أنها في الحال المتقدمة على وجود الفعل ليست بقدرة لهذا الفعل - إلا القول ببقاء الأعراض وهو باطل، والقول بوجود قدرة لا مقدور لها ولا مقارناً لها ولا متأخرا عنها، وهو محال. ثم يقال لهم : إذا كانت القدرة في زمان وجودها موجودة، أيستحيل وجود الفعل معها أم لا؟ فإن قالوا: لا، فقد أقروا بما ذهب إليه خصومهم، وبطل جميع ما يتعلقون به.
And some Mu'tazila carried this speech to the extent that they constructed an impossibility, and allowed the survival (baqa') of power and said: 'Power exists before the action, then it survives to the time of the existence of the action, so action is not established from one who is not capable.' And we have established the proof of the invalidity of the statement of survival of accidents, so they cannot cling to this impossibility. Then we say: Suppose that power survives to the time of the existence of the action, but with this it does not benefit you. For we say to you: When it survives to the time of the existence of the action, is it in the state of survival a power for this action that is simultaneous with it at the time of survival? Or is it a power not for this action but for another action that is done by it in the second [time] from the time of the existence of this action? If they say: It is in the state of survival a power for another action other than this one that occurred in the state of its survival, and the power for this action is in the first of the states of its existence and the action follows it, then in the second it is in the state of its existence a power for another action that exists in the third of the states of its existence, and this is the statement of some of them. We say: This is like the first. For since it is not in every state a power for the action that is simultaneous with it, but rather it is a power for what follows it, every action is established not by its own power, and the existence of that which is a power for other than it does not benefit nor repel what we have necessitated. And if they say: It is in the second a power for what is established in it of the action simultaneous with it. We say: If it is in the second a power for what is established in it of the action, then it is the power for the action that is simultaneous with it, and all their effort is invalidated, and most of their doubts are dissipated, and nothing remains for them from the statement of its precedence over the time of the existence of the action - with that it is in the state preceding the existence of the action not a power for this action - except the statement of survival of accidents, which is false, and the statement of the existence of a power that has no object of power (maqdur) nor simultaneous with it nor posterior to it, and that is impossible. Then it is said to them: When power is existent in the time of its existence, is the existence of the action impossible with it or not? If they say: No, then they have acknowledged what their opponents hold, and all that they cling to is invalidated.
Source pages p. 824

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of objections about divine power and action. Some Mu'tazila, in response to the previous argument, claimed that power can survive (baqa') and thus exists before the action and endures to the time of the action. The author first notes that the invalidity of the survival of accidents has already been proven. Then he proceeds to argue dialectically: If power does survive, is it a power for this action that occurs simultaneously with it in the state of survival, or for another action? If they say it is for another action that occurs later, then this action is not performed by its own power, which is absurd. If they say it is a power for this action in the second moment, then power is for the action simultaneous with it, which means it must be concurrent, contradicting their claim that power precedes the action. Thus, the only remaining position is the survival of accidents, which is false, and the existence of a power with no object, which is impossible. Finally, the author asks whether action can exist with power at the same time. If they admit it can, they concede the point. This passage effectively shows the absurdity of the Mu'tazila position.

Key Terms
قدرة
بقاء
فعل
مقدور
أعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that a power existing before an action can cause that action even if it is not specifically directed at it.
Correction: Every action requires a power that is specifically for it at the time of the action, not a power for a different action.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the consequence if the surviving power is a power for another action?
A) The action is performed by its own power.
B) The action is established without its own power.
C) Power cannot survive.
D) The Mu'tazila are correct.

Key Takeaway
The first horn shows that if power survives as a power for another action, then the present action is not caused by its own power, which is absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a good deed you intend to do but keep postponing. This lesson shows that if you have the power to do it now but reserve it for a 'later' action, then the present deed is not being performed by its own power—it becomes disconnected from your capacity. How does this realization compel you to act immediately? What is one specific action you will take right after this reflection, without delay?
?
You often rely on your own power to do actions, but this lesson reminds you that power must be directly tied to the action. Is there an area of your life where you assume your ability will automatically carry over to future actions, neglecting the present moment? Commit to one act of worship or obedience today, not with a general sense of capability, but with a concrete intention that directly drives the action now.
?
Al-Nasafi forces you to see that if power is for another action, the current action lacks its own power. In your relationship with Allah, do you sometimes 'save' your good intentions for later, treating them as separate from the action itself? How does this disconnect affect your sincerity? Ask Allah to grant you the grace to act on your good intentions immediately, making each action a direct expression of the power He has given you at this very moment.

The Second Horn and Final Conclusion
Al-Nasafi refutes the second horn and concludes with a final question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second horn of the dilemma forces the Mu'tazila into accepting the impossibility of surviving accidents or power without an object, and how the final question compels them to concede the opponents' position.

Core Idea
Power must be simultaneous with its action, not merely preceding.

Having refuted the first horn, Imam al-Nasafi now turns to the second possibility: the Mu'tazila might claim that the surviving power is a power for this action in the second moment. This leads to even deeper absurdities. Finally, a direct question leaves them with no escape but to concede the very point they were trying to avoid.

Guided Path
1
Examine the second horn
Read the Mu'tazila response that the surviving power is a power for this action simultaneous with it at the second moment.
This is the alternative to the first horn.
2
Analyze the consequences
Notice that this forces them to admit either the survival of accidents (already proven false) or the existence of power without any object (impossible).
The author reduces their position to two unacceptable options.
3
Understand the final question
The author asks: when power exists, is it impossible for action to exist with it? If they say no, they concede that action coexists with power, which is the opponents' view.
This seals the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

Where in your own life are you clinging to an argument that collapses under logical examination? Are you willing to let go of a false belief about Allah's decree that you hold onto out of habit?
?

How does this reshape your understanding of divine power and human responsibility? Will you now submit to the truth even if it challenges your preconceived notions?

Refutation of Double Punishment for Omission and Commission
Nasafi refutes double punishment for omission plus ugly act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi rejects the idea that not performing a commanded act plus committing an ugly act merits two separate punishments.

Core Idea
Punishment is for actual deeds, not double-counting omission and commission.

Nasafi encounters an objection from a heretical view that separates an omission from a sinful act to justify double punishment. He firmly dismisses this as alien to Islamic consensus. Let's examine his concise refutation and its implications for Islamic theology of human action.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the hypothetical scenario and the opponent's reasoning, then Nasafi's blunt verdict.
The opponent is unnamed but likely part of a known theological debate.
2
Identify the two grounds for punishment
The opponent claims punishment for the ugly act committed and for the good act not done. Identify these two parts in the text.
This double counting is the core of the dispute.
3
Understand Nasafi's dismissal
Nasafi says this is 'خروج عن أقاويل أهل الإسلام' – a departure from Islamic consensus. Reflect on why he considers it a departure rather than a valid opinion.
Islamic theology ties punishment to actual moral responsibility, not to mere absence of good.
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider the broader principle: should omission alone be punishable if one also commits a sin? How does this relate to justice?
This connects to the broader debate on qadar and human acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قيل له: إن هذا المأمور لو لم يفعل المأمورية وتغير تغيرا قبيحا؟ قال: استحق قسطين من العذاب أحدهما للقبيح الذي فعله، والآخر للحسن الذي لم يفعله وهذا كله خروج عن أقاويل أهل الإسلام.
Then it was said to him: If this commanded person does not perform the commanded thing and changes in an ugly way? He said: He deserves two portions of punishment: one for the ugly thing he did, and the other for the good thing he did not do. And all of this is a departure from the statements of the people of Islam.
Source pages p. 830

Commentary
This passage presents a specific objection against a heretical view (likely of Abu Hashim or similar). The opponent argues that if a person who is commanded to do something not only omits it but also performs an ugly action, he deserves two punishments: one for the ugly act and one for not doing the good act. The author (Nasafi) dismisses this as a departure from the consensus of Islam, implying that punishment is only for what one actually does, not for mere omission when combined with a sinful act. The argument is that the opponent's framework improperly separates the omission and the commission as two independent grounds for punishment, whereas Islamic theology ties punishment to actual deeds (whether acts or omissions in specific contexts).

Key Terms
قسطين من العذاب
تغير قبيح
أقاويل أهل الإسلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that omitting a commanded good act always adds an extra punishment even if one commits a sin.
Correction: Nasafi rejects this double count, stating it is a departure from Islamic consensus. Punishment is for the overall moral failure, not separate tallies.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the ruling on the view that someone who omits a commanded act and does an ugly act deserves two punishments?
A) It is a valid theological opinion.
B) It is a departure from Islam.
C) It is a matter of ijtihad.
D) It is universally accepted by scholars.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi decisively rejects the idea that omission and commission can be punished separately, aligning with the Islamic consensus that moral responsibility is holistic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you miss a prayer or commit a sin, do you ever mentally 'balance' the scales by thinking of your good deeds? Does this passage challenge that habit? How would it change your repentance if you saw each omission as part of one whole disobedience?
?
Consider a time you excused a small sin because you were already doing something good. How does the holistic view of punishment in this lesson make you rethink the seriousness of combining omission and commission? Are you willing to submit your entire moral record to Allah without counting?
?
This lesson teaches that Islamic theology does not atomize deeds. Do you live as if every moment of obedience and disobedience is interconnected? What would it mean for you to start seeing your life as a single, unified response to Allah's command?

Refutation of Double Punishment and the Goodness of Leaving Prayer
Nasafi refutes two absurd consequences of the opponent's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the absurd consequences of the opponent's view that every prohibition commands the opposite act, and why equating permissible with good leads to heresy.

Core Idea
A false premise about commands leads to heresy: leaving prayer becomes good.

In this passage, Nasafi continues his refutation of a heretical stance on divine commands and human actions. He highlights two absurd implications: first, that committing a sin would incur double punishment, and second, that omitting an obligatory act like prayer would be considered good. The argument reveals why the opponent's reasoning is not only flawed but also contradicts fundamental Islamic teachings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to get the overall argument.
The Arabic contains a slightly garbled phrase, but the main points are clear.
2
Understand the first absurdity
Identify the argument that if every prohibition implies a command to the opposite, then committing zina would require two punishments: one for the sin and one for not doing the commanded opposite (leaving zina).
3
Understand the second absurdity
Notice the opponent's claim that if leaving prayer is not prohibited, it is permissible and thus good. See how this follows from their principle that all permissible acts are good.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why Nasafi says this is a complete departure from the religion. The opponent's logic leads to the absurdity that omitting an obligation is a good act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قيل له: إن المكلف لما نهى عن الزنا فقد أمر بتركه لا محالة، فينبغي أن يقال: إنه لما زنى يجب عليه حدان أحدهما للزنا الذي فعله، والآخر للحسن الذي لم يفعله وهو ترك الزنا. وكذا في جميع هذه الأفعال، فأما ترك هذه الأفعال فغير واجب عليه، ويلزم ألا يكون ترك الزنا وشرب الخمر واللواطة طاعة ويرتكب ذلك. أسباب الحدود، فزعم عنه توجه هذا الإلزام عليه أنه نهى عن وكذا زعم أن ترك الصلاة إذا لم يكن منهيًا عنه ليس بقبيح، ثم عنده ما ليس بقبيح بل هو مباح يكون حسنًا إذ من مذهبه أن كل مباح حسن، وإنما يجب بحسن زائد فيكون على هذا ترك الصلاة حسنًا، وهذا خروج عن الدين والانسلاخ منه بمرة، نعوذ بالله من قول هذا عقباه.
Then it was said to him: When the morally responsible person is forbidden from zina, he is necessarily commanded to leave it. So it should be said: When he commits zina, two punishments are due: one for the zina he did, and the other for the good he did not do, namely leaving zina. Likewise in all these actions. As for leaving these actions, it is not obligatory upon him, and it follows that leaving zina, drinking wine, and sodomy would not be obedience, and he commits them. The causes of the punishments, so he claimed that this implication applies to him because he forbade... And likewise he claimed that leaving prayer, if it is not prohibited, is not ugly; then in his view what is not ugly but permissible is good, since his doctrine is that every permissible thing is good, and it is only obligatory due to an added goodness. So according to this, leaving prayer is good, and this is a departure from the religion and a complete shedding of it. We seek refuge with Allah from the end of such a statement.
Source pages p. 830

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of the heretical view. The author argues that if every prohibition implies a command to its opposite, then committing zina would incur not just the punishment for zina but also an additional punishment for not doing the good act of leaving zina. This would apply to all sins, leading to an absurd multiplication of punishments. The author further notes that the opponent claims that leaving prayer, if not explicitly prohibited, is not ugly but rather permissible and therefore good (since in his system all permissible acts are good). This would make omitting prayer a good act, which is clearly heretical. The author concludes that such a position is a complete departure from and shedding of the religion.

Key Terms
حدان
ترك الصلاة
مباح حسن
خروج عن الدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every permissible act is good and therefore rewarded.
Correction: Permissible acts are not inherently good; goodness depends on intention and divine command. Otherwise, omitting obligations could be considered good, leading to heresy.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is a consequence of the opponent's view that every permissible act is good?
A) All sins become permissible.
B) Leaving prayer becomes a good act.
C) Prayer becomes obligatory.
D) Punishment becomes unlawful.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's view leads to the heretical conclusion that leaving prayer is good, demonstrating a complete departure from the religion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever rationalized missing a prayer by telling yourself it's 'not a big deal'? How does this passage's warning that such rationalization can lead to heresy change your view of even a single missed prayer? What will you do to ensure you never treat prayer as optional?
?
If you think an action is only bad if explicitly forbidden, do you see how that logic could make you justify negligence in obligations? How does the opponent's conclusion that leaving prayer is good expose the danger in your own thinking? What step can you take today to strengthen your commitment to commanded acts?
?
The author calls the opponent's view 'a complete shedding of the religion.' Have you ever felt that minor sins or omissions are trivial? Does this statement jolt you into seeing how seemingly small deviations in belief can lead to a total loss of faith? What one change will you make in your daily practice to guard against such reasoning?

Why Power Cannot Precede Action: Independence and Consensus
A proof refuting the precedence of power over action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that human power precedes action leads to the conclusion of independence from God, and how the consensus of asking for help (mu'awana) and tawfiq supports the concurrent view.

Core Idea
If power precedes action, we don't need God in the moment of acting—that is kufr.

In this passage, the author presents a key proof from the Maturidi school against the idea that human power (qudra) comes before the action. He argues that such a view would make the servant independent of God at the moment of action, which is disbelief. He then uses the universal Muslim practice of asking God for help and success to show that we are never independent—our power is given with the action, not before.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage ends abruptly with 'أئمتنا' – the original likely continues.
2
Identify the main claim and its consequence
Find the claim being refuted (power precedes action) and the consequence the author derives (independence from God).
3
Understand the consensus argument
Notice how the author uses the universal practice of asking for help (mu'awana) and tawfiq to argue that power must be concurrent with the act.
If we already had power, why would we ask?
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why independence from God is considered kufr. How does this relate to the core idea of tawhid?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقدم القدرة على الفعل يؤدي إلى الاستغناء عن الله: ومن مشاهير الدلائل لنا في المسألة: أن القول بتقدم القدرة على الفعل يوجب استفتاء العهد عن الله تعالى وقت الفعل والقول بغنية العبد عن الله تعالى في لحظة من عمره كفر. وكذا إجماع أهل القبلة على سؤال المعونة وطلب التوفيق من الله تعالى في كل وقت، ولو كان الإنسان أعطى قدرة الطاعة قبل وجودها لم يكن للطلب معنى. وراء ذلك طرق كثيرة أوردها أئمتنا
The precedence of power over action leads to independence from God: Among the well-known proofs for us in the matter is that the statement that power precedes action necessitates the annulment of the covenant with God Almighty at the time of the action, and the statement that the servant is independent of God Almighty for a moment of his life is disbelief (kufr). Likewise, the consensus of Ahl al-Qibla on asking for help (mu'awana) and seeking success (tawfiq) from God Almighty at every time; and if man were given the power of obedience before its occurrence, the request would have no meaning. Beyond that are many paths (turuq) that our imams have mentioned.
Source pages p. 834

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalil) refuting the view that power (qudra) precedes the action (fi'l). The author states that this view leads to the claim that the servant is independent of God at the moment of action, which is kufr. He then provides another argument based on consensus (ijma'): all Muslims agree that they must ask God for help (mu'awana) and success (tawfiq) at every moment. If a person already possessed the power of obedience before performing the act, then such prayers would be meaningless—why ask for what one already has? Thus, power must be concurrent with the act, not preceding it. The author concludes by noting that there are many other proofs on this matter from the imams.

Refuting the Mu'tazilite Proof from the Verse on Strength
Refutes Mu'tazilite claim that capacity precedes action using Quranic verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that capacity must precede action by reinterpreting the verse 'Take with strength' and arguing that capacity is simultaneous with the action.

Core Idea
The very verse the Mu'tazila cite proves capacity is simultaneous, not prior.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi takes on a key Mu'tazilite proof from the Quran. They claim the verse 'Take what We have given you with strength' shows that capacity (istiṭāʿa) exists before the action. Al-Nasafi turns the tables: the verse requires strength at the time of taking, and because strength is an accident that cannot endure, it cannot exist before the act. Let's see the argument unfold.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite claim and al-Nasafi's approach
Read the opening lines where al-Nasafi states his intention to refute the opponents' doubts. Note that he first reinterprets the proof verses as referring to 'first capacity' (soundness of instruments).
This sets the stage for the specific verse argument.
2
Examine the reinterpretation of the proof verses
Al-Nasafi says the verses Mu'tazila cling to are about 'the first capacity', which is the health of causes and instruments, not the capacity that accompanies the action.
This is a key terminological distinction.
3
Analyze the argument from 'Take with strength'
Focus on how al-Nasafi uses the verse: taking with strength requires strength at the time of taking, not before. He compares it to taking with the hand—the hand must be present at the act.
Notice the logical force: if strength were absent, it would not be taking with strength.
4
Grasp why strength cannot precede action
Al-Nasafi argues that strength is an accident that cannot endure (istiḥālat baqā'ihā). Therefore, it cannot exist before the action and then remain until the action; it must be created at the moment of action.
This appeals to the nature of accidents in kalam.
5
Note the metaphorical interpretation as additional support
He mentions that some exegetes interpret 'strength' as seriousness and perseverance, which undermines the literal reading the Mu'tazila exploit.
This is an ancillary point, not the main argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونصرف العناية بعد هذا إلى دفع شبهات الخصوم وحل إشكالاتهم فنقول وبالله المعونة: ما تتعلق به المعتزلة من الآيات التي فيها إشارة إلى تقدم الاستطاعة، محمول على الاستطاعة الأولى التي هي سلامة الأسباب وصحة الآلات، وقوله تعالى: (خذوا ما آتيناكم بقوة) دليلنا؛ لأنه إنما يصير أخذا بالقوة إذا كانت القوة وقت الأخذ موجودة، فأما إذا كانت هي معدومة وقت الأخذ فلا يكون أخذا بقوة كالأخذ باليد، ثم ليس من ضرورة الأخذ بالقوة تقدمها على الأخذ بل وجودها عند الأخذ، ومن ضرورة وجودها عند الأخذ أن لا تكون موجودة قبله لاستحالة بقائها. على أن أهل التأويل قالوا: (خذوا ما آتيناكم بقوة) أي: بجد ومواظبة.
And we direct attention after this to repelling the doubts of the opponents and solving their problems, so we say, and with Allah is assistance: What the Mu'tazila cling to from the verses that indicate the priority of capacity (al-istiṭāʿa) is interpreted as the first capacity, which is the soundness of causes and the health of instruments. And His statement, the Exalted: 'Take what We have given you with strength' is our proof; because one only becomes a taker with strength if the strength exists at the time of taking. But if it is non-existent at the time of taking, then it is not a taking with strength, like taking with the hand. Then it is not a necessary consequence of taking with strength that it precede the taking; rather its existence is at the time of taking. And from the necessity of its existence at the time of taking is that it not exist before it, due to the impossibility of its endurance. Moreover, the people of interpretation said: 'Take what We have given you with strength,' meaning with seriousness and perseverance.
Source pages p. 835

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Mu'tazilite argument that uses Quranic verses to claim that capacity (istiṭāʿa) must precede the action. The author begins by stating his intention to refute the doubts of opponents. He interprets the verses the Mu'tazila rely on as referring to the 'first capacity'—the soundness of bodily instruments and causes, not the capacity that is directly linked to the action. He then focuses on the verse 'Take what We have given you with strength' (Quran 7:171). The author argues that this verse actually supports the Ash'ari/Maturidi position: 'taking with strength' requires that the strength be present at the time of taking, not before it. If strength were absent when the taking occurs, then taking with strength would be impossible, just as taking with the hand requires the hand to be present. Furthermore, it is not necessary for the strength to precede the taking; rather its existence coincides with the action. In fact, the strength cannot exist before the action because it is an accident that cannot endure (istiḥālat baqā'ihā). The author also notes that some exegetes interpret the 'strength' in the verse metaphorically as seriousness and perseverance, which undermines the literal sense the Mu'tazila try to exploit. This argument establishes that capacity is simultaneous with the action, not prior.

Refuting the Objection with Capacity for Opposites
Answers Mu'tazilite objection using capacity for opposites

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi/Ash'ari doctrine of capacity being suitable for opposites refutes the Mu'tazilite objection that simultaneous capacity entails tasking with the impossible.

Core Idea
Capacity for opposites resolves the paradox of impossible tasks.

The Mu'tazila argued that if capacity does not precede action, then commanding a disbeliever to believe while he disbelieves is an impossible task, which is morally repugnant. In this passage, the author presents the response from those who hold that capacity is suitable for opposites: the disbeliever's capacity was always fit for faith, so his failure to believe is his own choice, and the objection dissolves.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Mu'tazilite argument carefully: they claim that if capacity is simultaneous, then the disbeliever has no power for faith at the moment of command, so the command is impossible and thus ugly.
Notice that they equate lack of actual faith with lack of capacity.
2
Understand the response
The response asserts that capacity is suitable for opposites (faith and disbelief). The disbeliever's capacity for disbelief was also capable of producing faith. So he had the power to believe but misused it.
This is the key point: capacity is not restricted to one specific act.
3
See the refutation's force
The author then shows that the Mu'tazilite own position leads to the same requirement (to bring faith instead of disbelief), so their objection is self-defeating and invalid.
The objection is not only answered but turned against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم : إن الاستطاعة لو لم تكن سابقة على الفعل لكان فيه تكليف ما لا يطاق وهو قبيح، وهذا لأن قدرة الإيمان لو كانت مع الإيمان ولا إيمان مع الكافر فلا تكون له قدرة الإيمان، فكان تكليفه الإيمان تكليف ما لا يطاق. فنقول لهم: إن من قال من أصحابنا إن القدرة تصلح للضدين فهو يقول: إن قدرة الكفر كانت صالحة للإيمان فمنحه القدرة الصالحة للإيمان، فكان يجب عليه أن يكتسب الإيمان بدل الكفر. والمعتزلة لا يمكنهم إلا هذا إذ الواجب عليهم إيجاد الإيمان بدلاً من الكفر فأما إيجاده مع قيام الكفر فمحال، فكان هو ممكنا بأن يفعل بالقدرة الصالحة للضدين الإيمان لا الكفر ، فكذا هؤلاء، فكان الإلزام على هذا فاسداً.
And as for their statement: If capacity (al-istiṭāʿa) were not prior to the action, then it would entail tasking with what is unbearable (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq), which is ugly. This is because the power (qudra) for faith, if it were with faith, and there is no faith with the disbeliever, then he would have no power for faith, so tasking him with faith would be tasking with what is unbearable. So we say to them: The one among our companions who says that power is suitable for opposites (ṣāliḥ lil-ḍiddain) says: The power for disbelief was suitable for faith, so He granted him the power suitable for faith, so it was obligatory upon him to acquire faith instead of disbelief. And the Mu'tazila cannot but [say] this, for it is obligatory upon them to bring about faith instead of disbelief; as for bringing it about while disbelief subsists, that is impossible. So it was possible for him to do with the power suitable for opposites faith, not disbelief. So likewise these [people], so the objection on this basis is invalid.
Source pages p. 836

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the Mu'tazila and a response from the perspective of those who hold that capacity is suitable for opposites (ṣāliḥ lil-ḍiddain). The Mu'tazila argue: if capacity does not precede the action, then at the moment of action, a disbeliever has no power for faith (since he has disbelief), so commanding him to believe would be a task beyond his capacity (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq), which is morally reprehensible (qabīḥ). The author responds with a nuancing: According to the Ash'ari/Maturidi position, the power for disbelief is actually suitable for faith as well (since power can produce opposites). Therefore, the disbeliever is given a power that is capable of both faith and disbelief. It is his choice to use it for disbelief. So it is obligatory for him to direct that power towards faith instead of disbelief. The Mu'tazila themselves also require that the disbeliever bring about faith instead of disbelief; but they cannot claim that bringing faith while disbelief exists is impossible, because they hold that capacity precedes action. So the Mu'tazilite objection is self-defeating: the same logic applies to them, and thus the objection is invalid (fāsid).

القدرة تصلح للضدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: That the impossibility of an act logically forces capacity to precede the act in time.
Correction: The doctrine of capacity for opposites shows that simultaneous capacity is sufficient; the command is possible because the disbeliever's capacity was always capable of producing faith.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi doctrine of capacity for opposites refutes the Mu'tazilite objection by showing that the disbeliever always had the power to believe, so the command is not impossible.

The First Proof: Instruments Are Suitable for Opposites
First proof from instruments for capacity's suitability for opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that capacity (istiṭāʿa) is suitable for opposites, based on the nature of physical instruments.

Core Idea
Instruments like the tongue prove that capacity is versatile, not fixed.

The author now presents the proofs for the view that capacity is suitable for opposites. The first argument is straightforward: look at the instruments we use. The tongue, for example, can produce truth or falsehood, affirmation or denial. If the instrument itself can yield opposites, then the capacity that drives it must be equally versatile.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the author's structure: he first announces the need for proofs, then states the first proof with an example.
The passage ends abruptly with 'and likewise' (وكذا), indicating more examples follow.
2
Identify the key example
Focus on the example of the tongue. What opposite actions does the author mention? (truth/falsehood, affirmation/denial). This is the core of the proof.
3
Understand the logical connection
Ask yourself: why does the example of the tongue prove that capacity is suitable for opposites? The reasoning is that if the instrument (a cause of action) can produce opposites, then the capacity that operates through it must also be suitable for opposites.
This is an argument from the nature of causes (asbāb al-fiʿl).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنا لما سبق منا القول في كيفية الخلاف بين القائلين أن الاستطاعة الفعل في كون القدرة صالحة للضدين فلابد من أن نبين ها هنا لبعض ما يتعلق به كل فريق على طريقة الاختصار ليقف عليه قارئ كتابنا هذا فنقول : [أدلة الذين يقولون إن الاستطاعة صالحة للضدين]: إن القائلين بأنها تصلح للضدين يتعلقون بأشياء منها أن كل سبب من أسباب الفعل يصلح للضدين، فإن الآلات والأدوات المعدة لتتسم القدرة الناقصة صالحة للضدين كاللسان صلح للصدق والكذب والإقرار والتكذيب وغير ذلك، وكذا
Then, since we have previously stated the manner of disagreement between those saying that capacity (al-istiṭāʿa) is the action regarding the power being suitable for opposites, it is necessary that we explain here some of what each group adheres to, in a concise manner so that the reader of our book may grasp it. So we say: [Proofs of those who say that capacity is suitable for opposites]: Those who say that it is suitable for opposites cling to things, among them that every cause of an action is suitable for opposites. Source pages p. 836

Commentary
This passage begins a new section where the author presents proofs for the doctrine that capacity (istiṭāʿa) is suitable for opposites (ṣāliḥ lil-ḍiddain). He first explains why this is necessary: since the disagreement on whether capacity is prior to or simultaneous with action hinges on whether power can produce opposites, he must clarify the proofs for the latter position. The first proof he presents is from the nature of causes and instruments. Every cause of an action, such as a bodily organ, is suitable for opposite actions. For example, the tongue can be used for truth or falsehood, affirmation or denial, and other opposites. Similarly, other instruments and tools prepared for the incomplete power (presumably the power that is not yet fully realized in the action) are also suitable for opposites. The argument implies that since the instrument is suitable for opposites, the capacity that operates through that instrument must likewise be suitable for opposites. The passage is the beginning of an enumeration; the 'and likewise' indicates more examples follow, but the text cuts off at the end of the page.

Mu'tazilī Objection on Capacity and Māturīdī Response
Mu'tazilī objection and its Māturīdī refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Māturīdī doctrine of capacity (qudra) sufficient for both opposites refutes the Mu'tazilī claim that prior capacity is necessary to avoid assigning the impossible.

Core Idea
Capacity is general, not specific—the disbeliever could have chosen faith.

The Mu'tazila argued that if capacity did not precede the act, then commanding the disbeliever to believe would be impossible and therefore ugly. The Māturīdī response clarifies that capacity is not tied to a single act but is sufficient for opposites. This passage defends the validity of the command by showing the disbeliever possessed the power to believe but chose disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the Mu'tazilī objection in the Arabic and translation. Notice how they link prior capacity to the possibility of the command.
The objection is structured as a conditional: if capacity is not prior, then taklīf mā lā yuṭāq follows.
2
Identify the Māturīdī response
Read the response beginning with 'We say to them'. Note the key claim: capacity is sufficient for both opposites.
3
Understand 'sufficient for both opposites'
Grasp the idea that the same power can be used for either of two contradictory acts (e.g., faith or disbelief). The disbeliever had this power.
This is a distinctive Māturīdī position.
4
Trace the refutation of the objection
See how the Māturīdīs argue that since capacity is general, the command was possible and thus not ugly. The objection fails because it assumes a specific capacity.
The response also points out that the Mu'tazila themselves must accept this logic.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this matters: it defends divine justice and the coherence of command and capacity.
This connects to broader debates on human agency and God's justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم : إن الاستطاعة لو لم تكن سابقة على الفعل لكان فيه تكليف ما لا يطاق وهو قبيح، وهذا لأن قدرة الإيمان لو كانت مع الإيمان ولا إيمان مع الكافر فلا تكون له قدرة الإيمان، فكان تكليفه الإيمان تكليف ما لا يطاق. فنقول لهم: إن من قال من أصحابنا إن القدرة تصلح للضدين فهو يقول: إن قدرة الكفر كانت صالحة للإيمان فمنحه القدرة الصالحة للإيمان، فكان يجب عليه أن يكتسب الإيمان بدل الكفر. والمعتزلة لا يمكنهم إلا هذا إذ الواجب عليهم إيجاد الإيمان بدلاً من الكفر فأما إيجاده مع قيام الكفر فمحال، فكان هو ممكنا بأن يفعل بالقدرة الصالحة للضدين الإيمان لا الكفر ، فكذا هؤلاء، فكان الإلزام على هذا فاسداً.
And as for their saying: 'If capacity were not prior to the act, then it would entail assignment of what cannot be borne (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq), and that is ugly (qabīḥ). This is because the power for faith would be with faith, and there is no faith with the disbeliever, so he would have no power for faith. Thus assigning him faith would be an assignment of what cannot be borne.' We say to them: whoever among our companions says that capacity (qudra) is sufficient for both opposites (al-ḍidayn) says: the power for disbelief was sufficient for faith, so He granted him the power sufficient for faith, therefore it was incumbent upon him to acquire faith instead of disbelief. The Mu'tazila cannot (hold) except this, for it is obligatory upon them to bring about faith instead of disbelief. As for bringing it about while disbelief persists, that is impossible. So it was possible for him to perform by the power sufficient for both opposites faith and not disbelief. So it is with these (people). So the objection (ilzām) on this basis is corrupt.
Source pages p. 836

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Mu'tazilī objection and the Māturīdī response. The Mu'tazila argue that if capacity did not precede the act, then the disbeliever would have no capacity for faith at the time he is commanded to believe, making the command impossible and therefore 'ugly' (qabīḥ). The Māturīdī answer by affirming that capacity (qudra) is sufficient for both opposites (al-ḍidayn). The disbeliever was given a power that was sufficient for faith, but he chose disbelief. Therefore he was capable of faith and the command was valid. The Mu'tazila are forced to accept this because they hold that one must bring about faith instead of disbelief, but they cannot bring about both simultaneously. Thus the original objection fails because it assumes capacity is specific to one act, which the Māturīdīs reject.

Key Terms
استطاعة
قدرة
ضدين
تكليف ما لا يطاق
قبيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilī view that capacity must be prior to the act, otherwise the command is impossible.
Correction: The Māturīdīs hold that capacity can be simultaneous with the act because it is general for opposites, so the disbeliever had the power to believe.

Checkpoint
According to the Māturīdī response, why was the disbeliever obligated to have faith?
A) Because he had a power that was sufficient for both faith and disbelief.
B) Because his power for disbelief forced him to faith.
C) Because he had no power at all.
D) Because the command was given before his existence.

Key Takeaway
The Māturīdīs affirm that capacity is general for opposites, so the disbeliever had the power to believe, making the command valid and not impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the disbeliever had the power to believe but chose not to, what does that reveal about your own choices when you neglect obligations? How will you use the capacity Allah has given you today to obey Him instead of your desires?
?
Have you ever felt that a command of Allah was too difficult for you, making you excuse yourself? How does this lesson—that your capacity is already sufficient for both faith and sin—remove that excuse and demand a response from you right now?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that commanding the impossible is ugly, but Allah commands you to believe and obey. What specific sin or negligence are you holding onto while claiming you cannot do otherwise? Reflect: is it truly impossible, or have you simply chosen the easier path? What step will you take today to act on the capacity you already have?

First Proof: Capacity is for Both Opposites (Causes of Acts)
First proof using the analogy of causes and instruments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first proof demonstrates that human capacity is sufficient for both opposites through the analogy of causes and instruments.

Core Idea
Every cause of an act is naturally suited for opposite outcomes, proving capacity is flexible.

The Māturīdī argument that human capacity can produce either of two opposites relies on observable facts about causes. The author presents the first proof: all causes and instruments that receive capacity are themselves neutral and can be used for contradictory actions. This passage gives concrete examples to show that the same capacity can produce either of two opposites.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once, then the translation, noting the structure of the proof.
Pay attention to the examples given.
2
Identify the key parts
Find the statement of the principle, the two examples, and the generalization.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this proof supports the view that capacity is for both opposites and does not require a separate capacity for each act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن القائلين بأنها تصلح للضدين يتعلقون بأشياء منها أن كل سبب من أسباب الفعل يصلح للضدين، فإن الآلات والأدوات المعدة لتتسم القدرة الناقصة صالحة للضدين كاللسان صلح للصدق والكذب والإقرار والتكذيب وغير ذلك، وكذا اليد تصلح لقتل الكفار والجهاد معهم، ولسفك دماء المسلمين والسعي في الأرض بالفساد وإثارة العبث والفتنة في العباد وكذا كل شيء.
Indeed, those who say that it (capacity) is sufficient for both opposites cling to things, among them: that every cause (sabab) of an act is sufficient for both opposites, for the instruments and tools prepared for (receiving) incomplete capacity are sufficient for both opposites, like the tongue is suitable for truth and falsehood, affirmation and denial, and other things. Similarly, the hand is suitable for killing disbelievers and for jihad with them, and for shedding the blood of Muslims and causing corruption on earth and raising mischief and sedition among people. And so on for everything.
Source pages p. 836

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof (al-adilla) for the view that human capacity is sufficient for both opposites (al-ḍidayn). The argument is analogical: all causes and instruments (ālāt wa adawāt) that are the loci of capacity are suitable for producing opposite acts. The example of the tongue shows it can serve both truth and falsehood, affirmation and denial. The hand can serve both legitimate jihad and sinful corruption. Since these instruments receive capacity and are neutral, capacity itself must be equally flexible. This shows that a specific act does not require a unique capacity; rather, the same capacity can be directed to either of two opposites.

Key Terms
سبب
آلات
أدوات
اللسان
اليد
ضدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that capacity is uniquely tied to the specific act it produces, such that a capacity for truth cannot also produce falsehood.
Correction: This proof shows that the very same cause (e.g., the tongue) can produce either truth or falsehood, demonstrating that capacity is not specific to one act but is flexible.

Checkpoint
What example does the author give to show that causes of acts are sufficient for both opposites?
A) The tongue for truth and falsehood.
B) The eye for seeing good and evil.
C) The foot for walking to mosque and market.
D) The heart for belief and doubt.

Key Takeaway
The same capacity can produce opposite acts because the instruments of action are themselves neutral.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak, do you pause to consider that your tongue is equally capable of truth and falsehood? Which do you habitually choose, and what does that say about your heart? Commit today to use your tongue only for what pleases Allah.
?
Your hands can either serve others in jihad for Allah's cause or cause corruption and harm. Reflect on your daily actions: are your hands instruments of mercy or mischief? What one change can you make to ensure your hands are used for good?
?
The neutrality of your limbs means the responsibility for your actions lies entirely with you. If you sin, you cannot blame your hand or tongue—they are merely tools. How will this awareness change your response when you are tempted to make excuses for your misdeeds?

The Taklif Proof for Capacity Being for Both Opposites
The author gives a second proof from taklif ma la yutaq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the impossibility of assigning what cannot be borne (taklif ma la yutaq) proves that capacity must be sufficient for both opposites.

Core Idea
Capacity must be for both opposites; otherwise God's commands would be impossible.

The author now presents a second proof against the claim that capacity is specific to one act. This proof is based on the well-known principle that God does not command what is beyond one's ability. Since He commands faith from the disbeliever, the disbeliever must have had the capacity for faith, otherwise the command would be impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the second proof
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the conditional statement: 'if capacity were not sufficient for both opposites, there would be assignment of what cannot be borne.'
The proof is concise but relies on the principle that taklif ma la yutaq is impossible.
2
Identify the principle used
The principle is that God never commands something impossible for the individual. This is a fundamental kalam axiom.
3
Apply the principle to the case of the disbeliever
The disbeliever is commanded to believe. If his capacity could only produce disbelief, the command would be impossible. Therefore, his capacity must be able to produce faith as well.
This shows capacity must be for both opposites.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Thus, the claim that capacity is only for one specific act leads to an impossibility, so it must be false.
This is a logical (burhani) proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن القدرة لو كانت لا تصلح للضدين لكان فيه تكليف ما لا يطاق (على ما بينا أن الكافر مأمور بالإيمان ولو لم يكن معه القدرة الصالحة للإيمان لكان فيه تكليف بما لا يطاق). ومنها أن كل ما يحصل به شيء ولا يصلح لضده يكون الحاصل به بالطبع لا بالاختيار كالثلج الذي يحصل به التبريد دون التسخين والنار التي يحصل بها التسخين دون التبريد، فيكون القول بأنها لا تصلح للضدين قولاً بالاضطرار وتقرير هذا أن قدرة الكفر التي وجدت في الكافر لو كانت غير صالحة للإيمان ولا تصلح إلا للكفر لا يتمكن الكافر المختص بقدرة الكفر أن ينفك عن قدرة الكفر، وأن يعدل عنه وأن يأتي بالإيمان، وكذا في الجالس مع القيام والمتحرك مع السكون وكل متضادين، وفيه أمران أحدهما : تكليف ما ليس في الوسع، والآخر: ارتفاع الفرق بين المضطر ،والقادر ، والتمييز بين الأفعال الاختيارية والأفعال الاضطرارية، والفرق بينهما معلوم بطريق الاضطرار. فما أدى إلى خلافه كان بطلانه معلومًا بطريق الاضطرار.
And among them: that if capacity were not sufficient for both opposites, there would be assignment of what cannot be borne (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq), as we explained that the disbeliever is commanded to have faith, and if he did not have the power sufficient for faith, there would be assignment of what cannot be borne. And among them: that everything by which a thing occurs and is not sufficient for its opposite, that which occurs by it is by nature (bi-l-ṭabʿ) not by choice (bi-l-ikhtiyār), like snow, which produces cooling without heating, and fire, which produces heating without cooling. So the statement that it (capacity) is not sufficient for both opposites is a statement of compulsion (iḍṭirār). The clarification of this is that the power for disbelief that exists in the disbeliever, if it were not sufficient for faith and were only for disbelief, the disbeliever who is specifically attached to the power for disbelief would not be able to disengage from the power for disbelief, nor to turn away from it, nor to bring about faith. Similarly for the sitting person with respect to standing, the moving person with respect to stillness, and every pair of opposites. In this there are two matters: one of them is assignment of what is not within one's capacity (taklīf mā laysa fī l-wusʿ), and the other is the elimination of the distinction between the compelled (al-muḍṭarr) and the capable (al-qādir), and the discrimination between voluntary acts (al-afʿāl al-ikhtiyāriyya) and compelled acts (al-afʿāl al-iḍṭirāriyya). The difference between them is known by necessity (bi-ṭarīq al-iḍṭirār). So whatever leads to the opposite of that, its falseness is known by necessity.
Source pages p. 837

Commentary
This passage provides the second and third proofs (adilla) for the claim that capacity is sufficient for both opposites. The second proof repeats the argument from taklīf: if capacity were specific to one act, commanding opposites would be impossible, and since the command to faith is directed at disbelievers, they must have had capacity for it. The third proof is more elaborate: if a power only produces one specific effect without its opposite, that effect would be natural (bi-l-ṭabʿ) not voluntary (bi-l-ikhtiyār), like snow's cooling or fire's heating. This would lead to compulsion (iḍṭirār). To illustrate: if the disbeliever's power could only produce disbelief, he could never choose faith, just as a fire cannot cool. This would result in two unacceptable consequences: (1) assignment of what is beyond capacity (since he cannot do otherwise), and (2) the erasure of the distinction between voluntary and compelled acts—a distinction that is known by necessity (iḍṭirār). Therefore, the claim that capacity is not for both opposites is false.

Key Terms
تكليف ما لا يطاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that capacity can be specific to one act while the agent still has free choice.
Correction: The proof shows that if capacity cannot produce the commanded opposite, the command becomes impossible, negating free choice.

Checkpoint
What would be the consequence if capacity were not sufficient for both opposites, according to the second proof?
A) Acts would be by nature only.
B) There would be assignment of what cannot be borne.
C) The disbeliever would become a believer.
D) Capacity would be eternal.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of taklif ma la yutaq proves that capacity must be sufficient for both opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there commands of Allah that you have convinced yourself you cannot fulfill, perhaps because of your circumstances or past? How does this proof—that capacity must be for both opposites—challenge that excuse?
?
Think of a specific area in your life where you consistently choose disobedience while believing you have no choice. What would it mean for you to truly accept that you have the capacity to obey?
?
This lesson teaches that even the disbeliever is addressed with the command to believe. What command of Allah are you most avoiding right now, and what is stopping you from acting on it today?

The Compulsion Proof: Capacity Must Be for Both to Preserve Free Will
The author gives a third proof from the natural versus voluntary distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third proof shows that capacity being for both opposites is necessary to preserve the distinction between voluntary and compelled acts.

Core Idea
Capacity for only one act makes acts natural, not voluntary, destroying moral responsibility.

The author now presents a more elaborate proof. He argues that if a power can only produce one specific effect without its opposite, that effect would be natural, like fire's heat or snow's cold. This would mean all human acts are compelled, eliminating the difference between voluntary and involuntary actions—a distinction known by necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the third proof
Read the Arabic passage from 'ومنها أن كل ما يحصل به شيء' to the end. Notice the analogy with snow and fire.
The proof uses a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Understand the analogy
Snow produces cooling only; fire produces heating only. Similarly, if a capacity produces only one act, that act is by nature (bi-l-tab'), not choice.
3
Identify the two harmful consequences
The passage lists two matters: (1) assignment beyond capacity, (2) elimination of the distinction between the compelled and the capable, and between voluntary and compelled acts.
4
Reflect on the self-evident nature of the distinction
The author states that the difference between voluntary and compelled acts is known by necessity (bi-tariq al-idtirar). So any argument that denies it is necessarily false.
This is a strong epistemological claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن القدرة لو كانت لا تصلح للضدين لكان فيه تكليف ما لا يطاق (على ما بينا أن الكافر مأمور بالإيمان ولو لم يكن معه القدرة الصالحة للإيمان لكان فيه تكليف بما لا يطاق). ومنها أن كل ما يحصل به شيء ولا يصلح لضده يكون الحاصل به بالطبع لا بالاختيار كالثلج الذي يحصل به التبريد دون التسخين والنار التي يحصل بها التسخين دون التبريد، فيكون القول بأنها لا تصلح للضدين قولاً بالاضطرار وتقرير هذا أن قدرة الكفر التي وجدت في الكافر لو كانت غير صالحة للإيمان ولا تصلح إلا للكفر لا يتمكن الكافر المختص بقدرة الكفر أن ينفك عن قدرة الكفر، وأن يعدل عنه وأن يأتي بالإيمان، وكذا في الجالس مع القيام والمتحرك مع السكون وكل متضادين، وفيه أمران أحدهما : تكليف ما ليس في الوسع، والآخر: ارتفاع الفرق بين المضطر ،والقادر ، والتمييز بين الأفعال الاختيارية والأفعال الاضطرارية، والفرق بينهما معلوم بطريق الاضطرار. فما أدى إلى خلافه كان بطلانه معلومًا بطريق الاضطرار.
And among them: that if capacity were not sufficient for both opposites, there would be assignment of what cannot be borne (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq), as we explained that the disbeliever is commanded to have faith, and if he did not have the power sufficient for faith, there would be assignment of what cannot be borne. Commentary
This passage provides the second and third proofs (adilla) for the claim that capacity is sufficient for both opposites. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a capacity is specific to one act, the agent still has free choice through other capacities.
Correction: The proof shows that if the specific capacity cannot produce the opposite, the act is natural and the agent is compelled regarding that act.

Checkpoint
What two matters result from the claim that capacity is not for both opposites, according to the third proof?
A) Taklif beyond capacity and elimination of distinction between compelled and capable.
B) Taklif beyond capacity and eternal capacity.
C) Elimination of distinction and negation of God's power.
D) Taklif beyond capacity and the impossibility of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
If capacity were not for both opposites, actions would be natural and compelled, contradicting the self-evident distinction between voluntary and compelled acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you ever console yourself with the thought 'I couldn't help it' or 'it was out of my hands'? This lesson argues that if you truly had no capacity to do otherwise, your act would be natural, not voluntary—and you would not be accountable. But deep down, you know the difference between being forced and choosing. If you claim your capacity was fixed, are you not using theology to evade responsibility? How would your repentance change if you fully owned your freedom in that moment?
?
You habitually say 'God willed it' as a way to accept outcomes. But does that belief ever tempt you to treat your own choices as determined, as if you have no real alternative? The author insists the distinction between compelled and free acts is known by necessity. Look back at a recent decision—could you genuinely have chosen otherwise? If the answer is yes, then your disbelief or disobedience was your own. Can you bear the weight of that freedom without fleeing into fatalism?
?
Imagine standing before God on the Day of Judgment and trying to argue: 'My capacity was only for disbelief, so I couldn't believe.' According to this proof, that excuse would collapse because it would mean your disbelief was natural, not chosen—yet you know internally that you were not like fire forced to burn. In what area of your life are you most tempted to view yourself as a passive victim of your own habits or nature? What would it cost you to start seeing those acts as your own genuine choices, and to hold yourself fully accountable for them?

Two Views on Human Capability and Opposites
Two views on capability and opposites with criticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two major positions on whether human capability (qudra) extends to opposite actions, and the author's critique of them.

Core Idea
Capability over a thing does not necessarily extend to all its opposites.

This passage delves into a key debate in kalām: does a single capability (qudra) allow a person to perform opposite actions? The author summarizes two opposing views—the majority position and that of some later scholars—and then dismisses them as stemming from corrupt principles. As you read, note how each view defines the scope of capability and its relationship to opposites.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without getting stuck on unclear phrases. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
The passage starts abruptly; it seems to be a continuation from previous discussion.
2
Identify the two views
Look for the two positions: one attributed to 'the majority of them' (jumhūruhum) and another to 'some of their later scholars' (ba'd muta'akhkhirihim). Notice how each defines the scope of capability.
The first view says capability does not encompass the entire genus of opposites; the second says a single power can do all except one of a genus.
3
Examine the examples given
For the later view, the author gives examples: motion/stillness, gathering/separation, knowledge/ignorance, will. Understand what these pairs represent.
These are pairs of opposites within a genus.
4
Consider the author's criticism
Read the final sentence: 'And beyond that they have odious opinions...'. The author clearly rejects these views. Reflect on why he might call their principles corrupt.
The criticism is brief but shows the author's stance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جل وعلا قادر على أفعال عباده، وأن العبد إذا قدر على جنس من الفعل قدر على ما يقدر عليه ربه من أضداده. وزعم جمهورهم أن قدرة الإنسان على شيء قدرة على أضداد له لا يعم جنس الأضداد، وأن الباري جل وعلا لا يقدر على ما يقدر عليه عبده، ولا العبد يقدر على ما يقدر عليه ربه ، وإن كان مقدور كل واحد منهما ضد مقدوره أو جنسه. وزعم بعض متأخريهم أن القدرة الواحدة قدرة على جميع ما يصح أن يكون يصح أن يفعل بها إلا واحدًا من الجنس، فإذا وجدت له لا مقدورا له غير القدرة قدر بها على الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق والعلم والجهل والإرادة ثم يقول : إنها قدرة على أن يفعل بها من كل جنس ما لا يتناهى على التوالي وليست هي قدرة على أن يفعل بها في الحال شيئين من جنس واحد في محل واحد. ولهم وراء ذلك أقاويل مستشنعة دفعتهم إليها أصولهم الفاسدة.
He, may He be exalted, is powerful over the actions of His servants, and that the servant, when he is capable of a genus of action, is capable of what his Lord is capable of from its opposites. And the majority of them claimed that the human's power over something is power over its opposites that does not encompass the genus of opposites, and that the Creator, may He be exalted, is not powerful over what His servant is powerful over, nor is the servant powerful over what his Lord is powerful over, even if each one's object of power is the opposite of the other's object of power or its genus. And some of their later scholars claimed that a single power is a power over all that can validly be done with it except one of the genus, so when it exists for him, there is no object of power for him other than the power; he is powerful by it over motion and stillness, gathering and separation, knowledge and ignorance, and will. Then he says: It is a power to do with it from every genus what is infinite successively, and it is not a power to do with it at once two things of one genus in one locus. And beyond that they have odious opinions to which their corrupt principles drove them.
Source pages p. 838

Commentary
This passage presents two divergent views on human capability (al-istita'a) and its relationship to opposite actions. The author begins by establishing that God is powerful over His servants' actions, and that when a servant is capable of a genus of action, he is capable of what his Lord is capable of from its opposites—a point of agreement. Then he reports the view of the majority (jumhūruhum): they hold that a human's power over a thing is power over its opposites, but not over the entire genus of opposites. Importantly, they maintain that God is not powerful over what the servant is powerful over, and vice versa, even if each one's object of power is opposite or of the same genus as the other's. Next, the author reports the view of some later scholars (ba'd muta'akhkhirihim): they claim that a single power is power over all that can be done with it except one item of the genus. For example, with one power a person can perform motion and stillness, gathering and separation, knowledge and ignorance, and will—but not two opposite things simultaneously in the same locus. They add that this power can produce an infinite number of acts successively, but not two at once. The author concludes by criticizing these views, stating that they have other odious opinions driven by their corrupt principles.

Key Terms
قدرة
استطاعة
ضد
مقدور
جمهور
متأخريهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human capability is unlimited and can produce any opposite simultaneously.
Correction: Both views restrict capability: the majority denies encompassing the entire genus, and the later scholars deny simultaneous opposite acts in one locus.

Checkpoint
According to the majority view, does human power extend to the entire genus of opposites?
A) Yes, it extends to all opposites.
B) No, it does not encompass the full genus.
C) It extends only to one opposite.
D) It depends on the individual.

Key Takeaway
The early kalām debate on capability and opposites reveals differing attempts to define the scope of human power while preserving God's unique omnipotence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own ability to act, do you ever feel that your power is independent of God's? How does this lesson's critique of excessive human capability challenge that feeling? What step can you take today to acknowledge your complete reliance on Allah in a specific act you plan to do?
?
The lesson shows that some scholars tried to expand human capability to cover almost everything. Are there areas in your life where you act as if your own power is unlimited, forgetting that all ability comes from Allah? What would it look like to sincerely say 'Lā ḥawla wa lā quwwata illā billāh' before your next decision?
?
The author dismisses the later view as leading to 'odious opinions.' Have you ever held a belief about your own ability that, upon honest reflection, conflicts with tawḥīd? What would it mean to surrender that belief today and ask Allah to purify your understanding of His sole control over all things?
