Proofs from Religious Practice That Capability Does Not Apply to Opposites
First three proofs from religious practice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how acts of worship like supplication and seeking refuge demonstrate that the capability for obedience is distinct from the capability for disobedience.

Core Idea
Prayers for help prove we lack both powers; otherwise prayer would be meaningless.

The author begins by presenting proofs rooted in everyday religious practice. Muslims ask Allah for tawfiq and seek refuge from khidhlan, indicating that they recognize the power for obedience is distinct from the power for disobedience. These acts of worship would be pointless if each person had both capabilities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on religious proofs
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the initial claim that the power for obedience is tawfiq and for disobedience is khidhlan.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the proof from supplication
Find the statement that people ask Allah for protection and help. Why would they do that if they already had both powers?
3
Examine the proof from tranquility
The text says that if protection had deviation, the heart would not be at ease. What does this imply about the nature of these powers?
4
Consider the labeling proof
No one calls a disbeliever protected or a believer abandoned. If each had both powers, these descriptions would be arbitrary.
This highlights the asymmetry in divine assistance.
5
Summarize the conclusion
The passage concludes that the power of each type is different. Reflect on how these three proofs reinforce each other.
The conclusion sets up the next set of arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الذين يقولون بأن الاستطاعة لا تصلح للضدين وأدلتهم وأما القائلون إنها لا تصلح للضدين وقوة الطاعة غير قوة المعصية يقولون: إن قوة الطاعة التوفيق، وقوة المعصية الخذلان والترك على ما يختار قالوا دليل ذلك أن الناس يسألون من الله العصمة والمعونة على علم منهم أن ليس معه زيغ، ويسألون التوفيق على الإحاطة أن معه الإصابة، ويقولون: اللهم قوني على طاعتك وأعني على أداء ما افترضت على، ويتعوذون بالله من الخذلان، ولو كان بكل واحد منهما ما يكون الآخر لم يكن الذي يسأل بالسؤال أولى من الذي يتعوذ منه، ولو كان بالعصمة زيغ لم يطمئن القلب عند الوجود، فثبت أن قوة كل نوع غير قوة النوع الآخر، ولأن أحدًا لا يطلق القول بأن الكافر معصوم وأن المؤمن مخذول، ولو كان مع كل واحد منهما ما يصلح للأمرين جميعًا لم يكن أحدهما بالشهادة له بالخذلان والآخر بالشهادة له بالتوفيق والعصمة أولى من القلب أو يوصل كل واحد منهما بأنه موفق مخذول وذلك باطل، ولأن الجمع بين الضدين حال وجود القدرة غير ممكن، وهي لا تبقى ليفعل بها ضدًا في وقت والضد الآخر في وقت آخر، فلا يتصور أن تكون قدرة لهما بل كانت قدرة لما وقع بها وكذلك الزمن والعليل والطفل يقدرون على الزحف من الجبل مع العجز عن الارتقاء فيه وكذا العليل ينحدر من السلم على جهد واستمساك ولا يقدر على الارتقاء فيه وكذلك الصحيح ينزل البئر بالجبل ولا يصح منه الارتقاء عنه، وكذا الحي على تفريق أجزاء نفسه ولا يقدر على جمعها، ويقدر على السبب الذي يحدث عقيبه الانكسار من الزجاج وغيره من الأجسام، ولا يقدر على السبب الذي يحدث عقيبه الانحيار ، وسوى هذا لهم حجج أخر أعرضنا عن ذكرها.
Those who say that capability does not apply to opposites and their proofs. As for those who say it does not apply to opposites and that the power of obedience is not the power of disobedience, they say: The power of obedience is divine success (tawfīq), and the power of disobedience is abandonment and letting alone (khidhlān) according to what He chooses. They said the proof of that is that people ask God for protection and help, knowing that they do not have with them deviation, and they ask for success with certainty that they have correctness, and they say: 'O God, strengthen me for Your obedience and help me to perform what You have obligated upon me,' and they seek refuge in God from abandonment. And if each one had what the other has, the one who asks would not be more deserving of the asking than the one who seeks refuge from it. And if protection had deviation, the heart would not be at ease when it exists. Thus it is established that the power of each type is other than the power of the other type. And because no one says that the disbeliever is protected and the believer is abandoned. And if each one had with it what is suitable for both matters, then one of them would not be more deserving of being attested as abandoned and the other of being attested as successful and protected than the opposite, or each one would be described as both successful and abandoned, and that is false. And because the combination of opposites at the moment of the existence of power is impossible, and it does not remain so that one opposite is done with it at one time and the other opposite at another time. Thus it is inconceivable that it is a power for both; rather, it is power for what occurs with it. Similarly, the elderly, the sick, and the child are able to crawl down the mountain while being unable to ascend it, and likewise the sick person descends the ladder with effort and holding on and is not able to ascend it. Similarly, the healthy person descends into the well with a rope and cannot ascend from it. And likewise, the living person is able to separate the parts of himself and is not able to gather them. He is able to cause the breaking of glass and other bodies, and is not able to cause the [opposite] gathering. And besides this they have other arguments which we have refrained from mentioning.
Source pages p. 839

Commentary
This passage presents the proofs of those who assert that capability (al-istita'a) does not apply to opposites, and that the power for obedience is distinct from the power for disobedience. The author first identifies the proponents: they say the power for obedience is tawfīq (divine success) and the power for disobedience is khidhlān (divine abandonment). They then give several proofs. First proof: People pray to God for protection and help, indicating they recognize their lack of inherent ability; if they possessed both powers, such supplication would be pointless. Second proof: If protection were accompanied by deviation, the heart would not find tranquility; thus the power for obedience and disobedience are different. Third proof: No one calls a disbeliever 'protected' or a believer 'abandoned'; if each had both powers, such descriptions would be arbitrary. Fourth proof: It is impossible to combine opposites at the moment of power's existence, and power does not persist to perform one opposite later; thus power is only for what actually occurs. They provide examples: the elderly and sick can descend but not ascend; a person can descend into a well but not ascend; a person can separate his limbs but not reassemble them; one can cause breakage but not its repair. The author notes that they have additional arguments which he refrains from mentioning.

استطاعة
ضدين
قوة الطاعة
قوة المعصية
توفيق
خذلان
عصمة
معونة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think these are merely linguistic arguments without theological weight.
Correction: These proofs are rooted in actual religious practice and reflect the community's intuitive understanding that divine assistance is specific to each act.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the act of asking Allah for tawfīq prove that capability does not apply to opposites?
A) Because asking shows ignorance.
B) Because if one already had both powers, asking would be pointless.
C) Because only believers ask for help.
D) Because the Prophet commanded it.

Key Takeaway
The religious practices of supplication and seeking refuge confirm that the power for obedience and the power for disobedience are distinct divine grants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray 'O God, strengthen me for Your obedience,' do you truly believe you are powerless without His specific help? How does that belief manifest in your daily struggle against sin?
?
If your power to obey and your power to disobey are distinct divine grants, which one are you actively seeking from Allah right now? What does your supplication say about your heart's direction?
?
You have never – and will never – call a disbeliever 'protected' or a believer 'abandoned'. Yet do you sometimes treat your own obedience as if it came from you and your disobedience as if it were a random failure? How can you align your self-talk with the truth that every power is a distinct gift?

Logical and Empirical Proofs That Capability Does Not Apply to Opposites
Logical argument and empirical examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that combining opposites is impossible and the empirical examples that support the specificity of capability.

Core Idea
Power cannot combine opposites because it only exists for the action that actually occurs.

The author now presents a logical proof and a series of striking examples from common experience. He argues that it is impossible for a single capability to produce two opposite actions because the capability does not persist over time. He then illustrates this with cases like descending vs. ascending, separating vs. gathering, and breaking vs. repairing.

Guided Path
1
Read the logical argument
Read the passage from 'ولأن الجمع بين الضدين' to the end. Focus on the statement that combining opposites is impossible and power does not remain.
2
Understand the logic
The author argues that power exists only at the moment of the act; it cannot be stored to perform the opposite later. Therefore power is for what actually occurs.
This is a key metaphysical point.
3
Examine the examples
Read each example: the elderly descending vs ascending, the sick on a ladder, the healthy in a well, separating vs gathering parts, breaking vs repairing. Note the pattern.
Each example shows one action possible while its opposite is impossible.
4
Recognize the principle
All examples illustrate that capability is specific to a particular action, not general for opposites.
5
Conclude
The author ends by noting there are more arguments. Reflect on how these examples from daily life reinforce the logical proof.

Source Text

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Consider a sin you have fallen into repeatedly. You might have thought you had the ability to avoid it, yet you fell. How does the lesson's claim that power is only for what occurs change your understanding of your own responsibility and need for God's help?
?
If your power is only for the action that actually happens, then your actual deeds define your real capability. Are your daily actions reflecting the person you claim to be? What would it take to align your actions with your highest intentions?

Maturidi Rebuttal: Mu'tazila Actually Hold Taklif Ma La Yutaq
Nasafi turns the accusation of taklif ma la yutaq back onto the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi argument turns the accusation of taklif ma la yutaq back against the Mu'tazila by analyzing their view on power at the time of the act.

Core Idea
If power is absent at the moment of action, every command is impossible to fulfill.

In this passage, the Maturidi theologian Nasafi exposes what he sees as a fatal inconsistency in the Mu'tazilite position on divine command. He argues that their denial of concurrent power with the act leads directly to the very absurdity they accuse others of: commanding the impossible. Let's see how this counter-argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the entire Arabic passage and its English translation without pausing for analysis. This will give you the full scope of the argument.
Notice the rhetorical opening inviting the Mu'tazila to reflect.
2
Contrast the two positions on power
Identify the Maturidi position: power exists at the time of the act. Then the Mu'tazilite position: power is absent at the time of the act.
This contrast is the core of the argument.
3
Examine the two Mu'tazilite sub-arguments
Nasafi presents two scenarios for why power is absent: (a) accidents cannot persist, so power vanishes before the act; (b) even if power persists, it is for a different act, not the current one.
Both lead to the same conclusion: the agent is not capable at the moment of the act.
4
Understand the consequence: taklif ma la yutaq
If the agent has no power at the time of the commanded act, then the command is impossible to fulfill. This is exactly taklif ma la yutaq.
Thus the Mu'tazila are committing what they accuse others of.
5
Reflect on the ironic reversal
Nasafi notes the irony that the Mu'tazila accuse the Maturidis (who affirm power at the time of the act) of holding taklif ma la yutaq, while in fact the Mu'tazila themselves hold it.
This rhetorical move strengthens the Maturidi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنَّا نقول: إن المعتزلة لو نظروا عن بصيرة ورفضوا التعصب جانبًا وأعرضوا عن تقليد أسلافهم لعرفوا أنهم هم القائلون بتكليف ما لا يطاق وليس في الوسع لا نحن، وذلك لما بينا أن عندنا كان الفاعل وقت الفعل قادرًا وكانت القدرة وقتئذ موجودة، ومن زعمهم أن لا قدرة للفاعل على الفعل البتة، إذ هي منعدمة وقت الفعل ولا يتصور وجودها معه، أما على قول من يقول باستحالة بقاء الأعراض فظاهر، وكذا على قول من يقول ببقائها لما بينا أن من زعمهم وإن كانت موجودة كذا الفعل إلا أنها قدرة لغيره من الأفعال ولا يوصف هو بتلك القدرة القائمة للحال أنه قادر على ذلك الفعل بل يوصف بأنه قادر بها على فعل آخر وإنما يوصف بكونه قادرا على هذا الفعل بهذه القدرة قبل وجود الفعل فإنه لا يتصور على قولهم حصول فعل من قادر، ولا قادر يتصور منه الفعل، فكان كل مأمور بفعل مأمورًا بما هو غير قادر عليه وعندنا كل مأمور بما هو قادر عليه ومن أعجب الأقاويل قول من ينسب القائل بأنه كلف ما له عليه القدرة إلى القول بتكليف ما لا يطاق وينسب القائل أنه كلف ما ليس هو بقادر عليه إلى القول
Then we say: If the Mu'tazila looked with insight and abandoned partisanship and turned away from the imitation of their predecessors, they would know that they are the ones who say that what cannot be borne is obligated, and not we. That is because we hold that the agent at the time of the act is capable and the power at that time exists, while according to their claim, there is no power for the agent over the act at all, since it is non-existent at the time of the act and its existence with it is inconceivable. This is obvious according to the one who says that the persistence of accidents is impossible, and likewise according to the one who says they persist, because even if it exists along with the act, it is a power for other acts, and he is not described by that power subsisting at the moment as being capable of that act; rather, he is described as being capable by it of another act. He is only described as being capable of this act by this power before the existence of the act. So according to their view, no act occurs from a capable agent, nor is there any capable agent from whom an act can be conceived. Thus, every commanded person is commanded with something he is not capable of, while according to us, every commanded person is commanded with something he is capable of. And among the strangest sayings is the statement of one who attributes the saying of one who says that he was obligated with what he had power over to the saying of taklif ma la yutaq, and attributes the saying of one who says that he was obligated with what he is not capable of to the saying...
Source pages p. 841

Commentary
This passage is a Maturidi rebuttal turning the accusation of taklif ma la yutaq back against the Mu'tazila. The author (Nasafi) argues that if the Mu'tazila examined their own doctrine honestly, they would see that they are the ones who actually commit to the impossibility of taklif. The reasoning is as follows: The Maturidis affirm that the agent (fā'il) has power (qudra) at the very moment of the act; thus, the command is for something within capacity. The Mu'tazila, on the other hand, deny that power coexists with the act—either because they hold that accidents cannot persist, so power vanishes before the act, or because even if it persists, it is power for a different act and not for the present one. Consequently, in their system, no agent can truly be described as 'capable' (qādir) of the act at the moment of performance. Hence, every command given to a person is for something they are not capable of at that moment—which is precisely taklif ma la yutaq. The author then ridicules the irony: some (i.e., the Mu'tazila) accuse the Maturidis (who say the obligated person has power over the act) of holding taklif ma la yutaq, while they themselves (the Mu'tazila) actually oblige what is beyond capacity. The passage ends abruptly, but the point is clear.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Maturidis hold that power must exist before the act, but here they affirm power at the time of act.
Correction: The Maturidis hold that power coexists with the act, not before it. The Mu'tazila deny this coexistence, leading to taklif ma la yutaq.

Checkpoint
According to this Maturidi argument, why does the Mu'tazilite position lead to taklif ma la yutaq?
A) Because they believe power is created by God after the act.
B) Because they deny that power exists at the moment of the act.
C) Because they require the act to be meritorious.
D) Because they deny God's omnipotence.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi argument demonstrates that denying power at the moment of the act logically entails that every divine command is a command for the impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you quick to accuse others of a fault that you yourself unknowingly commit? When you look at the Mu'tazila being caught in their own argument, what blind spots in your own life might you be ignoring? Take a moment to ask Allah for insight before judging anyone.
?
This lesson shows that denying human power at the moment of action makes God's commands impossible. Do you live as if you believe you have no real power over your own actions, excusing sin as 'destiny'? Or do you claim full power without need of Allah's giving? Which extreme is closer to your heart, and what will you change today to acknowledge that your ability is from Allah every moment?
?
Nasafi says the Mu'tazila would know the truth if they abandoned partisanship and imitation. Is there a truth you avoid because it would humiliate your group or tradition? Are you willing to be corrected by the Quran and Sunnah even if it comes from someone you dislike?

The Mu'tazila are the true proponents of taklif ma la yutaq
Nasafi argues that the Mu'tazila are the true proponents of 'taklif ma la yutaq' and exposes the absurdity of their accusation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi claims the Mu'tazila are the ones who affirm 'taklif ma la yutaq' based on their view of power.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's own theory of power proves they believe in obligating the impossible.

In this passage, Nasafi turns the Mu'tazila's accusation back on them. He argues that their theory of power (qudra) leads to the very doctrine they condemn. By examining the nature of power at the time of action, Nasafi shows that the Mu'tazila's position entails that every commanded act is beyond the agent's capacity. This is a classic example of refutation by internal critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the full English translation. Let Nasafi's argument unfold without interruption.
Pay attention to the logical flow: claim, proof, conclusion.
2
Identify the main claim
Nasafi's main claim is that the Mu'tazila—not the Maturidis—are the actual proponents of 'taklif ma la yutaq'. Find where he states this explicitly.
The claim appears at the start and is reinforced at the end.
3
Analyze the argument about power
Nasafi argues that the Mu'tazila's view of power (that it does not exist at the time of action) leads to the conclusion that every commanded act is beyond the agent's capacity. Trace the steps of this reasoning.
He contrasts the Maturidi view (power exists at time of act) with the Mu'tazili view.
4
Reflect on the final proverb
The proverb 'She threw me with her own affliction and slipped away' illustrates that the Mu'tazila project their own fault onto others. Consider how this sums up Nasafi's argument.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنَّا نقول: إن المعتزلة لو نظروا عن بصيرة ورفضوا التعصب جانبًا وأعرضوا عن تقليد أسلافهم لعرفوا أنهم هم القائلون بتكليف ما لا يطاق وليس في الوسع لا نحن، وذلك لما بينا أن عندنا كان الفاعل وقت الفعل قادرًا وكانت القدرة وقتئذ موجودة، ومن زعمهم أن لا قدرة للفاعل على الفعل البتة، إذ هي منعدمة وقت الفعل ولا يتصور وجودها معه، أما على قول من يقول باستحالة بقاء الأعراض فظاهر، وكذا على قول من يقول ببقائها لما بينا أن من زعمهم وإن كانت موجودة كذا الفعل إلا أنها قدرة لغيره من الأفعال ولا يوصف هو بتلك القدرة القائمة للحال أنه قادر على ذلك الفعل بل يوصف بأنه قادر بها على فعل آخر وإنما يوصف بكونه قادرا على هذا الفعل بهذه القدرة قبل وجود الفعل فإنه لا يتصور على قولهم حصول فعل من قادر، ولا قادر يتصور منه الفعل، فكان كل مأمور بفعل مأمورًا بما هو غير قادر عليه وعندنا كل مأمور بما هو قادر عليه ومن أعجب الأقاويل قول من ينسب القائل بأنه كلف ما له عليه القدرة إلى القول بتكليف ما لا يطاق وينسب القائل أنه كلف ما ليس هو بقادر عليه إلى القول بتكليف ما يطاق ولو لم يكن هذا وقاحة أو حماقة فلا وجود لهما إذا، فظهر بهذا أنهم هم القائلون بالجبر المعتقدون بتكليف ما لا يطاق، فتبرؤهم عن ذلك ونسبة خصومهم إلى ذلك جلوس تحت المثل السائر رمتني بدائها وانسلت
Then we say: If the Mu'tazila had looked with insight and abandoned partisanship and turned away from the imitation of their forefathers, they would know that they are the ones who say [obligation of] what is not endured and beyond capacity—not we. That is because we have shown that, according to us, the agent is capable at the time of the act and power exists at that time. But according to their claim, the agent has no power over the act at all, since it is non-existent at the time of the act and its existence with it is inconceivable. As for the one who says it is impossible for accidents to endure, it is clear; likewise, according to the one who says they endure, because we have shown that according to their claim, even if power exists like the act, it is power for other acts, and he is not described by that power subsisting at the moment as being capable of that act; rather, he is described as capable of another act by it. He is only described as being capable of this act by this power before the existence of the act. For according to their statement, the occurrence of an act from a capable agent is inconceivable, and no capable agent from whom an act is conceivable exists. Thus every one commanded to do an act is commanded to do what he is not capable of, while according to us, everyone commanded is commanded to do what he is capable of. Among the most astonishing sayings is the saying of one who attributes to the speaker that he obligated what he had power over as the statement of obligating the unendurable, and attributes to the speaker that he obligated what he is not capable of as the statement of obligating the endurable. If this is not insolence or stupidity, then both have no existence. Thus it becomes clear that they are the proponents of predestination who believe in obligating the unendurable. Their disavowal of that and their attribution of it to their opponents is sitting under the proverbial saying, 'She threw me with her own affliction and slipped away.'
Source pages p. 841

Commentary
This passage is a polemical argument in which Nasafi turns the tables on the Mu'tazila, claiming that they—not the Maturidis—are the true proponents of 'taklif ma la yutaq' (obligation of the impossible). He begins by stating that if the Mu'tazila would examine the matter with insight and abandon partisanship and blind imitation of their predecessors, they would realize that they themselves hold this position. The proof is based on the nature of power (qudra). According to the Maturidis, the agent has power at the time of the act. But according to the Mu'tazila, power does not exist at the time of the act; it is either non-existent (if accidents cannot endure) or if it does exist, it is not a power for that specific act but for another. Consequently, the agent is never truly capable of the act he performs; he is commanded to do what he cannot do. Thus the Mu'tazila's own theory implies that every commanded act is beyond the agent's capacity. Nasafi then highlights the absurdity of the Mu'tazila's accusation: they accuse the Maturidis of holding that God obligates what is beyond capacity, while the Maturidis say God obligates only what is within capacity. In reality, it is the Mu'tazila who hold the objectionable doctrine, and their attempt to disavow it and attribute it to others is like the proverb, 'She threw me with her own affliction and slipped away'—they project their own fault onto others.

قدرة
معتزلة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maturidis are the ones who believe that God can obligate what is beyond human capacity (taklif ma la yutaq).
Correction: Nasafi argues that it is the Mu'tazila, due to their theory that power does not exist at the time of action, who logically end up holding that every command is for something the agent cannot do—thus they are the real proponents of taklif ma la yutaq.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why are the Mu'tazila actually the ones who affirm 'taklif ma la yutaq'?
A) Because they deny that God has power over actions.
B) Because they claim that power does not exist at the time of action, so the agent is commanded to do what he has no power over.
C) Because they believe in free will.
D) Because they deny the existence of God.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's argument reveals that the Mu'tazila's own premises lead to the conclusion that they believe in obligating the impossible, not the Maturidis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Nasafi says the Mu'tazila would 'abandon partisanship' if they looked with insight. Where in your life have you clung to a viewpoint out of group loyalty or habit, refusing to examine it honestly? Will you today ask Allah to show you the truth even if it means abandoning a cherished position?

The Mu'tazila accused others of a fault that was actually their own. Think of a recent argument where you blamed someone else. Could the fault you saw in them actually be a reflection of your own heart? Are you ready to repent of that projection and seek forgiveness?

The Distinction Between Being Prevented from Power and Wasting It
Nasafi addresses an objection and illustrates with an analogy about a servant and an axe.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila objection that a disbeliever cannot have power for faith by distinguishing between being prevented from power and wasting power, using a detailed analogy.

Core Idea
Lack of power excused; wasting power punished.

This passage tackles a key objection from the Mu'tazila: if power cannot extend to opposites, then a disbeliever has no power for faith, making faith impossible for him. Nasafi responds with a crucial distinction and a vivid analogy. Let's see how he defends the Maturidi position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the full English translation. Get a feel for the argument and the analogy.
The passage is continuous; notice the flow from objection to response to analogy.
2
Identify the two scenarios in the analogy
Nasafi presents two cases of a servant ordered to carve wood. In the first, the adze is locked away; in the second, the servant knows where the key is and can obtain it. Contrast the outcomes.
The first scenario results in excuse, the second in punishment.
3
Understand the key distinction
The difference is between being prevented from having power (excused) and wasting the power one could have acquired (blameworthy). Apply this to the disbeliever: he is not prevented but wastes his power by occupying himself with disbelief.
The Mu'tazila objection collapses because it confuses prevention with wastefulness.
4
Apply the analogy to the disbeliever's situation
Just as the servant could have fetched the adze but didn't, the disbeliever has sound limbs and means but turns away from faith. God gives power to those who intend an act; the disbeliever's lack of power is his own doing.
This shows that the obligation of faith is not impossible; the disbeliever is accountable for his wastefulness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقى أن على قول من يقول : إن القدرة لا تصلح للضدين لم تكن مع الكافر في حالة كفره قدرة الإيمان
فنقول : انعدام قدرة الإيمان كان بتضييعه القدرة لاشتغاله بضده لا يمنع القدرة عنه، ومن منع عنه القدرة فهو معذور، فأما من ضيع القدرة فهو غير معذور بل يعاقب، فإن من أمر عبده بنحت خشب ثم غاب عن العهد ثم جاء والخشب غير منحوت فلامه على ترك النحت فقال العبد : إن القدوم كان في هذا البيت وبابه مقفل، فلم أتمكن من استخراجه والنحت به عذر العبد ولو لم يعذره سيده نسب إلى السفه، ويمثله لو كان قال له: إن القدوم في البيت ومفتاحه في موضع كذا فاذهب وخذ المفتاح وافتح الباب وأخرج القدوم وانحت به ثم غاب السيد ثم حضر ووجد الخشب غير منحوت فلام العبد، فاعتل بأن القدوم في البيت ولم أستخرجه ولم أتمكن من نحته بلا قدوم، كان للمولى أن يعاقبه على ترك النحت، وإن كان لا قدرة له عليه بدون القدوم إلا أنه تمكن من تحصيله وحيث لم يحصله وبقي على العجز كان ذلك بتضييعه القدرة مع تمكنه من تحصيلها، فكذا فيما نحن فيه أجرى الله تعالى العادة أن من كانت له أعضاء صحيحة وأسباب سليمة لو قصد فعلاً أعطاه قدرة ذلك الفعل، وإنما لم يعطه قدرة فعل لإعراضه عنه مضيعًا للقدرة.
It remains that according to the one who says that power does not extend to opposites, there would not be with the disbeliever in the state of his disbelief the power for faith. We say: The non-existence of the power for faith was due to his wasting the power by occupying himself with its opposite; that does not prevent power from him. Whoever is prevented from power is excused, but as for the one who wastes power, he is not excused; rather, he is punished. For if a master ordered his servant to carve wood, then was absent from the appointment, then came and the wood was not carved, and he blamed him for leaving the carving, and the servant said: 'The adze was in this house and its door was locked, so I could not extract it and carve with it,' the servant is excused, and if his master did not excuse him, he would be considered foolish. The example is that if he had said to him: 'The adze is in the house and its key is in such a place; go take the key, open the door, bring out the adze, and carve with it,' then the master was absent, then came and found the wood not carved, and he blamed the servant, and the servant excused himself by saying: 'The adze was in the house but I did not extract it and could not carve without an adze,' the master would have the right to punish him for leaving the carving. Even though he has no power over it without the adze, he was able to acquire it, and since he did not acquire it and remained in incapacity, that was due to his wasting the power while being able to acquire it. Similarly in our case, God Almighty has established the custom that whoever has sound limbs and sound means, if he intends to do an act, He gives him the power for that act. He only does not give him the power for the act because of his turning away from it, wasting the power.
Source pages p. 842

Commentary
This passage answers an objection brought by the Mu'tazila: if power does not extend to opposites (i.e., one cannot have power for faith and disbelief simultaneously), then the disbeliever, while in disbelief, has no power for faith. Nasafi responds by making a crucial distinction between being prevented from power and wasting power. The disbeliever does not lack all power; rather, he wastes the power he has by occupying himself with the opposite (disbelief). If a person is prevented from having power (e.g., locked door), he is excused. But if he wastes the power he could have acquired, he is not excused. Nasafi illustrates this with a detailed analogy: A master orders his servant to carve wood. In one scenario, the servant cannot access the adze because the door is locked—he is excused. In another scenario, the master tells the servant where the key and adze are, and the servant still fails to carve because he didn't fetch the adze—he deserves punishment. Even though he had no power to carve without the adze, he had the ability to acquire it, and his failure to do so is blameworthy. Analogously, God has established the custom that He gives power for an act to those who intend it and have sound means. If a person turns away from faith and does not seek the power, he is blameworthy. Thus the objection is refuted: the disbeliever's lack of power for faith is due to his own wastefulness, not because God imposed an impossible obligation.

Key Terms
qudra
taklif
'ajz
tadyi' (wasting)
mu'tazila

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any lack of power automatically excuses a person from obligation.
Correction: The passage shows that if the power could have been acquired through one's own effort and was not, the person is accountable. Only being prevented from power (e.g., locked door) excuses.

Checkpoint
What is the point of the analogy of the servant and the axe in this passage?
A) That anyone denied a tool is excused.
B) That if the servant could have obtained the tool but did not, he is blameworthy.
C) That God does not give power to sinners.
D) That the Mu'tazila are wrong about everything.

Key Takeaway
God's custom is to give power for an act to those who intend it and have sound means; turning away from faith wastes that power, making the disbeliever blameworthy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there acts of obedience you have neglected, knowing full well that you had the means to prepare for them—like a one-time opportunity to pray on time, a charity you could have given, or a word of truth you could have spoken? What excuse will you offer to your Master?
?
When you find yourself spiritually weak or unable to do good, do you first ask yourself whether you wasted the very power God had placed within your reach by busying yourself with its opposite?
?
You know that God does not wrong anyone—so if you fail to have faith or to act, who is really to blame after reading this passage? Will you now trace every spiritual failure back to your own turning away, or will you continue to shift the responsibility?

Maturidi Response: Why the Disbeliever Remains Obligated
The author answers the Muhaqqiqun's objection about capability and obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi argument that a disbeliever who has lost the capability for faith due to his own choice is still obligated, unlike a person with external paralysis.

Core Idea
Choice to waste capability forfeits excuse, unlike external paralysis.

In this passage, the author responds to an objection that since a single capability cannot apply to opposites, a disbeliever lacks capability for faith and should be excused. The author argues that the disbeliever's own choice caused the loss, so he remains accountable. This clarifies a key Maturidi principle: obligation hinges on sound means, not on actual capability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation once to get the overall flow of the argument.
Note the parallel drawn between the disbeliever and a paralytic.
2
Identify the objection
Spot the objection from the Muhaqqiqun: since capability is not for opposites, the disbeliever cannot have faith capability, so why obligate him?
The objection uses the analogy of paralysis to claim it is obligating the impossible.
3
Understand the author's distinction
The author distinguishes: in paralysis, the preventer is external, so the person is excused. In disbelief, the person himself caused the loss through his choice, so he remains obligated.
Key phrase: 'by that preventer, [the person] was prevented from capability, and by these meanings he was wasting capability'.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The Maturidi rule: obligation revolves around the soundness of instruments and means, not the reality of capability.
This explains why the disbeliever is accountable despite lacking actual capability for faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يجاب عن تمويه محققيهم : أن القدرة لما لم تصلح للضدين، وقدرة الكفر لا يتصور حصول الإيمان بها، فكان قيام قدرة الكفر وقيام نفس الكفر ووجود إرادة الكفر من هذا القادر مانعة من وجود قدرة الإيمان كوجود الزمانة والشلل في اليد يكون مانعا من وجود قدرة البطش. ثم التكليف مع الشلل تكليف ما لا يطاق فكذا هذا بل أولى، لأن المانع هناك ثلاثة أشياء: وهي قدرة الكفر وإرادته ونفسه. فإنا نقول : بذلك المانع كان ممنوع القدرة ، وبهذه المعاني كان مضيعًا القدرة، فكان ذلك معذوراً ولم يكلف، وبقي هذا مكلفا ولم يقدر على ما ذكرت في المثال. وكما بينت أن في حال وجود الكفر يستحيل وجود الإيمان، وعندهم يؤخذ بذلك الكفر لحصوله باختياره وفوات القدرة بتضييعه وتفويته، ثم صار الحاصل أن لأجل هذه العلة صار التكليف عندنا دائرًا مع صحة الآلات والأسباب دون حقيقة القدرة.
And with this, the confusion of their investigators is answered: that since capability (qudra) is not suited for opposites, and the capability of disbelief cannot be imagined to attain faith by it, so the existence of the capability of disbelief, the existence of disbelief itself, and the existence of the will of disbelief from this capable one prevent the existence of the capability of faith, just as the existence of chronic illness and paralysis in the hand prevents the existence of the capability of striking. Then obligation (taklif) with paralysis is an obligation of what is not possible, so likewise this, rather more so, because the preventer there are three things: the capability of disbelief, its will, and itself. For we say: by that preventer, [the person] was prevented from capability, and by these meanings he was wasting capability, so that one was excused and not obligated, while this one remains obligated and not capable of what you mentioned in the example. And as I have explained that in the state of the existence of disbelief, the existence of faith is impossible, and according to them, he is taken to account for that disbelief because it occurred by his choice and the loss of capability by his wasting and losing it. Then the result is that for this reason, obligation according to us revolves around the soundness of instruments and means, not the reality of capability.
Source pages p. 843

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection raised by the investigators (muhaqqiqun) of the Mu'tazila or perhaps others. The objection is that since a single capability (qudra) cannot apply to two opposites (like faith and disbelief), and the capability for disbelief cannot produce faith, then the existence of the capability for disbelief, the actual disbelief, and the will for disbelief prevent the existence of the capability for faith, similar to how a paralyzed hand cannot have the capability to strike. The objector then claims that obligating such a person is like obligating the impossible. The author answers that in the example of paralysis, the person is excused because the preventer is external, but in the case of disbelief, the person himself caused the loss of capability by his choice, so he remains obligated. The conclusion is that for the Maturidis, obligation depends on the soundness of means and instruments, not on the actual existence of capability.

Key Terms
قدرة
تكليف
ضدان
شلل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maturidi position obligates the impossible.
Correction: The author argues that the disbeliever is not obligated to do the impossible because he himself caused the loss of capability. Obligation is based on the soundness of means at the time of the command, not on the actual presence of capability after it has been wasted.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the disbeliever not excused from obligation like a paralytic?
A) Because the disbeliever still has capability for faith
B) Because the disbeliever chose to waste his capability
C) Because the disbeliever's capability is not prevented
D) Because the disbeliever is not really incapable

Key Takeaway
The Maturidis hold that obligation depends on the soundness of means, not on the actual possession of capability, and a person who wastes his capability by choice remains accountable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you used your own choices as an excuse to avoid a difficult obligation, convincing yourself that you 'can't' when really you 'won't'? What spiritual duty have you been telling yourself is impossible, when actually you are just choosing to waste your capability?
?
The passage shows that the disbeliever's prevention is self-caused. Think of an area in your life where you have allowed your own desires, intentions, or past actions to create a barrier between you and obedience. Is that barrier real or chosen? What would it take for you to stop treating it as paralysis and start owning it as a wasted capability that you must recover?
?
If obligation depends on the soundness of means, not on your current possession of capability, what does that mean for the excuses you make today? Are you going to continue pretending you are a paralytic, or will you accept that you are responsible for the capability you have wasted and begin to act?

The Maturidi Condition for Taklif vs. Other Schools
The author compares the Maturidi, Mu'tazili, and Ash'ari conditions for taklif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position that the condition for valid obligation is soundness of instruments, not actual capability, and how it differs from the Mu'tazila and Ash'aris.

Core Idea
Sound instruments, not actual power, make an obligation valid.

The author begins by stating the Maturidi position on the condition for taklif (religious obligation). He contrasts it with the Mu'tazila and Ash'aris, showing that each school has a different requirement. This lesson unpacks these positions and clarifies the Maturidi stance based on rational proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the first part of the passage
Read from the beginning up to 'دلائلال معقولة' and its translation.
Focus on the three positions being compared.
2
Identify each school's view
Note what the Mu'tazila, Ash'aris/Jabriyya, and Maturidis stipulate for taklif.
The author clearly states each.
3
Understand the Maturidi distinction
The Maturidis require soundness of causes and instruments (salāmat al-asbāb wa al-ālāt) but not the reality of capability (ḥaqīqat al-qudra).
This is the key difference.
4
Reflect on the rational proofs mentioned
The author refers to rational proofs that have passed (presumably earlier in the text). Contemplate why sound instruments are sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الابد لصحة التكليف من سلامة الآلات خلافًا للمعتزلة والأشاعرة. والمعتزلة يشترطون لصحة التكليف حقيقة القدرة، ويموهون أن التكليف بدونها تكليف ما ليس في الوسع، ثم هم القائلون به في التحقيق دون خصومهم. والأشعرية والجبرية لا يشترطون لصحة التكليف لا استطاعة الأسباب والآلات ولا حقيقة الاستطاعة، فألحقت المعتزلة حقيقة القدرة بقدرة سلامة الأسباب لاشتراط التكليف، وألحقت الجبرية استطاعة سلامة الأسباب بحقيقة الاستطاعة في ترك الاشتراط للتكليف، وأصحابنا اشترطوا لذلك سلامة الأسباب والآلات ولم يشترطوا حقيقة القدرة، والصحيح ما ذهبنا إليه لما مر من الدلائل المعقولة، وقد ورد كتاب الله تعالى بتقرير ذلك، فإنه تعالى خاطب من ببخارى - عند وجود الزاد والراحلة وتوفر الأسباب والآلات - بأداء الحج وأوجب عليه ذلك، وإن كانت قدرة أداء أفعال الحج منعدمة ببخارى، وأوجب - عند عموم التغير وقصور من بالثغور من أهل الإسلام عن مقاومة من بإزائهم من الكفرة وعجزهم عن الذب عن البيضة وحماية الحوزة - على من بأطراف دار الإسلام من المسلمين الذين بهم وكفاية، جهاد أولئك الأعداء وذبهم عن حريم دار الإسلام، وإن كانت قدرة مجاهدة العدو بأقصى الروم والهند منعدمة. ولا شك أن الحج والجهاد يجبان أولاً، ثم لما لم يتمكن من أدائهما إلا بقطع المسافة وجب عليه ذلك. والخروج على ما هو الأصل أن الأمر بالفعل أمر به وبما لم يتوصل إلى أدائه إلا به، ولا وجه إلى القول بأنه يجب عليه الخروج وقطع المسافة ليحصل له قدرة الأداء ثم يجب الأداء حينئذ لأن إيجاب تحصيل شرائط الأداء ثابت لما هو من ضرورة الخروج عن الواجب، فأما إيجاب تحصيل أسباب الوجوب أو شرائطه فغير ثابت البتة لما في الأول لاتباع التابع المتبوع وفي الثاني اتباع المتبوع التابع، وهو قلب المعقول – يدل عليه أن من وجد الزاد والراحلة ولم يخرج إلى الحج حتى مات يأثم إثم ترك الحج بإجماع الأمة لا إثم ترك الخروج وصاحب موسى عليه السلام عاتبه عند ترك الصبر مع قوله: {إِنَّكَ لَن تَسْتَطِيعَ مَعِيَ صَبْرًا} [الكهف] لما أن أسباب الصبر وآلاته كانت ثابتة، وإنما المنعدم حقيقة استطاعة الصبر، دل أن الخطاب يعتمد الاستطاعة الأولى لا الثانية.
The bottom line for the soundness of obligation (taklif) is the soundness of instruments, contrary to the Mu'tazila and the Ash'aris. The Mu'tazila stipulate for the soundness of obligation the reality of capability (ḥaqīqat al-qudra), and they delude that obligation without it is an obligation of what is not within capability, yet they are the ones who affirm it in reality, not their opponents. The Ash'aris and the Jabriyya do not stipulate for the soundness of obligation either the capability of causes and instruments or the reality of capability. So the Mu'tazila attached the reality of capability to the capability of soundness of causes for the condition of obligation, and the Jabriyya attached the capability of soundness of causes to the reality of capability in abandoning the condition for obligation. Our Companions (aṣḥābunā) stipulated for that the soundness of causes and instruments and did not stipulate the reality of capability. The correct is what we have gone to, due to the rational proofs that have passed. And the Book of Allah Almighty has come confirming that: indeed, He addressed the person in Bukhara – when provisions and mount were present, and causes and instruments were available – to perform the Hajj and made it obligatory upon him, even though the capability to perform the actions of Hajj was non-existent in Bukhara. And He made obligatory – given the general change and the insufficiency of those at the frontiers of Islam to resist the disbelievers facing them, and their inability to defend the egg (of Islam) and protect the territory – upon those at the borders of the abode of Islam among the Muslims who are sufficient for it, the jihad against those enemies and their defense of the sanctuary of the abode of Islam, even though the capability to wage jihad against the enemy at the farthest reaches of Rome and India was non-existent. There is no doubt that Hajj and jihad are obligatory first, then when one cannot perform them except by traversing the distance, that becomes obligatory upon him. And the principle is that the command to do an act is a command to do what is necessary to accomplish it. There is no basis for saying that he must travel and traverse the distance to acquire capability for performance, then performance becomes obligatory at that point, because making acquisition of conditions of performance obligatory is established as a necessary consequence of exiting from the obligatory. As for making acquisition of causes or conditions of obligation obligatory, it is not established at all, because in the first [case] it is following the follower of the followed, and in the second it is following the followed of the follower, which is an inversion of reason. Evidence for this: that whoever has found provisions and mount and did not go to Hajj until he died, he is sinful with the sin of abandoning Hajj by consensus of the ummah, not the sin of abandoning travel. And the companion of Moses (peace be upon him) was reproached by him when he left patience, with His statement: 'Indeed, you will never be able to bear with me patience,' because the causes and instruments of patience were present, and what was absent was only the reality of the capability of patience. This indicates that the address relies on the first capability (istiṭāʿa), not the second.
Source pages p. 843

Commentary
This passage is a comparative doctrinal exposition and proof. The author states the Maturidi position that the condition for valid obligation (taklif) is the soundness of instruments and means, not the actual existence of capability (qudra). He contrasts this with the Mu'tazila, who require actual capability, and the Ash'aris and Jabriyya, who require no capability at all. He then provides a scriptural proof from the obligations of Hajj and jihad, where people are obligated despite lacking actual capability at their location. He further explains the principle that commanding an act includes commanding its prerequisites. He refutes the idea that one must first acquire capability before the act becomes obligatory, because that would lead to absurdity. He supports this with the consensus that a person who has means for Hajj but doesn't go is sinful for abandoning Hajj, not for abandoning travel. And he uses the story of Moses and Khidr to show that the obligation is based on the availability of means (first capability), not the actual capability (second capability).

Key Terms
تكليف (taklif)
قدرة (qudra)
استطاعة (istiṭāʿa)
آلات (instruments)
أسباب (causes)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Maturidis deny any role for capability in obligation.
Correction: They actually require the availability of external means, not the internal power.

Checkpoint
What do the Maturidis stipulate for the soundness of taklif?
A) The reality of capability
B) Soundness of instruments only
C) Neither instruments nor capability
D) Both instruments and capability

Key Takeaway
For the Maturidis, the condition for a valid obligation is the presence of external means, not the actual ability to perform the act at that moment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused yourself from an act of worship by saying 'I can't' when, in truth, the means (time, health, resources) were present? What would this lesson say about that excuse?
?
The Mu'tazila demand actual capability before obligation; the Ash'aris demand none. Which of these extremes do you unconsciously lean toward when evaluating your own responsibilities before Allah? How can you correct that?
?
When you face a command of Allah that feels difficult, do you immediately assume it is beyond your ability, or do you look for the instruments Allah has already given you? What would change in your life if you consistently acted on the latter?

Quranic Evidence from Hajj and Jihad for Taklif's Condition
The author provides Quranic evidence and rational argument for the Maturidi condition for taklif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the obligations of Hajj and Jihad demonstrate that obligation is based on the availability of means, not on actual capability.

Core Idea
Hajj and Jihad are obligatory even when actual ability is absent, proving means are what count.

After presenting the Maturidi position, the author provides scriptural evidence from the Quranic obligations of Hajj and Jihad. He shows that Allah commands these acts even when the person lacks the actual capability at their location, establishing that the condition is sound instruments. This lesson unpacks this argument and the subsequent principle that a command to an act includes its prerequisites.

Guided Path
1
Read the scriptural proof
Read from 'وقد ورد كتاب الله تعالى' to the end of the passage, along with the translation.
Focus on the two examples: the person in Bukhara for Hajj, and the Muslims at frontiers for Jihad.
2
Identify the principle behind the examples
The author says that Hajj and Jihad are obligatory first, then travel becomes obligatory as a prerequisite.
This shows that the initial obligation relies on means availability.
3
Understand the refutation of the opposing view
The author rejects the idea that one must first acquire capability before the act becomes obligatory.
He explains why that would be illogical.
4
Examine the story of Moses and Khidr
The story is used to show that the address relies on the first capability (means), not the second (actual).
Khidr was reproached for leaving patience due to lack of actual capability, but the means were there.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الابد لصحة التكليف من سلامة الآلات خلافًا للمعتزلة والأشاعرة. والمعتزلة يشترطون لصحة التكليف حقيقة القدرة، ويموهون أن التكليف بدونها تكليف ما ليس في الوسع، ثم هم القائلون به في التحقيق دون خصومهم. والأشعرية والجبرية لا يشترطون لصحة التكليف لا استطاعة الأسباب والآلات ولا حقيقة الاستطاعة، فألحقت المعتزلة حقيقة القدرة بقدرة سلامة الأسباب لاشتراط التكليف، وألحقت الجبرية استطاعة سلامة الأسباب بحقيقة الاستطاعة في ترك الاشتراط للتكليف، وأصحابنا اشترطوا لذلك سلامة الأسباب والآلات ولم يشترطوا حقيقة القدرة، والصحيح ما ذهبنا إليه لما مر من الدلائل المعقولة، وقد ورد كتاب الله تعالى بتقرير ذلك، فإنه تعالى خاطب من ببخارى - عند وجود الزاد والراحلة وتوفر الأسباب والآلات - بأداء الحج وأوجب عليه ذلك، وإن كانت قدرة أداء أفعال الحج منعدمة ببخارى، وأوجب - عند عموم التغير وقصور من بالثغور من أهل الإسلام عن مقاومة من بإزائهم من الكفرة وعجزهم عن الذب عن البيضة وحماية الحوزة - على من بأطراف دار الإسلام من المسلمين الذين بهم وكفاية، جهاد أولئك الأعداء وذبهم عن حريم دار الإسلام، وإن كانت قدرة مجاهدة العدو بأقصى الروم والهند منعدمة. ولا شك أن الحج والجهاد يجبان أولاً، ثم لما لم يتمكن من أدائهما إلا بقطع المسافة وجب عليه ذلك. والخروج على ما هو الأصل أن الأمر بالفعل أمر به وبما لم يتوصل إلى أدائه إلا به، ولا وجه إلى القول بأنه يجب عليه الخروج وقطع المسافة ليحصل له قدرة الأداء ثم يجب الأداء حينئذ لأن إيجاب تحصيل شرائط الأداء ثابت لما هو من ضرورة الخروج عن الواجب، فأما إيجاب تحصيل أسباب الوجوب أو شرائطه فغير ثابت البتة لما في الأول لاتباع التابع المتبوع وفي الثاني اتباع المتبوع التابع، وهو قلب المعقول – يدل عليه أن من وجد الزاد والراحلة ولم يخرج إلى الحج حتى مات يأثم إثم ترك الحج بإجماع الأمة لا إثم ترك الخروج وصاحب موسى عليه السلام عاتبه عند ترك الصبر مع قوله: {إِنَّكَ لَن تَسْتَطِيعَ مَعِيَ صَبْرًا} [الكهف] لما أن أسباب الصبر وآلاته كانت ثابتة، وإنما المنعدم حقيقة استطاعة الصبر، دل أن الخطاب يعتمد الاستطاعة الأولى لا الثانية.

Misconception: Some might think that the Maturidis ignore actual ability altogether and consider obligation regardless of feasibility.

What does the author use as evidence to show that obligation relies on the first capability (istiṭāʿa) and not the second?

?

?
The story of Moses and Khidr shows that having the external means (like a ship) does not guarantee the internal capability (patience). Is there an area in your life where you rely on outward resources while neglecting the inner strength needed? What step will you take today to build that inner capability?
?
If you were the person in Bukhara with provisions and a mount, but you stayed home because you felt unable to actually perform Hajj from there, would you be sinful? This lesson says yes. How does this change your view of your own excuses? What obligation are you avoiding right now that you actually have the means to fulfill?

Al-Maturidi's Refutation of al-Ka'bi on Impossible Obligation
Al-Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi on impossible obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Maturidi's refutation of al-Ka'bi's claim that impossible obligation is rationally evil, and the distinction between two meanings of 'capacity'.

Core Idea
Not all impossible obligation is evil; it depends on the meaning of 'capacity'.

Al-Maturidi, with his characteristic precision, responds to al-Ka'bi's objection that the Maturidi position on divine obligation entails 'taklif ma la yutaq' (obligation of the impossible), which al-Ka'bi claims is rationally evil. In this concise passage, al-Maturidi dissects the meaning of 'capacity' and 'reason', showing that al-Ka'bi's objection only holds under a limited understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse. Then read the literal translation. Let al-Maturidi's concise style come through first.
2
Identify the objection and response
Find al-Ka'bi's claim and al-Maturidi's initial response. Notice how he distinguishes between two meanings of 'reason' and 'capacity'.
3
Analyze the example of Musa's companion
Al-Maturidi gives the story of Khider (the companion of Musa) as evidence. Consider how God's knowledge of inability does not make the obligation unjust.
4
Evaluate the final argument
See how al-Maturidi turns the tables: if al-Ka'bi is truthful, his own statement reduces to a claim that the act is impossible, not that it is evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعبر الشيخ أبو منصور رحمة الله عن هذه المعاني بأوجز لفظ وأحسن عبارة فحكى إلزام الكعبي أن ما قلتم تكليف ما لا يطاق وهو قبيح في العقل ثم أجاب فقال: إنما هو في العقل الذي لا يعرف الطاقة غير قوة الظاهر، وهي الصحة، فأما غيره فليس كما تقول بل كلف الله تعالى ثُمَّ صاحب موسى بما يعلم أنه لا يستطيع. ثم يقال له: وكذلك تكليف ما لا يطاق لوقت الفعل قبيح في العقل، والذي ادعيته من القبح إنما هو في عقل من يحيل وجود الفعل ولا قوة وقت الفعل، فصار قوله عند التحصيل هو القبيح في العقل إن صدق فيما ادعى.
And Sheikh Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, expressed these meanings with the most concise wording and the best expression, and he recounted al-Ka'bi's objection that what you say is an impossible obligation and it is evil in reason, then he answered and said: It is only in the reason that does not know capacity except as manifest power, which is soundness; as for other than that, it is not as you say. Rather, God Almighty imposed [an obligation] upon the companion of Moses [Khider] that He knew he could not do. Then it is said to him: Similarly, the obligation of that which cannot be done at the time of action is evil in reason, and what you claim of evil is only in the reason of one who deems the existence of the act impossible and that there is no power at the time of the act. So his statement, upon analysis, becomes that which is evil in reason if he is truthful in what he claims.
Source pages p. 845

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation by Abu Mansur al-Maturidi of an objection raised by al-Ka'bi. Al-Ka'bi had argued that the Maturidi position entails 'taklif ma la yutaq' (obligation of the impossible), which is rationally evil. Al-Maturidi responds by distinguishing between two meanings of 'reason' ('aql) and 'capacity' (taqa). He states that the claim of evil applies only in a reason that recognizes no capacity other than overt physical ability (quwwa al-zahir), which is essentially health and soundness. But for a reason that acknowledges another dimension—where God knows that the person cannot do the act, as with the companion of Moses (referring to the story of Khider)—it is not considered evil. Then al-Maturidi further argues: even if we accept that impossible obligation is evil, it is only evil according to a reason that deems the act impossible and that there is no power at the time of the act. Thus, al-Ka'bi's own statement, when analyzed, amounts to the claim that the act cannot occur, so his objection collapses if he is truthful. The argument highlights a nuanced understanding of divine obligation and human capacity, emphasizing that God's knowledge of inability does not make the obligation unjust.

قبيح في العقل
الطاقة
قوة الظاهر
الصحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'impossible obligation' means God commands what is intrinsically impossible.
Correction: Al-Maturidi clarifies that it refers to what is beyond the person's actual ability at the time, not something intrinsically impossible.

Checkpoint
According to al-Maturidi's response, what does al-Ka'bi's reason accept as the only meaning of 'capacity'?
A) The power to do both the act and its opposite
B) Overt physical ability, which is health
C) The ability to choose freely
D) The power that precedes the act

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi shows that the apparent evil of impossible obligation depends on a narrow definition of capacity, and that God's actions are not bound by such limited rationality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a command from God that seems beyond your capacity—like a trial or a duty you feel you cannot fulfill—do you trust that He knows your true ability better than you do? How will you respond next time such a feeling arises?
?
If you have ever judged God's decree as unfair or too difficult, could it be that you were using a limited understanding of capacity, like al-Ka'bi? What can you change in your heart to rely on a deeper trust in God's wisdom?
?
Al-Maturidi uses the story of Khidr to show that God's obligations may appear impossible from one perspective but are not unjust. In your own life, where have you dismissed a divine command or decree as 'unjust'? Will you commit to re-examining that judgment with the broader perspective this lesson teaches?

Divine Inventive Power Precedes, Human Acquisitive Power Is Concurrent
Al-Nasafi distinguishes divine inventive power from human acquisitive power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi distinction between divine inventive power (which must precede the act) and human acquisitive power (which is concurrent with the act).

Core Idea
Divine power creates from nothing; human power only acquires what God creates.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi explains a key Maturidi doctrine: God's power is inventive, affecting both non-existence and existence, so it must precede the act. Human power, however, is not inventive; it attaches to something already created to make it an acquisition. This clarifies why the issue of capacity (istita'a) is discussed right after the creation of actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage carefully to grasp the distinction between divine and human power.
Notice the technical terms: ikhtira' (invention) and kasb (acquisition).
2
Identify the condition for divine power
Divine power must affect both non-existence and existence at the moment of origination, necessitating its precedence over the determined thing.
3
Contrast with human power condition
Human power does not require precedence; it requires the existence of the invented thing to attach to it as an acquisition.
4
Note the connection to istita'a and khalq al-af'al
The passage explains why the issue of capacity is traditionally discussed after the creation of actions.
This shows the systematic ordering of topics in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكثير من أصحابنا يقولون بأن قدرة الباري جل جلاله متعلقة بالاختراع ومن شرط الاختراع أن تؤثر قدرة المخترع في العدم والوجود جميعًا حال حدوث المقدور، وذلك يوجب سبق القدرة للمقدور ليصبح تأثيرها في العدم، والقدرة المحدثة غير صالحة للاختراع بل هي صالحة لكون المخترع كسبًا للقادر فلم يكن من شرطها التقدم على المقدور ، بل من شرطها وجود المخترع ليتعلق بها فيكون كسبًا له، وعن هذا المعنى قيل : إن مسألة الاستطاعة قريبة لمسألة خلق الأفعال، فإن عندهم لما كانت قدرة العبد للاختراع لابد من أن تكون سابقة، وعندنا لما لم تكن للاختراع لم تكن سابقة بل كانت مقارنة لما وجد بالاختراع، ولهذا المعنى جرى العرف من المتكلمين بإيراد هذه المسألة عقيب مسألة خلق أفعال العباد. ثم نقول لهم : إن تعلق القدرة يستحيل بأي موجود ؟ بموجود وقع عنه الفراغ؟ أم بموجود لم يقع عنه الفراغ بعد؟ فإن قال بالأول، فهو مسلم غير أن الأعراض ليست لها حالة الفراغ إذ هي الحالة الثانية في حالة الوجود، وهي لا تبقى فتنعدم في تلك الحالة، وإن قال بالثاني فهو غير مسلّم أن القدرة لا تتعلق به ويوجدان معا على ما مر، وهذا هو الجواب عن قولهم : أن القدرة لو كانت توجد مع الفعل لم يكن إضافة الفعل إلى القدرة أولى من إضافة القدرة إلى الفعل فإن السواد باتصاف المحل بكونه أسود ، وكذا الحركة مع المتحرك والموت مع الميت والحياة مع الحي العصا اتصافها وخروج من اليد مع الإلقاء، وطلوع الشمس مع وجود النهار كل ذلك يوجدان معاً ولا تخفى العلة والمعلول، ويعرف ببديهة العقل حصول أحدهما بالآخر، واستحالة إضافة العلة إلى الحكم ، ولهذا قالت الفلاسفة: العلة متقدمة على المعلول لا تقدماً زمنيًا بل تقدمًا من حيث الرتبة، وحقيقة الكلام ما وجميع الصفات مع
And many of our companions say that the power of the Creator, exalted be His Majesty, is attached to invention, and among the conditions of invention is that the power of the inventor affects both non-existence and existence at the moment of the occurrence of the determined, and that necessitates the precedence of power over the determined so that its effect in non-existence can happen. And the originated power is not suitable for invention; rather, it is suitable for the invented thing to be an acquisition for the capable one. So it is not a condition for it to precede the determined; rather, the condition for it is the existence of the invented thing so that it attaches to it and becomes an acquisition for it. And because of this meaning it is said that the issue of capacity is close to the issue of the creation of actions. For according to them [the opponents], since the servant's power is for invention, it must be preceding; according to us, since it is not for invention, it is not preceding but rather concurrent with what came into existence through invention. And for this meaning, the custom of the theologians has been to present this issue after the issue of the creation of the acts of servants. Then we say to them: The attachment of power is impossible to what existing thing? To an existing thing from which one has become free? Or to an existing thing from which one has not yet become free? If they say the first, it is conceded, except that accidents have no state of freedom, for they are in the second state while in the state of existence, and they do not endure, so they become nonexistent in that state. If they say the second, it is not conceded that power does not attach to it, and they both exist together as previously mentioned. And this is the answer to their statement: If power were to exist with the act, then attributing the act to power would not be more appropriate than attributing power to the act. For blackness is with the attribute of the substrate being black; likewise motion with the moving thing, death with the dead, life with the living, the staff's being of it, and the coming out from the hand with the throwing, and the rising of the sun with the existence of daytime. All of these exist together, and the cause and effect are not hidden, and it is known by the clearness of reason that one occurs by the other, and the impossibility of attributing the cause to the effect. And for this reason the philosophers said: The cause is prior to the effect, not with temporal priority, but with priority in rank. And the reality of the statement is what... and all the attributes are with...
Source pages p. 846

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the nature of divine power (qudra al-bari) and human power (qudra haditha) by distinguishing between invention (ikhtira') and acquisition (kasb). Many Maturidi scholars hold that God's power is attached to invention, meaning it creates the object ex nihilo, affecting both non-existence and existence at the moment of origination. This requires that divine power precedes the determined object (maqdur) to affect non-existence first. In contrast, created power (qudra muhdatha) is not suitable for invention; rather, it is suitable for the invented thing to become an acquisition (kasb) for the capable agent. Therefore, it does not need to precede the act; instead, the condition is that the invented thing exists so that power can attach to it and make it an acquisition. This explains why the issue of capacity (istita'a) is treated right after the issue of the creation of actions (khalq al-af'al) in kalam works. Then a further objection is addressed: the opponent claims that if power coexists with the act, it is arbitrary why the act is attributed to power rather than power to the act. The answer uses multiple analogies: blackness and the substrate being black, motion and the moving thing, death and the dead, life and the living, the staff (referring to Moses' staff) and its transformation, the hand and throwing, sunrise and daytime. In all these pairs, both exist simultaneously, and it is self-evident (badiha al-'aql) that one occurs by the other, and that the cause is not temporally prior. The philosophers' view is cited: the cause is prior to the effect not temporally but in rank (ruba). This underscores the Maturidi position that concurrent causation is possible.

Key Terms
الاختراع
الكسب
القدرة البارية
القدرة المحدثة
الاستطاعة
خلق الأفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that human power must precede the act just like divine power.
Correction: The Maturidis hold that human power is not inventive; it only acquires, so it is concurrent with the act.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi scholars, why does human power not need to precede the act?
A) Because human power is invented by God
B) Because human power is only for acquisition, not invention
C) Because human power is temporal and cannot be eternal
D) Because human power is identical to the act

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi distinguish divine power as inventive (preceding) and human power as acquisitive (concurrent).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe that your actions are your own, yet this lesson tells you that your power is only concurrent and not creative. Does your daily experience of decision-making feel like you are inventing your choices? How can you reconcile that feeling with the truth that only Allah creates? Commit to reminding yourself before each action: 'My power is only an acquisition, not an invention.'
?
If divine power precedes and creates your actions, where does that leave your accountability? Do you blame your sins on God's decree, or do you accept your servant's role as an acquirer? This lesson says that your power attaches to the act after it is created. Does that make you more grateful for your good deeds and more repentant for your sins? Write a short prayer asking Allah to make your acquisitions pleasing to Him.
?
The theologians present this issue after the creation of actions. Have you ever truly internalized that your ability to do anything is a created gift? How would your view of your own abilities change if you saw them as purely concurrent and dependent? This week, try to catch yourself saying 'I can do this' and replace it with 'If Allah wills, I can acquire this action.'

Answering the Objection: Cause and Effect Can Be Concurrent
Al-Nasafi refutes an objection against concurrent power using analogies and causal priority in rank.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi response to the objection that if power coexists with the act, attribution is arbitrary, using examples of concurrent causation and the philosophical concept of priority in rank.

Core Idea
Concurrent causation is self-evident; cause precedes effect in rank, not time.

After establishing the concurrent nature of human power, al-Nasafi addresses a common objection: if power and act are simultaneous, why attribute the act to power and not vice versa? He responds with everyday examples where cause and effect coexist, and cites the philosophers' view that causal priority is not temporal but in rank.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
The opponent asks: to what existing thing can power attach? The response discusses the state of the act.
The answer distinguishes between accidents and substances.
2
Study the analogies
Al-Nasafi gives multiple examples: blackness, motion, death, life, staff, throwing, sunrise. All show cause and effect together.
Reflect on why these analogies are compelling.
3
Recognize the self-evident nature
He asserts that it is known by clear reason (badiha al-'aql) that one occurs by the other, and the cause is not attributed to the effect.
4
Understand priority in rank
The philosophers' view is cited: cause is prior to effect in rank, not temporally.
This resolves the objection without requiring temporal precedence.

?

The Mu'tazilite View: Servants Create Their Own Actions
Describes the Mu'tazilite position that servants create their own actions independently of God's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite position that humans create their own actions independently of God's will, and the nuance in their early terminology.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilites claimed humans create actions, even without God's will.

The Mu'tazilites strongly affirmed human free will, arguing that servants themselves bring their actions into existence. This passage from Imam al-Nasafi presents their view, noting both their bold claim and the caution of their early scholars about attributing true creation to humans.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the main assertion. Notice the contrast with divine management cut off entirely.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage states that servants invent actions, bringing them from non-existence to existence, and that this occurs whether God wills or not. This is the core Mu'tazilite ikhtiyar doctrine.
3
Examine the early exception
Note that early Mu'tazilites were cautious about using the word 'creation' for humans. They would still say 'There is no creator but God' while affirming the meaning of human creation. Why do you think they avoided the term?
This shows their desire to maintain public consensus on the phrase 'La ilaha illallah'.
4
Reflect on theological implications
Consider how this view differs from the Jabriyya (who deny human agency entirely). What does it say about God's power and human responsibility?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite position on human actions. It contrasts with the previous Jabr view by affirming that servants themselves create and bring their actions into existence from nothing, independently of God's will or management. They assert that actions come to be whether God wills them or not. The author notes that all Qadari Mu'tazilites held this view, though their early generations were reluctant to use the term 'creator' for humans, preferring to say 'there is no creator but God' while still maintaining that humans in some sense create. This highlights the theological tension within Mu'tazilism regarding the extent of human creative power.

Mu'tazilites held that humans create their own actions independently of God's will, but early adherents avoided the term 'creator' to maintain theological decorum.

?
The Mu'tazilites cut off God's management from human actions. In which areas of your life—perhaps your career, relationships, or personal achievements—do you mentally exclude God's involvement, acting as if you are the sole author of your outcomes? The Jabriyya Doctrine of Compulsion
Al-Nasafi presents the Jabriyya view that human actions are compelled by God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Jabriyya view that human actions are compelled by God and attributed to humans only metaphorically.

Core Idea
The Jabriyya see human actions as involuntary like trembling, not truly performed by us.

This passage from al-Nasafi presents the extreme predestinarian view of Jahm ibn Safwan and his followers. They held that all human actions are directly created by God and humans have no real power or choice. Understanding this view is essential for later refutations in the kalam tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice the structure: a claim, then explanation, then examples.
2
Identify the core claims
The passage makes three key claims: God creates all actions, humans have no power, and actions are involuntary. List them.
The text says 'made them all involuntary like the movements of a tremulous person'.
3
Understand the metaphorical interpretation
Attributing actions to humans is metaphorical. Compare 'Zaid went' to 'the boy grew tall' – both are events happening to a person, not actions performed by them.
This is a key distinguishing feature of Jabriyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قول جهم وأتباعه بالجبر: اختلف الناس في أفعال الخلق جعلها بعضهم الله تعالى، ونفوا عنها تدبير الخلق وأزالوا عنها قدرتهم بل لم يثبتوا لهم قدرة، وجعلوها كلها اضطرارية كحركات المرتعش وحركات العروق النابضة، وأحالوا اتصاف العباد بالقدرة وهو قول جهم بن صفوان الترمذي، وإضافتها إلى العباد عند هؤلاء مجاز، فيكون قول القائل: ذهب زيد ومشى عمرو بمنزلة قول القائل: طال الغلام ومات زيد وأبيض شعر عمر وشاخ عبد الله.
The doctrine of Jahm and his followers of compulsion: People differed regarding the actions of creation. Some of them made them [belong] to God, negated from them the management of creation, removed from them their power, rather they did not affirm for them any power, and made them all involuntary like the movements of a tremulous person and the pulsating movements of veins, and they deemed impossible the attribution of power to servants, and this is the doctrine of Jahm ibn Safwan al-Tirmidhi. Attribution of actions to servants according to these is metaphorical, so the saying of one who says: 'Zaid went' and 'Amr walked' is like the saying of one who says: 'The boy grew tall' and 'Zaid died' and 'Umar's hair turned white' and 'Abdullah became old'.
Source pages p. 849

Commentary
This passage presents the Jabriyya doctrine of compulsion (jabr) regarding human actions. Al-Nasafi begins by stating that people differed: some attributed all actions to God, negated human management and power, and made actions involuntary like trembling or pulse. They denied that servants can have power (qudra) and considered attributing actions to humans as merely metaphorical. He gives examples: saying 'Zaid went' is like saying 'the boy grew tall' – these are descriptions of events that happen to someone, not actions they perform. This is the view of Jahm ibn Safwan.

Key Terms

الاضطرار
القدرة
المجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Jabriyya deny that humans have any will at all.
Correction: The Jabriyya affirm that actions occur to humans, but they deny that humans have power (qudra) over them; actions are involuntary like trembling.

Checkpoint
